A tha furasda aithneach’,
Eadar bràigh Uisge Thurraid						2175
Is Caol Muile nan canach,
Ghearradh beum le’n airm ghuineach,
’Gan iomain mu’n fheamainn,
Ann an éirig nam muineal
Chaidh a chur ’san Aird-raithnich—					2180

O ’s iomadh fear gòirseid
Le ghunna bòidheach ’s lann dùbhghorm,
Le’n cuinnseiribh caola
’S an taoimean ’gan giùlan:
Mar bha Mac Laghmain ’s Mac Lachlainn,				2185
Mac Neachdain ’s Mac Dhùghaill,
Mac Iain Stiùbhairt o’n Apainn
’S Eóghan Abrach o’n Ghiùthsaich.

’S uaibhreach dhuibh-se bhith fiamhach
An taobh shìos de Bhun Abha,					2190
Ged théid Duibhnich gu’n dìcheall
’S gu dìdean a’ chlaidheimh;
’S leat gach neach thuirt mi chianaibh
Cho dìreach ri saighead,
Bidh Mac Raghnaill o’n tràigh leat					2195
Agus dà Mhac Gill’ Eathain.

Buail an teud sin gu sealbhail
’S na dèan searbh port na binneas,
Na cum t’aghaidh neo-shealbhaidh
Ri neach nach earb thu do shlinnein;					2200

[TD 173]

[Beurla]

[TD 174]

O thug an Rìgh an t-slat sgiùrsaidh
An glaic do dhùirn ’s nach tu shir i,
Uair mu seach air an tùirneal
Mar bhuaileas ùird air an innein.

’S ann ort a thàin’ an dà latha						2205
Ged bha e grathann gun tighinn,
Fhuair thu cùirt na bu leatha
An déidh t’athair a mhilleadh;
Ach gun aon bhuille claidheimh,
Gun sàthadh gatha no sgine,						2210
Mar gum bàidhte na coinnlean
Chaill thu t’oighreachd ’s do chinneach.

’S mairg a dh’iarr Iarl Atholl
Bhith toirt duibh fàbhar nach b’fheàirrd’ e,
O latha Rinn Lìomhann						2215
Thug sibh ìocadh mar eàrlais,
Mheall sibh null Marcus Atholl
Thar an abhainn mar eàrailt,
’Ga chur an làimh an Toll-bùtha
’N uair loisg sibh dùthaich Iarl Eàrlaidh.				2220

’S fheàrr leam fhaicinn na chluinntinn
Mar laigh a’ chuing air bhur muineal,
Gus ’n do thionndaidh a’ chuibhl’ oirbh
B’àirde Duibhnich na mullach;
’N uair a shaoil le Mac Cailein					2225
Bhith ’na bharraich air Muile,
B’fheàrr dha gleidheadh na bh’aige
Na bhith ’g agradh air tuilleadh.

Cha bu chomain Mhac Mhoirich
Teachd ad choinneamh ach ainneamh				2230
O linn mhic Choimisteir Stiùbhairt
Air an d’ rug sibh ’n tùs falachd;
Ma tha fear am brath foill duit
No neach a thairgeadh do mhealladh
Buin an ceann deth gun ghribeadh
An éirig fir Tobar-Mhoire.						2235

[TD 175]

[Beurla]

[TD 176]

Nam biodh fear a’ bheòil mhóir ann
O nach dòirteadh glòir bhraimeis,
’S dearbh gun cailleadh sibh geòidh ris
Nach b’fhiach an ròstadh ri h-aingeal;
Ged a ghlac iad le foill thu						2240
Bu tu saighdear bu ghlaine;
Gheibhte sgapadh nan caorach
Nam biodh do dhaoin’ air an talamh.

Ach cha b’fhiach leis an Iarla
Beart bhiastail ro ghràineil,						2245
Ach marcachd gu h-uasal
O fhuair e gu làimh sibh;
Gun bhàs fir no gille,
Gach nì shir e gu làimh dha,
Leig sibh fodhaibh ’nar sìneadh					2250
Cho mìn ris an làmhainn.

Tha thu ’d Mharcus fial Atholl,
Ad Mhorair rath’ Tulaich-bhàirdne,
Tha thu sud, ’s mar as còir dhuit
Tha thu ’d Lord Baile-bhàinidh;					2255
Tha thu ’t Iarla air Saoil
Chan eil cùis réidh gun do làmh ris,
Tha thu ’d Bhicunt Both-chuidir,
Cha mhisd’ do chuideachadh Fàcland.

[TD 178]

TUIRNEAL A’ CHNATAIN

CHA b’e tùirneal a’ chnatain						2260
Chuir am dhùisg mi ’sa’ mhadainn,
Ach an tùchan-s’ tha marcachd air m’ fhéithibh.

Fear do chéille bhith ’n Sagsann,
Gun fhios nach b’éigneach a’ bheart e,
Ma thig eug ort an taice Rìgh Seurlas.				2265

A chraobh stàilinn chruaidh chuilinn
Chaidh uainn air sàile do Lunnainn,
’S tearc mo ghàir’ ach gun cluinneam deagh sgeul ort.

Do thighinn fallain slàn uaithe,
Mar ruaig falaisg bhàrr cruadhlaich,					2270
No o gharadh a’ ghuail ’s nam balg-séididh.

Dh’fhalbh Mac Cailein, fear-buairidh,
Le sac gearrain de thuaileas,
Chur a ghearan an cluasaibh Rìgh Seurlas.

Ged a sgrìobhte leat Muile						2275
Bhiodh tu ’g iarraidh gu tuille,
Cha robh ’m bliadhna ’s an uiridh cho réidh dhuit.

Is iomad taigheadas òrail
Mhùirneach aighearach cheòlmhor
A ghreas t’athair gu fòrnadh na déirce;				2280

’Gan robh beathachadh bòidheach
Tha ’n diugh ’ga chaitheadh mu d’ bhòrd-sa,
Cleas na famha cur fòdh’ a cheart éiginn;

Cleas a’ bhaigeir mhóir làidir
Rinn a shaidseach a chàramh						2285
Leis gach paidreig a thàthadh ri chéile.

[TD 179]

[Beurla]

[TD 180]

Ach b’ait leam Duibhnich ’san dranndail
Fo dhruim an Tùir Fhrangaich
Agus cuibhreach ro theann air am féithibh.

Mhaighdean dubh-riabhach smachdail,				2290
Dh’fhàg i ’n t-Iarla gun mharsainn,
Thug i ’m fiabhras a Marcus Earr’-Ghàidheal.

Caiptean caol Loch nan Eala
Thug le féill as a’ bhail’ e,
Gun do chaochail e talla ’n am éirigh.				2295

Is mairg a dhùisgeadh a chadal
An laoch nach mùchtadh le bagradh,
’S e borb àrdanach acainneach gleusda.

Ghabh thu bhràid air do mhuineal,
Nach gabhadh càch orra chunnart,					2300
Thoirt do chàirdean a tunnaibh na féithe.

Eóghain òig Tòrr a’ Chaisteil,
Rinn thu chòir mar mo bheachd-sa,
Thog thu crò agus geata nach leum iad.

Thog thu bàrd ann an Dubhaird,					2305
Streap thu ’m bàrr croinne giuthais,
Leat bu mhiann a bhith ’n druidheachd an dreugain.

Thog thu ’n t-sròlbhratach bhuidhe
Os cionn stòl nam pìc iubhair,
Caol chòrcach an siubhal gach té dhiubh.				2310

Nam biodh a’ chùis mar a theirinn,
Bhiodh tu ’d dhiùc air gach eilean,
Leat bu mhiann a bhith ’d speireig ’sna speuraibh.

Is ann latha Sròn Nibheis
Bu droch còcaire gill’ thu;						2315
Chuir thu spògan air bhioraibh ’s dhroch ghréidh thu.

[TD 181]

[Beurla]

[TD 182]

Thug thu fathraigeadh fairge
Do luchd nam fallainnean dearga,
Bha ruith fala is earrdhearg mu’n sléisdibh.

Fhuair thu garbhbhata cuilinn						2320
Cheud là dhearbh thu bhith ’d dhuine
Mun d’fhàs calg ort de fhionnadh no dh’fheusaig.

Cha bu shùgradh do sheanchoin
An cnàimh smuais thoirt a d’ dhreamchraos
’N uair a theannadh tu teanchair do dheudaich.			2325

Cha bu shùgradh do sgoileir
Dol a dhranndan ri d’ choileir
’N uair a thionndadh tu chorrfhiacail gheur ris;

Le luchd nam feadanan dùbhghorm
D’am bu fhreagarrach fùdar						2330
’N uair a spreigeadh na h-ùird ri spuir gheura;

Bheireadh dùsgadh le h-ainiochd
Air ùdlaich’ an langain,
Garbh stùcach mór eangach an t-sléibhe.

Bhiodh an t-sùil air neo ’n t-eanchainn				2335
Mu dheireadh drùdhadh bhur n-eanbhruich;
Cha bhi mise d’a sheanchas nas léir dhomh.

ORAN AIR FEACHD RIGH SEUMAS

IS mithich dhuinn màrsadh as an tìr
On chuir sinn dìth air feòil nam mart;
Tamall an òrdugh dhuinne ’s d’ar mórshluagh,			2340
Dh’imich ar n-òigridh uainn a mach;
A chuilein òig, ma tha thu leòinte,
Gun seall an Rìgh Mór riut anns gach beairt;
Air madainn Di-màirt a rinn sinn màrsadh
Is facal gach sàirdsein a’ ruith oirnn mu seach.			2345

Aig leathtaobh an t-sàile tharraing na h-àrmainn
Suas ’nam bragàdaibh gu glé cheart;
Mu bheul an anmoich shuidhich sinn campa,
Is dh’imich ar ceannard uainn a mach;
Facal ar Còirneil ri Sir Dòmhnall,					2350
Mar sud is ar n-òrdugh bhith ’nar glaic:
“Na leigibh bonn dàil an seasamh a’ gheàird,
Agus cumaibh ur naimhdean uaibh a mach.”

Bu fhliuch a’ mhadainn a thog sinn ar breacain
’S a chaidh sinn air astar gus an taigh ’gan robh chairt;		2355
’N uair rinn sinn éirigh gun d’rinn sinn ar éideadh
Is chaidh sinn ’nar leum fo na cnapanna-saic;
’S bu lughaid air n-airtneal ’n uair thàinig am feasgar
’N uair loisgeadh an lasag bu lìonmhor srad;
O cheann Loch Iall gun d’ rinn sinn triall,				2360
’S ’n uair chrom a’ ghrian gun d’ rinn sinn stad.

Aig ceann Loch Lòchaidh shuidhich sinn campa
Latha roimh Dhi-dòmhnaich ’s dà latha ’na dhéidh,
Chruinnich ar càirdean uil’ air an làraich,
’S thog iad an làmhan an làthair Mhic Dhé;				2365
Bu bheag ar spéis do dh’airgead no spréidh,
’S gun d’fhàg sinn ’nar déidh ar mnathan ’s ar clann;

[TD 185]

[Beurla]

[TD 186]

Cheart aindeoin an sport, ged chiùrrt’ againn corp,
Cha dèan sinn bonn clos gus an cosgar leinn Gall.

Labhair an Gràmach a b’fheàrr nàdur:				2370
“A chlanna nan Gàidheal, na faiceam ur gruaim,
Togaibh ur n-inntinn, thàinig an tìm dhuibh,
’S mithich dhuinn màrsadh do’n tìr so shuas”.
Dh’fhalbh sinn a muigh cho ro stàtail
Gus an do ràinig sinn bràighe Ghlinn Ruaidh;			2375
Mach ri Gleann Turraid is monadh sin Dhrumainn
Dh’imich gach duine bha guineach ’san ruaig.

Mach monadh Dhruim-uachdair dh’imich na h-uaislean
Bu mhór cruadal ’s bu bheag sgìos;
’N uair ràinig sinn Atholl cha d’fhuair sinn ach mnathan—		2380
Chaidh fir as an rathad mun gabhte dhiubh cìs;
An déidh mheadhon-latha, sinn a’ falbh air ar n-athais,
Aig leathtaobh na h-abhann ghabh sinn a sìos;
Thàinig marcaich a steach air beulaibh a’ phass
Dh’inns’ gun tàinig am prasgan is Còirneal Mac Aoidh.		2385

B’aithghearr a’ chéilidh thug muinntir Rìgh Seumas,
Leathtaobh an t-sléibhe ghabh iad a suas;
Bu lìonmhor fallas air gach mala
Aig dìreadh a’ bhealaich an taobh mu thuath;
Ceann na cuimhne roimh a mhuinntir,				2390
Pàirt d’ar n-ionndrainn e bhith uainn:
B’aigeantach spòrsail aigne Chlann Dòmhnaill,
Ged fhuair iad an leònadh bu deònach leo ’n uair.

Ghluais gach fine gun tlàths gun tioma
Gun sgàth gun ghiorag ’nan ionadan féin;				2395
Chaidh sinn cho stàtail am brollach ar nàmhaid,
’S cha tilgte crann sàthte an là sin gun fheum;
Aig deireadh an latha ’n uair tharraing sinn claidheamh
Bha toiseach ar sgathaidh ’n am laighe do’n ghréin,
Cheart aindeoin an sparraidh, ged bu làidir am barail,		2400
Gun do chaill iad am fearann ’s an t-anam ’na dhéidh.

[TD 187]

[Beurla]

[TD 188]

Ceannard an àigh, gun do thuit thu ’sa’ bhlàr!
’S bu sgathach do làmh gus an tàinig an uair;
’S e do bhàs, a Dhùn-dighe, dh’fhàg ormsa trom-lighe,
Chuir toll ann mo chridhe ’s a dh’fhàg snigh’ air mo ghruaidh;	2405
Bu bheag air son t’éirig na thuit de na béisdean,
An cogadh Rìgh Seumas ged dh’éirich leinn buaidh;
Ach sgapadh nan cuileag air muinntir Rìgh Uilleam,
Tha sinne fo mhulad ged chuir sinn iad uainn.

Còirneal Ramsaidh bu mhór anntlachd				2410
Anns an am ud tighinn a steach;
Gun robh sinn cho aingidh anns an am sin,
Gréim air Gall cha leigeamaid as;
“Chòirneil <eng>Balfour, ye son of a whore,
I’ll make you sure, ye’ll come to the fight”;<gai>			2415
Bhrist iad do chrùn is t’ad air do shùilean,
’S gheàrr iad do bhùtainn air cùlaibh do chas.

CATH RAON RUAIRIDH

AN ainm an àigh nì mi tùs
Air a’ mheanm-sa tha ’m rùn:
Chan i so ’n aimsir mun dùin an Céitean oirnn.			2420

Nam faicinn luingeas an Rìgh
Cur an spionnaidh gu tìr:
Chan e Uilleam tha mì cho déidheil air;

Ach Rìgh Seumas ’s a shìol
A dh’òrdaich Dia dhuinn g’ar dìon:					2425
Cha rìgh iasaid d’am fiach dhuinn géilleachdainn.

Ach mur tig thu air ball,
’S do léinte-criosa ’gan call,
Is ceud misde leam thall ’san Eiphit thu.

An comunn ciotalach tlàth						2430
Shuidh an ionad nan Stàt,
Mar chommittee chuir Sàtan saoile riu.

Tha cuid diùbh fathasd tha beò
Nach d’fhuair an radh’ ach gu fòil,
Reic iad t’athair air òr ’s bu déistneach e.				2435

Paca sligheach nan cealg
D’am bu dligheach a’ mheirg,
Dhubh am fitheach le salchar eucoir sibh.

Cha b’e ’m brathadair còir
Bha cur gabhail fo’n fhòid,						2440
Ach fear an taigh’ nach bu chòir bu phéacan duibh.

Anns a’ bheithe bheag òg
Bha fo bhaile Mhic Dheòrs,
Gur h-iomadh fear sròil bha reubte ann.

[TD 191]

[Beurla]

[TD 192]

Is iomadh bearraid is gruag						2445
Bha ’gan spealtadh mu’n cnuac:
Bha fuil dhaithte ’na stuaigh air feur a muigh.

Fhuair sibh deannal ’sa’ choill
O lannaibh Shìol Chuinn
Chuir ’nar deannaibh thar tuinn trom-chreuchdach sibh.		2450

An Raon Ruairidh nam bad
’S lìonmhor uaigh is corp rag,
Mìle sluasaid is caib ’gan léidigeadh.

A shàr Chléibhears nan each,
Bu cheann-féadhn’ thu air feachd:					2455
Mo chreach léir an tùs gleac mar dh’éirich dhuit.

Bu lasair theine dhaibh t’fhearg
Gus an d’éirich mi-shealbh:
Bhuail am peileir fo earball t’éididh thu.

Bu mhór cosgradh do làmh						2460
Fo aon chlogaide bàn,
’S do chorp nochdaidh geal dàn gun éideadh air.

Cha robh eascaraid suas
Eadar Arcaibh is Tuaid
Mur bhith ’n tacaid a bhuail ’san eudainn thu.			2465

’N uair bhrùchd t’uaislean a mach,
Cha sgaoth bhuachaille mhart,
Ach luchd bhualadh nan cnap gu spéireadail.

Air a’ bhruthach a stad
Os cionn dubhar nam bad						2470
Luchd cur ’nan siubhal gu grad nan eucorach.

Clann Dòmhnaill an àigh,
Luchd a chonnsach’ gach blàir,
Cha do ghabh iad riamh sgàth roimh reubalaich.

[TD 193]

[Beurla]

[TD 194]

’S lìonmhor spalpaire dian						2475
Bha fo d’ bhrataich dol sìos:
Cha b’ascartach lìn do réisimeid.

’S iomadh fiùran deas òg,
Gun làn dùirn air de fheòil,
Ghearradh cloigne is smòis is féitheannan.				2480

Mo ghaol an Dòmhnall Gorm Og
O’n Tùr Shléiteach ’s o’n Ord:
Fhuair thu deuchainn ’s bu mhór an sgeula e.

Mo ghaol an Tàinistear ùr
’S a gheur Spàineach ’na smùid:					2485
Cha b’e ’n t-ùmaidh air chùl na sgéithe e.

Mo ghràdh an t-Alasdair Dubh
O Ard Gharaidh nan sruth
Chuir ’nan siubhal gu tiugh na reubalaich.

’S do bhràthair eile Iain Og,						2490
Dh’aomaich peileir throimh fheòil:
’S caol a theirinn e beò o’n spéileireachd.

Tha an cogadh so searbh,
Air a thogail gu garg:
Ge ceann nathrach bidh earball peucaig air.				2495

’S e Prionns Uilleam ’s a shluagh
Dh’fhàg an dùthaich so truagh
’N uair a chuir iad thar cuan Rìgh Seumas uainn.

Guidheam sgrios agus bàs,
Goirt is miosgainn is plàigh						2500
Air bhur sliochd, mar bh’air àl na h-Eiphite;

Gach aon latha dol sìos,
Guin gach claidheimh ’nur bian,
Coin ag caitheamh an dìol air sléibhte dhibh.

MURT GHLINNE COMHANN

NAM faighinn mar dh’iarrainn
Air sliochd Dhiarmaid na ròice,
Sgrios gun duilleach gun iarmad					2510
Air gach sìochair tha beò dhiubh;
Gun aon duine bhith làthair
De na thàinig dhe’n t-seòrs’ ud,
Gus am biodh an cuid àite
Aig mo chàirdean Clann Dòmhnaill.					2515

Nam bu mhise b’fhear òrduigh
Air a’ mhóran a bh’aca,
Dh’fhàgainn uil’ Earra-Ghàidheal
Aig Raghnall na Ceapaich;
’S na bh’aig Morair Bhraghaid-Albainn				2520
Bhith am pàipearan glaiste
Aig Mac Mhic Iain o’n Innein
An déidh gach sgiorradh a bh’aca.

Cha bu mhór leam sud dha-san
Bho’n ghràisg bha cur as da,						2525
Rinn am murt ri aon oidhche
Leis an fhoill a bha peacach;
Leam is duilich ri ràdha
Gun d’ rinn am Bràighdeach dhuit taice;
Nam bu mhise b’fhear binn ort					2530
Chaill thu cinnteach an craiceann.

Chan fhàgar Fear Achadh-triachtain
An dìochuimhn ’s cha b’airidh,
Chuirinn sìos e do Chòmhghall—
Bhiodh caoraich mhór’ ann gu ramhath;				2535
Na bh’aig Mac Mhic Iain Riabhaich
Eadar grian agus gealach,
Bhiodh do chòraichean sgrìobhta,
Is làmh an Rìgh ris ’ga cheangal.

[TD 200]

Nam faighinn ur càradh						2540

Ged a thigeadh am bàs orm
Am màireach le cabhaig,						2545

ORAN AIR RIGH UILLEAM AGUS BANRIGH MAIRI

HÌ rin hà rin ho ró o bha hó
Hì rin hà rin ho ró a bha hì
Biodh gach duin’ agaibh brònach					2550
Air son fòirneart mo Rìgh.

An diugh chuala mi naidheachd
Air alt nach b’aimhealtach leinn,
Nan cumadh e chasan,
’S gum biodh an t-athsgeul cho binn,					2555
Rìgh Seumas le faram
Cur a dharaich ’na still;
On ’s leat uachdar na mara,
Gluais is tarraing gu tìr.

Mhic Muire na h-Oighe,						2560
Coimhead fòirneart mo Rìgh;
Có b’urrainn d’ar smàladh
Ach do làmhan-s’ bhith leinn?
Faic a nise Prionns Orainns
Cur na còir os a cinn;							2565
Ach as do chobhair, a Shlànaighear,
Thig furtachd ’s slàint’ air gach tinn.

A Rìgh chumhachdaich fheartaich
’Ga bheil beachd air gach nì,
Cum air aghaidh an ceartas,						2570
An lagh seachranach pill;
Faic luchd nam bréid daithte
Bhith gun ghealt ann am prionnt,
’S ma tha ’n eucoir ’nan aigne
Beum do shlat os an cionn.						2575

’N uair a thàinig thu Shasann
’S tu rinn aiseag a’ bhreamais,

[TD 203]

[Beurla]

[TD 204]

Sheilbh chòir thoirt air éiginn
O’n athair-chéile thug bean duit;
Cha b’i reula nan dùilean						2580
Bha dèanamh iùil duit ’san aineol,
Mar bha roimh na trì Rìghrean
An uair bha Iosa ’na leanabh.

Thug thu ’m follais an t-Slànaighear
Sgeula gràin do luchd teagaisg;					2585
Is gur mór am fàth nàire
An cóig àithne a bhristeadh;
A nighean fhéin ’s mac a pheathar,
An aghaidh labhairt an Sgriobtuir,
Mar bhreun ghearran ’sa’ chathair,					2590
’S nach b’fhear-taighe d’an sliochd e.

Is fìor mhallaichte ’n lànmhain
Thum an spàin anns an roinn ud,
’N seilbh còir thoirt a dh’aindeoin
Le mùthadh malairt an troidhteir;					2595
Ged a stadadh an claidheamh
Gun bhuille chaith’ ach na rinn e,
Bidh gach fuil ’g éigheach am flaitheas
Ad dhéidh a latha ’s a dh’oidhche.

’S mairg a chreideadh droch naidheachd				2600

<eng>
NOTES

FÒGRADH RAGHNAILL OIG

The MacDonalds of Keppoch were deeply implicated in the struggles of their kinsmen in Islay against the Earl of Argyll in the first quarter of the seventeenth century, in their efforts to retain possession of the MacDonald territories of Kintyre and Islay. In 1615 Alasdair nan Cleas, 10th of Keppoch, with Ranald Og his eldest son, and the eldest son of Clanranald, assisted Sir James MacDonald of Islay in escaping from Edinburgh Castle where he had been imprisoned by the Earl of Argyll. They accompanied him to Argyllshire and took part in the rebellion which he stirred up there against the Scottish Government, which supported Argyll. As a result of these proceedings they were outlawed and Sir James fled to Spain, where he was joined three years later by Keppoch and his second eldest son Dòmhnall Glas (Gregory, p. 305 ff.).

According to the authors of Clan Donald, Alasdair nan Cleas was the principal person concerned in the escape of Sir James from Edinburgh Castle (Clan Donald, Vol. II, p. 624). In the poem which follows this one Iain Lom ascribes the honour of providing the key by means of which he effected his escape to Raghnall Og. The Clan Donald historians further state that the latter did not escape to Spain with his father and brother, but chose to remain in Lochaber, where he lay concealed for several years (ibid., p. 629).

We have an interesting glimpse of Raghnall Og’s hiding place in Uamha an Aghastair in the vicinity of Loch Treig in the following stanza which is quoted by D. C. Macpherson in his account of the Clan Donald of Keppoch:

<gai>
Bha mi là air lorg taghain
’S thug mi i gu crò,
Aite nach bu mhisde leam—
Cidsin Raghnaill Oig:
Bha tuagh is tàl is tora ann
Is coire bhruicheadh feòil,
’S gun robh de bhoicinn ghobhar ann
Na dh’fhóghnadh dhomh ri m’ bheò.<eng>
(Celtic Magazine, Vol. IV, p. 373)

[TD 231]

On 21st October 1618 a commission was granted to Lord George Gordon for the pursuit and apprehension of Ranald MacRonald son of MacRonald of Garavagh, who since his father’s flight from the country was the sole outstanding outlaw from the rebellion of Sir James MacDonald (P.C.R., Vol. XI, p. 456). This commission Lord Gordon accepted, and promised that he would capture or kill him before the following March (ibid., p. 551). The task proved more difficult than he had anticipated, for it is recorded that on 27th July 1621 a further commission of fire and sword was granted to Lord Gordon against Ranald Og, “a chief man in the Rebellion in the Isles in 1615 and since then lurking in the Highlands and causing disturbance there” (P.C.R., Vol. XII, pp. 551, 552). The outcome is uncertain, but it is probable that after the fall of Argyll, when Sir James and the Chief of Keppoch were recalled from Spain, Raghnall Og was pardoned also (Gregory, p. 401).

Iain Lom says that Ranald Og made his escape to Spain, and MacKenzie, in his History of the MacDonalds (p. 485), makes a similar statement, as does Macpherson (Celtic Magazine, Vol. IV, p. 373), but from the evidence furnished by the Records of the Privy Council it would appear that this was not in fact the case. It is quite possible, however, that the poet may have had more information on the subject than was available to the Privy Council. The events recorded took place in all probability before the poet was born, and it is questionable whether Iain Lom is indeed the author of these lines, although the poem is ascribed to him in D, the only primary source. From this source derives the only other known version, which is found in Maclean Sinclair’s edition of the poet’s works.

8. This line is quoted in an elegy which was composed for Alexander MacDonald, 16th Chief of Keppoch, who fell at Culloden (D, p. 19).

16. In an article by the late Mr Charles E. Whitelaw on Glasgow and Stirling swordsmiths the following description of a blade by the Stirling smith Walter Allan occurs: “The blade is single edged with one flute close to the point at some little distance from the back, the intervening space being filled in with a succession of segmental marks; it is signed Andria Farara” (Transactions of the Glasgow Archaeological Society, Vol. VIII, Part IV, Supplement, p. 35). According to Mr George Davidson, Curator of the Highland Folk Museum, Kingussie, the Andrea

[TD 232]

Ferrara mark is bitten in by acid and not punched by small tools as is usual on imported blades. Walter Allan was admitted Armourer burgess of Stirling in 1732 and his father was also Armourer there (Extracts from the Records of the Royal Burgh of Stirling, 1667-1752, p. 133). It is possible that they carried on an older Stirling tradition of blade making.

27. Poets and musicians were among intimate friends who attended the chief after he retired for the night.


TILLEADH RAGHNAILL OIG

In these lines the poet gives expression to his joy at the return of Ranald Og, and requests that a toast be drunk to celebrate the event. He refers to the part played by Ranald Og in the release of Sir James MacDonald of Islay from Edinburgh Castle, and the bounty granted to him by the King, after he had been restored to royal favour. The available evidence suggests that the person concerned is not Raghnall Og but Alasdair nan Cleas.

The source is D, from which derives also the version in O.

34. D is defective metrically, as there should be three unstressed syllables between the last stress in l. 1 of the couplet and the first stress in l. 2 (cf. l. 52). The form aisiridh is therefore adopted in preference to aisre which is the form in the original.

36.
<gai>Is tu b’fhaicillich ceum
Mu’n taice-sa an dé.<eng>

“At this hour of yesterday thou wert the most wary of step”.
(Watson, Gaelic Songs of Mary MacLeod, ll. 339-340)

42. The final word in the line is omitted in D and is supplied by Maclean Sinclair. Another possible emendation is to substitute Agus for Is in the following line (cf. l. 67).

50 The metre is defective in D and Maclean Sinclair’s emendation has been adopted.

51. luchd-chleòc: the context suggests gaolers, but the expression may be used of Lowlanders, as for example in “Ruaig Ghlinne Freoin” (1603):

[TD 233]

<gai>Bha mi lathair an latha
’N robh do bhraithrin is t athair
Far an d’fhag iad nan luighe luchd chleoc.<eng>
(“Scottish Gaelic Folk-Poetry” by Derick S. Thomson, Scottish Gaelic Studies, Vol. VIII, p. 17).

63. Alasdair nan Cleas was recalled from Spain by King James in 1620 and received from him a yearly pension of 200 merks stg., together with a remission for all his past offences (Gordon, Earldom of Sutherland, p. 239; Gregory, pp. 401-402). In March 1622 he was detained in Edinburgh by the Scottish Privy Council until he should find security for his own personal behaviour and for the maintenance of peace—this despite the fact that he had a six months’ protection from the King in his possession (P.C.R., Vol. XII, p. 696). He made his peace with the Scottish Council, however, and settled in Lochaber. It is probable that Ranald Og was also pardoned about this time now that his father was restored to favour.

69. Sir Lachlan Mackintosh of Dunnachtan was granted a commission of fire and sword on 9th July 1618 against Keppoch and his sons, but this commission was withdrawn the following October in favour of one to Lord Gordon against Keppoch’s eldest son, who was then the only outstanding rebel—his father and brother having escaped to the Continent (P.C.R., Vol. XI, pp. 403-404, p. 456).

71. maireann: 3rd sg. pres. indic. of the verb mair, endure; here we have an early occurrence of the verbal form used as an adjective equal to beò. In Irish this form is used sometimes independently as a verb, and sometimes as an adjective with beò in the sense of continuing to live. (See Professor Fraser’s note on the word in Scottish Gaelic Studies, Vol. I, p. 204.)


CUMHA AONGHAIS MHIC RAGHNAILL OIG

The subject of this poem is Angus MacDonald of Keppoch (only son of Raghnall Og referred to above), who was killed at Sròn a’ Chlachain above Killin in a skirmish which took place between a rieving party from Lochaber and the Campbells of Breadalbane. There is a discrepancy between the genealogies of the MacDonalds of Keppoch as given by MacKenzie and the authors of Clan Donald. According to the latter Raghnall Og died without issue shortly after 1640, and was succeeded by his

[TD 234]

brother Dòmhnall Glas (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 420). MacKenzie on the other hand states that Raghnall Og was succeeded by his son Angus, who in turn was succeeded by his uncle Dòmhnall Glas, son of Alasdair nan Cleas (History of the MacDonalds, p. 487). The latter is supported by MacVurich.

Professor Watson states that a document in the possession of Mr John MacGregor, W.S., Edinburgh, proves conclusively that the skirmish of Sròn a’ Chlachain took place on 4th June 1646, and not in 1640 as has hitherto been accepted (BGh, p. 325). But, according to MacVurich, Angus MacDonald’s uncle Dòmhnall Glas, presumably as head of the MacDonalds of Keppoch, joined Alasdair mac Cholla in August 1644, so the subject of this poem was probably dead before this date (Reliquiae Celticae, Vol. II, p. 181). Furthermore Spalding states that in 1640 Argyll “plunderit and spolzeit all Lochquaber, and brynt McDonaldis houss of Keppoche”, and in another source we are told that these injuries were not long unavenged, for the men of Lochaber went with a company of 120 men to Breadalbane and from there carried away a great drove of cattle (Spalding, Trubles, Vol. I, p. 292; MacFarlane, Genealogical Collections, Vol. I, pp. 306-307). We may compare the title of the poem as given by Turner, which states that Angus MacDonald was killed “when Clan Iain of Glencoe lifted the spoil of Breadalbane”.

The source is Turner, from which derive also the versions in GlB, O, BGh. I is closely related to T although there are several differences in readings. Six stanzas with an English translation (vv. 1, 3, 5, 6, 8, 10) are included in the Killin Collection of Gaelic Songs, pp. 54-56. With the exception of one reading these are identical with T. A longer version of the poem is found in the Dornie MSS., in which Angus is represented as Iain Lom’s brother, and Iain Lom as a redoubtable swordsman (Appendix B, p. 385).

83-84. Cf. Mairearad Nighean Lachlainn’s description of the Clan Maclean after they had lost their Chief:

<gai>Iad gun iteach gun lìnnidh
Ach mar gheòidh air an spìonadh.<eng>
(BGh, ll. 3709-3710)

85. The poet compares his lot with that of Oisin son of Fionn, who is represented as a pathetic and solitary figure, having outlived his companions and existing in an atmosphere to which his spirit is far from attuned. According to two ballads contained in

[TD 235]

the sixteenth-century MS., The Book of the Dean of Lismore, Oisin, bereft of the companionship of the Fianna, is compelled to draw stones for the building of St Patrick’s Church in Elphin—a task which has little appeal for him (Ross, Heroic Poetry from the Book of the Dean of Lismore, p. 8 ff., p. 36 ff.). The expression “mar Oisean an déidh nam Fiann” (or “na Féinne”) is very common in Gaelic poetry.

94. càramh: the form given by Turner is caradh, but the Lochaber form is càramh.

95. This was quite a usual way of expressing grief for the death of a chief. The same sentiment is expressed in a lament composed for Hector Maclean who was killed in the battle of Inverkeithing:

<gai>
Thuit mo chomhalta tapaidh
Fo bhaile na faiche,
Làn cruadal le gaisge;
Ged bu chruaidh leam mar thachair,
Chan e sin tha mi ’g acain
Ach an sgiùrsadh fhuair Eachann roimh naimhdean.
<eng>

My stalwart foster-brother fell below the town where the battle-field was, full of hardihood and valour; although what has happened distressed me, that is not what I lament, but the routing which Hector sustained from his enemies.
(E, p. 233)

100. It is said that Angus, who was badly wounded, was left concealed in a bothy with one or two of his clan. They were discovered by a party of the pursuing Campbells who killed them all (MacDonells of Keppoch, p. 48). There is a tradition that the Keppoch Chief fell at the hands of James Menzies of Culdares (Huntly’s captor), who on that day had married a daughter of Campbell of Breadalbane. The marriage festivities at Finlarig Castle were interrupted by the news that a party of men from Keppoch and Glencoe had plundered Strathfillan and Glendochart. (For a good account of the skirmish see GSI, XXVI, pp. 147-148; cf. Killin Collection, p. 54.)


ORAN DO DHÒMHNALL GORM OG

This poem is addressed to Sir Donald MacDonald, or as he was better known Dòmhnall Gorm Og mac Ghille-easbuig

[TD 236]

Chléirich, who succeeded his uncle Dòmhnall Gorm Mór as eighth Chief of the MacDonalds of Sleat in 1617, and was married to Janet, daughter of Lord MacKenzie of Kintail (Clan Donald, III, 470). It is not certain when he was knighted, but in the Records of the Privy Council, 17th July 1617, he is referred to as Sir Donald Gorme of Sleat. He was a staunch Royalist and when the Civil War commenced King Charles signed a commission issued from Birkes near Berwick-on-Tweed (dated 11th June 1639) appointing the Marquis of Antrim and Sir Donnell Gorm MacDonnell of Sleat “conjunctlie and severallie His Majesty’s Lieutenants and Commissioners within the whole Highlands and Isles of Scotland” (Hill, MacDonnells of Antrim, pp. 253-254, pp. 444-446). This is perhaps what Murdo MacKenzie had in mind when he referred to him as “Ursann chatha Innse Gall” (BGh, l. 5898).

The text followed is that of E, which is the oldest source and that on which all other versions are based.

112. gun sgàth: may mean without fear, and so boldly; the word has also the meaning of covering, and it is possible that in this context the meaning is uncovered, or openly.

121. The reading in the original—Gam biodh a shùgrid sherbh—might be interpreted as in Text, or: Dh’am bitheadh an sùgradh searbh.

124. The yew, which was native to the Highlands, was regarded as the best wood for bows. There is an interesting note in Sean Dana, which states that among the Highlanders of later times the yew which grew in the wood of Easragan in Lorn was regarded as the best. The feathers most in vogue for the arrows were furnished by the eagles of Loch Treig, the wax for the string by Baile na Gailmhinn, and the heads by the smiths of the race of Mac Pheidearain:

<gai>
Bogha dh’iubhair Easragain,
Is ite fireun Locha Tréig,
Céir bhuidhe Bhaile na Gailmhinn,
’S ceann o’n cheàrd Mac Pheidearain.<eng>
(Smith, Sean Dàna, p. 8)

130. Cf.:
<gai>Ite an eòin léith
Briogadh ’na déidh.<eng>
(BGh, ll. 6342-6343)

[TD 237]

131-132. Transposed also in GlB and BGh (2nd and 3rd edns.) from the order of E.

152. ’n fhàsag: E reads Nàsaig, while in O the form is nasag (an empty shell), and in BGh (2nd edn.), àsaig (àsuinn, acfhuinn, tools, apparatus). Nàsag = ’n fhàsag—fàs, empty, hollow.

Cf.: <gai>’S truagh leam d’athair ’s do bhraithrean
A cur aisig air seòl,
’Dhol g’ad iarraidh le scriobain,
’S tu ’n ad shìneadh ’san ròd.<eng>
(CC, p. 59)

According to Mr Angus MacLeod by whom the above lines are quoted in BGh (3rd edn.), p. 324, the reference is to the launching of a small boat which was rowed back and fore, to recover the body of the drowned man.

165. Leith was the chief port in Scotland in the seventeenth century, and small organs, many of which were imported from Germany, were introduced into private chapels and churches under Archbishop Laud’s influence (Wedgwood, The King’s Peace, p. 94).

171. Fòirn’: i.e. pl. fòirne; or fòirne used as nom. sing. Cf. Note 549, post. Assonance requires ò, and the metre one syllable. Professor Watson suggests that foirim which is the reading in E may be a misprint for foirin, i.e. foireann, f., a troop; chess-men, the complement of a chess-board (BGh, pp. 324, 367). He draws attention to a further instance denoting a similar usage:

<gai>Foireann air thì,
Dolaran sìos,
Galain de’n fhìon bhàrcaideach.<eng>
(Ibid., ll. 6375-6377)

173. Somerled, regulus of Argyll, and founder of the family de Insulis or MacDonald Lords of the Isles, in the course of his struggle to quell Norse power in Scotland, defeated Godred, King of Man and the Isles, in a battle fought in 1156 off the north coast of Isla. The latter ceded to him the islands south of Ardnamurchan Point, including Kintyre, which as late as the seventeenth century was classed by the Scottish Government as one of the Western Isles. (For an explanation of this see Gregory, p. 14.) On the death of Somerled in 1164 these lands were divided among his three sons, one of whom, Reginald by name, received

[TD 238]

Islay and Kintyre, and on his death in 1207 they passed to his son Donald, the eponymous ancestor of the clan. The MacDonalds styled themselves “de Isla”, of Isla, and also “de Insulis”, of the Isles. A claim to the Earldom of Ross was first submitted by Donald, second Lord of the Isles, by reason of his marriage to Lady Mary Leslie, who later became Countess of Ross, but after the battle of Harlaw in 1411 he was compelled to surrender this claim. The Lords of the Isles did not enter into possession of the Earldom until after the death of James I, who was assassinated on 21st February 1437, when Alexander, eldest son of Donald of Harlaw, became Earl of Ross in right of his mother, Countess of Ross, and his title acknowledged by the Crown (Clan Donald, I, p. 192 ff.).

There was no earldom of Isla or Kintyre but, as these lands remained central possessions of the Lords of the Isles who were styled “de Isla”, and as they were also in possession of the Earldom of Ross, Iain Lom combines both concepts, as does a contemporary of his, Màiri Nighean Alasdair Ruaidh. In her lament for Roderick MacLeod, 17th Chief of Harris and Dunvegan, she refers to him thus:

<gai>De shliochd àrmann Chinn-tìre,
Iarla Ile agus Rois thu.<eng>
(Gaelic Songs of Mary MacLeod, ll. 703-704)

His mother was a daughter of Sir James MacDonald of Sleat, and the MacDonalds of Sleat were descended from Hugh, a younger son of Alexander, Earl of Ross and Lord of the Isles—hence the reference here.


LÀ INBHIR LÒCHAIDH

The poet in exultant mood gives a graphic and spirited account of the battle of Inverlochy, which took place on Sunday, 2nd February 1645, and resulted in a victory for the Royalists. He is not here primarily concerned with the wider issues of the battle—his theme is rather the defeat of his inveterate enemies the Campbells by Clan Donald. It is of interest to note the title of the poem as given by Turner: “Oran air latha blair Ionmhur-Lochaidh, eidear Clann Donuill agus na Caimbeulaich”.

Following upon the devastation perpetrated by the army of Montrose in Argyllshire in the winter of 1644 (Spalding, Trubles,

[TD 239]

Vol. II, pp. 315-330), Montrose with his army marched over Connel of Lorn until they came to Inverlochy (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 182). From Inverlochy, Montrose set out to Inverness to engage the Earl of Seaforth, who was reported to be on his way to attack him with a force of 5,000 horse and foot (Wishart, Memoirs of Montrose, p. 82). He had not gone far, according to MacVurich, when news was brought him that Argyll had entered Inverlochy and had burnt the Braes of Lochaber (ibid., p. 182). Argyll’s army consisted of 3,000 men (Spalding loc. cit., p. 443; Wardlaw MS., p. 292). Patrick Gordon states that it was when Montrose was at Kilcumin that information concerning Argyll’s movements was conveyed to him (Britane’s Distemper, p. 99). MacVurich makes no mention of the name of Montrose’s informant, and neither does Patrick Gordon, while Wishart merely states that the news was conveyed by a “trusty messenger” (p. 83). Balhaldy, Lochiel’s biographer, citing Bishop Guthry, states that Montrose’s informant was despatched by Ailean Dubh mac Dhòmhnaill Duibh, Alan Cameron, the aged Chief of Lochiel, whose grandson Ewen was at this time under the guardianship of Argyll (Memoirs of Lochiell, p. 70). Napier refers to a tradition current in his own day in Atholl, which stated that Iain Lom, the Keppoch bard, was the informant (Napier, Life and Times of Montrose, p. 293). This tradition is corroborated in the New Statistical Account of Kilmonivaig contained in the Statistical Account of Scotland (Vol. XIV, p. 508), and is the one adopted by Neil Munro in John Splendid. It is certain that Iain Lom was with the clans on their return march to Inverlochy, and that he was an eye-witness of the battle.

With Seaforth approaching from the north, and Argyll in his rear, both with forces numerically superior to his own (Montrose had 1,500 men; Wishart, p. 82), Montrose was in danger of being trapped in the Great Glen. He resolved, therefore, to attack Argyll at Inverlochy and thus to show, as Wishart states, that the latter could be beaten even in the Highlands, “where he was adored like some awful god by the simple people” (ibid., p. 83). In order that his attack on the enemy should be a surprise one, he decided to embark upon a route hitherto unattempted by any army, and on Friday 31st January set out from Kilcumin with his army on a march which has been described by John Buchan as “one of the great exploits in the history of British arms” (Montrose, p. 223). Iain Lom’s description an tionndadh duineil is well warranted. A good account of the severe climatic

[TD 240]

conditions which they had to contend with is given by Patrick Gordon (Britane’s Distemper, p. 100). MacVurich, who is the only authority for the route taken, states briefly that Montrose and his army marched over Lairig Thurraid into Glen Roy and over the Spean (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 184). It has been well reconstructed by Dr A. Cameron-Miller in an article entitled “Montrose in Lochaber, 1645”, Celtic Monthly, Vol. XVIII, p. 184; cf. also Watson, Rosg Gàidhlig, p. 237.

The manuscript sources are M, McL, McN.¹ The two latter, which supply a shorter version of the poem, are closely related and have the same sequence of lines and stanzas. The chorus is different, but otherwise differences in readings are mainly orthographical. Two stanzas which form part of the poem addressed to An Ciaran Mabach are incorporated in both sources (see Appendix C, p. 387, vv. 6, 7). The most complete version of the poem is that of Turner, on which the foregoing text is based. The seventh stanza is not found in this source, but is included in M, and a parallel version is supplied by McL and McN. The version in SO (reproduced in Am Filidh Gaidhealach, pp. 14-18, and Loyal Lochaber, pp. 431-433) and BGh is also based on Turner. Maclean Sinclair’s text derives from M with the exception of the following stanzas from Turner’s version: chorus, vv. 11, 14, 20 (in Text) O; vv. 11, 14, 16, 17, 20, GlB. The sixteenth and seventeenth stanzas, which refer to the Marquis of Huntly, are excluded from the text of the poem in O, on the grounds that they cannot be reconciled with historic facts. (See O, p. 115.) For a translation of the poem see MacDiarmid, Golden Treasury of Scottish Poetry, pp. 32-35. An English rendering of five stanzas is included by Napier in Life and Times of Montrose, p. 298.

188. iomairt (SO, BGh): in view of the unexpected nature of Montrose’s attack, and also from the point of view of assonance, this reading is preferable to that of the earlier versions.

191. Iain Lom did not witness the battle from the top of Inverlochy Castle as is stated in certain sources (Logan, Scottish Gael, Vol. II, p. 256; Celtic Magazine, Vol. V, p. l00; Lees, History of the County of Inverness, p. 311; Loyal Lochaber, p. 60, footnote; Clan Donald, Vol. II, p. 332). During the battle the castle was occupied by a detachment of about fifty soldiers from the Stirling Regiment, which formed part of Argyll’s army (Britane’s Distemper, pp. 101-102; Trubles, Vol. II, p. 444). The

[TD 241]

reading barr caisteil as given by T is therefore incorrect, and bràigh which is the reading in M is adopted.

194. Cùil Eachaidh: a hillock near Kilcumin, and therefore Iain Lom was obviously present during the return march to Inverlochy.

196-197. These lines refer to the depredations wrought by Argyll’s army in Lochaber, when, together with Sir Duncan Campbell of Auchinbreck, who had been recalled from Ireland, he went in pursuit of Montrose (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 183; Britane’s Distemper, p. 99).

202. Sir Mungo Campbell of Lawers, who with his left wing opposed Alasdair mac Cholla at Auldearn, and fell in that battle (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 187; cf. Earldom of Sutherland, p. 525). According to Alasdair mac Mhaighstir Alasdair he was left dispirited and speechless at Auldearn:

<gai>Mar sin ’san Allt-Eireann ur hero triath Labhar
A dh’fhàg Alasdair euchdach gun spéirid ’s gun labhairt,
An reubalach breun sin le réisimeid churst.<eng>

Likewise at Auldearn, the valiant Alasdair left your hero the laird of Lawers, spiritless and speechless—that loathsome rebel with his accursed regiment (Poems of Alexander MacDonald, p. 104; Highland Songs of the Forty-five, p. 58).

204. McL and McN read fear cleoc, probably to be interpreted as Lowlander. A similar reading is found in M, l. 206 (cf. l. 51 n.).

209. Cf. MacVurich’s statement: “do báthadh iliomad diobh ar bhun neimheis” (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 184).

213. The reference is to Sir Duncan Campbell of Auchinbreck, who, according to an anonymous poem, was killed by Mac Cholla’s own hand at Inverlochy (BGh, p. 213). A Lochaber tradition states that Mac Cholla offered him the choice of death by hanging or by execution, which elicited the reply—“Dà dhiù gun aon roghainn” (An Teachdaire Gàidhealach, Vol. II, p. 135; cf. Nicolson’s Gaelic Proverbs, p. 161).

222. Tom na h-aire: a hillock north of Inverlochy Castle.

230. mór dhragh na falachd: translation conjectural. Professor Matheson suggested comparison with mordraige (see Contribb.

[TD 242]

R.I.A. Dict.); ducorastar Dia deilb mordraige 7 fir boith forsinní Dauid, Ml. 52, translated “a form of great ruggedness” in Thes. Pal. Possibly a gen. of mordrach—cf. aurddrag, erdrach, sprite, spectre. (Cf. Stories from the Táin, Glossary.) The second part might then correspond to the second part of erdrach. The first part mor, compositional form of muir? But cf. R.I.A. Dict. Contribb. s.v. Morrigan, where it is mentioned that the first part has been regarded as cognate with O.H.G. mara and A.S. maere. Cf. Eng. nightmare (defined in Concise Oxford Dict. as “female monster sitting upon and seeming to suffocate sleeper, incubus, etc.”), Fr. cauchemar. Note also phrase fulacht na Morrigna (R.I.A. Dict. fulacht—cooking-pit etc.); mordhrach would represent a later form of mordrach, and so mordhragh (cf. aurddrag; g = gh?).

231. Cf. Alasdair mac Mhaighstir Alasdair’s reference to the battle of Inverlochy:

<gai>Na leig sibh am baiteal ud Lòchaidh air dhearmad,
’N uair a bha sinn le’r lannaibh mar choirce ’gur searbh-bhuain?<eng>

Have you forgotten that battle of Inverlochy, when with our swords we mowed you down like standing corn?
(Highland Songs of the Forty-five, p. 58)

242. John of Moidart, Chief of Clan Ranald, joined Montrose at Blair Atholl prior to the winter campaign in Argyll, and brought with him eight hundred of the strongest and most valiant men among the Highlanders, according to Patrick Gordon (Britane’s Distemper, p. 94). Over two hundred years later he is referred to as:

<gai>Iain Mùideartach an curaidh
Dh’iomair cluich air Lòchaidh.<eng>

John of Moidart, the warrior who waged a fight at Inverlochy.
(BGh, 11. 13-14)

245. Among the prisoners at Inverlochy was Sir Donald Campbell of Barbreck (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 184; Trubles, Vol. II, p. 296).

246. A reference to the success which Alasdair mac Cholla met with in 1644, when he went to recruit forces for the King’s cause (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 178).

249. The Earls of Huntly were Lieutenants-General of the

[TD 243]

North of Scotland—hence the designation “Cock of the North”. The precise meaning here is not clear, but the poet probably means that Alasdair mac Cholla, by reason of his recruiting success, was able to overcome Huntly’s lack of co-operation at this time. In Badenoch, for example, where Huntly was overlord, 300 of the Macphersons joined Mac Cholla under their Chief, Ewen Og (ibid.). Despite his alleged loyalty to the Royalist cause and his adamant refusal to join forces with the Covenanters even when he was their prisoner (Trubles, Vol. 1, p. 179), the Marquis of Huntly refused to co-operate with Montrose.

252. A feather from the inner corner of the wing was regarded as worthless. The prized feathers were those from the extremity of the wing and from the tail.

253. In his own right Huntly attempted to raise an army for the King in April 1644, but when Argyll approached he disbanded his army and fled to Strathnaver (Trubles, Vol. II, p. 326 ff.; Britane’s Distemper, p. 53).

259. According to MacVurich, Sir Lachlan Maclean of Duart, who joined Montrose at Inverlochy, had only about twelve of his people with him (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 182).


ORAN DO ALASDAIR MAC CHOLLA

The battle of Auldearn was fought on 9th May 1645 ten miles from Inverness between a Covenanting force under General Hurry and the Royalist army under Montrose. The poet regards Alasdair mac Cholla as the hero of Auldearn although he concedes that he was hard pressed until he was relieved by Montrose.

The oldest version of the poem is found in Turner. From this source derives directly the version in GlB and BGh. O is also based on T but in this version Maclean Sinclair has introduced a number of readings which differ substantially from the original. A stanza of his own composition is incorporated, and the final verse is excluded from the text of the poem. (See O, p. 116.)

276. Maclean Sinclair objects to the reading mórshluagh on the grounds that Mac Cholla had not a large host under him, and in the following stanza he uses the word còmhlan (l. 282) to describe the men who accompanied Mac Cholla, but the body of men who were under MacDonald’s command at Auldearn were more numerous than the word còmhlan would imply.

[TD 244]

MacVurich states that Alasdair mac Cholla had three hundred and fifty men under him, having received three hundred foot of the men of the Bog of Gight, Strathbogie and Braemar from Lord Gordon before the campaign commenced, in exchange for ninety of his own veteran soldiers. He was left with fifty of his own men whom he divided into two equal companies, placing one in the van and one in the rear, with the Gordon foot in the centre (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 186). The number mentioned by Wishart (p. 99) is four hundred foot, and with this figure Alasdair mac Mhaighstir Alasdair, writing a hundred years later, agrees. In his poem “Di-Moladh Chabair-Féidh”, which consists of a denunciation of the MacKenzies, he makes several references to the battle of Auldearn:

<gai>
Bha Frisealaich ’gur còmhnadh
’S iomadh seòrsa bharrachd orr’;
Bha Ionar-nis glé dhòmhail
Le móshluagh a’ ghearasdain;
Ach ’s aon cheithir cheud ’nan ònar
A bha fo òrdugh Alasdair
Thug bristeadh as ur mórshluagh
’S bha iomadh spòltadh salach ann.
<eng>

Frasers assisted you and many others besides. Inverness was very crowded with the army of the garrison. But four hundred only were under the command of Alasdair, and they caused a breach in your army and much foul hacking ensued.
(Poems of Alexander MacDonald, p. 298)

Patrick Gordon is silent on this point. He makes no mention of the exchange of men referred to by MacVurich, but states that Alasdair mac Cholla had a regiment of Gordons under his command (Britane’s Distemper, pp. 123-126).

279. Professor Watson reads a’ chrònain, but the possessive pronoun as found in the oldest source is a better reading in this context. For Mac Cholla’s marching song see Lloyd, Alasdair Mac Colla, p. 56 ff.

282. Patrick Gordon gives a good account of the formation of the ground on which Mac Cholla took his stand at Auldearn, while Wishart is more vague, but the description of the actual battle and the part played by Mac Cholla is given in much greater detail by Wishart. He states that Alasdair mac Cholla, in command of the right wing and not the left as stated by

[TD 245]

MacVurich, was placed on ground fortified with dykes, ditches, brushwood and rocks, which thus provided a natural defence, thought by Montrose to be secure from any attack. This position Mac Cholla abandoned when he was subjected to the enemy’s first onset; faced by superior numbers, and cut off from the main body of the army, he was compelled to retreat to a neighbouring enclosure (Wishart, p. 99 ff.). There he put up a heroic struggle against his assailants, who attacked him closely with pikes. “And when they had fastened a number of pickes in his tairge”, says Patrick Gordon, “they could not mak him to shrink or bow so much as on kne to the ground” (Britane’s Distemper, p. 124). With this statement may be compared a fragment of a contemporary song:

<gai>
Bu mhaith thu là Allt Eireann,
’N uair leum thu feadh nam pìc,
Is olc air mhaith gun éireadh dhuit
Chan éigheadh tu “Relìobh”.
<eng>

Valiant were you at the battle of Auldearn, when you jumped among the pikemen, and whether good or ill befell you, you would not cry out “Relief”.
(Highland Monthly, Vol. I, p. 282)

Maclean Sinclair substitutes le d’chòmhlan for ad ònar in this line as he maintains that Mac Cholla was not alone “in the garden sheep-pen or whatever the enclosure may have been” (O, p. 115), but the poet probably means that he was cut off from the rest of Montrose’s army and had to rely, in the first instance at any rate, on his own resources for defending his position.

285. Following upon Mac Cholla’s retreat, Montrose with his own infantry, who were on his left flank, attacked the cavalry on the right wing of Hurry’s army, commanded by Major Drummond. They did not withstand the concentrated charge, and took to flight, thus leaving the infantry ranks exposed. According to Wishart (p. 101) “the foot fought on doggedly and fell man by man almost where they stood”. The defeat of the foot by Montrose was the determining factor in the battle, for he was thus enabled to concentrate on the opposing forces who were harassing Mac Cholla, and release him from his enforced confinement.

Montròsa is the English form of the name taken over into Gaelic. The name of the town Montrose is in Gaelic Mun-rois. The Clan Ranald seanchaidhs have the form Montrosa.

[TD 246]

288. Kinnudie is three-quarters of a mile west from Auldearn, and there General Hurry drew up his men in preparation for battle. James Fraser writes, “Very many killed among the banks and buishes of Kinudy” (Wardlaw MS., p. 296). The number of casualties is not mentioned by MacVurich, or Patrick Gordon. Wishart states that about 3,000 foot of the enemy were slain (p. 101), while the number given by Spalding is 2,000 (Trubles, Vol. II, p. 473).

291. A reference to the battle of Inverlochy, at which the poet was present. Maclean Sinclair does not accept Turner’s reading, as given in the foregoing text, on the grounds that Inverlochy had no connexion with Auldearn; but the battle of Inverlochy had taken place only a few months previously, and the part played by Clan Donald was still fresh in the poet’s mind.

292. domhach is interpreted as a savage, and geinneach as a short stout man in GlB and O. They are to be taken rather as mocking names for Duncan and Kenneth, which were common names in the MacKenzie clan. Similarly, in the following stanza, Tomai and Simidh are used disparagingly for Thomas and Simon, names common among the Frasers. The MacKenzies and Frasers fought under General Hurry, hence the poet’s scorn for them. According to Fraser they had the heaviest casualties in the battle (Wardlaw MS., p. 296).

299. Presumably the poet means Elgin (in Gaelic Eilginn Muireibh) here.

300. The reference is to the burning of Beaufort Castle and to the destruction wrought by Montrose in the surrounding district when in April 1646 he crossed the Spey and laid siege to the Government garrison at Inverness. James Fraser, himself an eye-witness, states that “betwixt the bridge end of Inverness and Gusachan 26 miles, there was not left in my countrie, a sheep to bleet, or a cock to crow day, nor a house unruffled” (Wardlaw MS., p. 315).

An excellent reconstruction of the battle and a lucid account of the full advantage which Montrose took of the strategic position offered by the ground at Auldearn is given by Gardiner, History of the Great Civil War, Vol. II, pp. 224-227.

[TD 247]

SORAIDH DO’N GHRÀMACH

Despite the fact that this poem was composed shortly after Charles I had been surrendered to the English army, and at a time when Royalist hopes were at a very low ebb, the note throughout is one of optimism. After the Royalist forces had been disbanded, by order of the King, Montrose retired to the Continent on 3rd September 1646 (Wishart, p. 188), but Iain Lom regards his exile as a temporary expedient and looks forward with confidence to his return. Alasdair mac Cholla, the hero of several of his poems, left the country the following year and fled to Ireland, but there is no doubt in the poet’s mind that he also will return accompanied by a strong force of men to resume the campaign with renewed vigour. The poet’s hopes were not fulfilled, however, for later that year, Mac Cholla was killed.

The text followed is that of McL MS. 225. A similar version with the same sequence of lines and stanzas is found in McN¹. Several orthographical differences occur, but there are no major differences in readings. The poem has been edited in MC. A number of different readings have been introduced in this version and the second stanza has been omitted. In McL and MC the poem is arranged in stanzas of four long lines.

316. Thomas the Rhymer was endowed with the gift of prophecy by a fairy sweetheart, according to legend, and according to Hebridean tradition lived in Tomnahurich near Inverness, which was believed to be a sìdh-mound. (For further references to him in Gaelic poetry see BGh, p. 283. See also Campbell, Superstitions of the Scottish Highlands, pp. 269-272.)

330. Nar: na-ro with pres. subj., expressing a negative wish, now obsolete; cf. l. 2665.

331. fuil bhrùite: bruised, i.e. discoloured, blood.

345. Sìol Ilpin: reference obscure.

356. iad: the Covenanters.

372. The battle of Alford, which was fought on 2nd July 1645, resulted in a signal victory for Montrose over the army of the Covenant under General Baillie (Britane’s Distemper, p. 129 ff.; Wishart, p. 108 ff.; Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 192 ff.; cf. Dr W. Douglas Simpson’s article entitled “The Topographical Problem of the Battle of Alford”, Aberdeen University Review, Vol. VI, p. 248 ff.).

[TD 248]

The reference here is to Lord George Gordon, eldest son of the Marquis of Huntly, who fell in the battle. (For an account of his character and death see Britane’s Distemper, p. 130 ff.; Wishart, p. 110 ff.)


CUMHA ALASDAIR MHIC CHOLLA

After the battle of Kilsyth (15th August 1645) Alasdair mac Cholla left Montrose and proceeded to Argyllshire, there to exact reparation for the treatment meted out to his friends by the Marquis of Argyll (Guthry, Memoirs, pp. 158-159). The following year the Royalist forces were disbanded and Montrose, Huntly and Alasdair mac Cholla were ordered by the King to lay down their arms, “for each of them had a severall armie” (Britane’s Distemper, p. 194). The Scots army was remodelled and placed under the command of Lieutenant-General David Leslie, who proceeded forthwith to the north to subdue Huntly and then directed his attention to Alasdair mac Cholla, who continued to hold Kintyre and Islay by military force. Argyll joined forces with Leslie and attacked him unawares in Kintyre and dispersed his men. Sir James Turner, who was Adjutant-General of the combined forces of Leslie and Argyll, states that if he had occupied the passes leading into Kintyre as he should have done, it would have been almost impossible for an attacking force to enter the country. (Turner, Memoirs, p. 45.) Mac Cholla escaped to Islay and from there crossed to Ireland about the end of May 1647 (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, pp. 202-204; Guthry, Memoirs, p. 199). In Ireland he did not remain long inactive, as he was appointed Lieutenant-General of the Confederated Catholics, who at this time were offering resistance to a Parliamentary force under Murrogh O Brian, Baron Inchiquin. On 13th November 1647 the armies met at Cnoc na n-Os in Co. Cork, and while Mac Cholla was on an eminence reconnoitring, he was surrounded by fourteen of Inchiquin’s troopers and slain (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 204; MacDonnells of Antrim, pp. 112-114). Eachann Bacach the Maclean bard states:

<gai>Dh’imich Alasdair fhéin bhuainn
Thuit le baran an Eirinn.<eng>

Alasdair also has departed from us—he fell at the hands of a baron in Ireland.
(Maclean Bards, Vol. I, p. 38)

[TD 249]

The primary sources are E, McL MSS. 105, 136, McN¹,². McL 203 supplies one stanza only—a variant form of v. 20. The versions in McL 105 and McN¹,² are similar, although a few minor differences in readings do occur, while the version in McL 136 is less complete and contains a number of different readings. The order of stanzas does not appear to be established. E is closely related to McL 105 and McN¹,², and with the exception of vv. 3, 13, which are omitted in this source, the same sequence of lines and stanzas is observed. AM derives directly from Turner’s revised version of E, while O is based on the earlier edition. The longest extant version is found in I. (See Appendix D, p. 390.) The text followed here is that of McL 105 supplemented by several readings from E.

400. Cf.:
<gai>Nam b’e do chleachdadh an lùireach
Is claidheamh cùil a’ chinn aisnich.<eng>
(Allan MacDougall, Songs, p. 3; cf. pp. 20, 40, vv. 3, 1)

Alasdair mac Cholla’s sword, which had a steel apple running on a groove along the back, is preserved in Loughan Castle, County Tipperary (Dublin Magazine and Citizen for April 1843, pp. 25-26). For a description of a typical Scottish basket-hilted sword in its original condition, belonging to the last quarter of the seventeenth century, see Trans. Glasgow Archaeological Society, Vol. VIII, Part IV, Supplement, p. 16.

412. Colla Ciotach was in command of a garrison of 200 men which had been placed in Dunnyveg Castle in Islay by his son Alasdair mac Cholla. A stout resistance was offered to Leslie and Argyll, but the garrison eventually capitulated through lack of water. The terms of capitulation were that the officers were to be allowed to go where they chose, while the soldiers were to be transported to France. Colla Ciotach while conversing with the Captain of Dunstaffnage Castle was taken prisoner. Hill suggests that it was through the influence of this person, who was a Campbell, that Colla was betrayed to his enemies (MacDonnells of Antrim, p. 110; Turner, Memoirs, pp. 47-48). He was hanged by the orders of Argyll on 21st September 1647 (Guthry, Memoirs, p. 201; Turner, op. cit., p. 48). It is recorded that Colla Ciotach was hanged on the mast of his own galley, which was placed in the cleft of a rock in the immediate neighbourhood of Dunstaffnage Castle, where he had been imprisoned (Maclean, History of the Clan Maclean, pp. 145-146; cf. Lloyd, Alasdair Mac Colla, p. 10; Gregory, p. 414).

[TD 250]

418. The Campbells, whose descent in the oldest extant pedigrees is traced to Duibhne, wrongly alleged in later pedigrees to be the son of Diarmaid renowned in Fenian legend (Skene, Celtic Scotland, Vol. III, p. 459).

431. Ranald Og MacDonnell, one of the Irish officers whose exploits at Auldearn are referred to by MacVurich, was captured by Leslie and put to death at Inveraray (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, pp. 202-204).

432. A reference to those who accompanied the Marquis of Antrim when he returned to Ireland about the end of September 1647 (MacDonnells of Antrim, p. 273, n. 50). According to MacVurich a good many of the gentry of the Hebrides such as the Clan Maclean and the MacDonalds of Clan Ranald flocked to the standard of Antrim when he arrived in Kintyre in order to set an army afoot on behalf of the King. By the King’s own order Antrim disbanded his army and returned home (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 202).

435. Among those who accompanied Antrim was Angus Og. Chief of the MacDonalds of Glengarry, who joined Montrose at Inverlochy and remained with him throughout his campaign. Hill states that Glengarry had a regiment of Scottish Highlanders under his command (op. cit., p. 334, n. 213), but the poet here implies that very few men of his own clansmen accompanied him.

443. Iain Lom makes a brief reference only to what has been described as “one of the most disgraceful incidents in Scots history” (Macphail, Highland Papers, Vol. II, p. 248). In May 1647 Alasdair mac Cholla left a garrison of about 500 men (A.P.S., Vol. VII, p. 337) in Dunavertie Castle in Kintyre under Archibald Og MacDonald of Sanda. (The number stated by Turner is 300. Memoirs, p. 46.) The garrison, which consisted mainly of MacDougalls of Lorne and soldiers from Antrim, was attacked by the covenanting army under Lieutenant-General David Leslie and the Marquis of Argyll (MacDonnells of Antrim, p. 107). A strong resistance was offered but finally they surrendered when their water supply was cut off—“inexorable thirst made them desire a parley” (Turner, Memoirs, p. 47). The terms of capitulation offered by Leslie were that they should yield “on discretion or mercy” (ibid.). On emerging from the Castle the men of Dunavertie were all put to the sword with the exception of one young man, John MacDougall by name. Turner alleged that

[TD 251]

Leslie had been instigated to murder the prisoners by his Chaplain, Nevoy by name, who urged him not to spare them, and threatened him with the curses that befell Saul for sparing the Amalekites—identifying the latter with the men of Dunavertie (ibid.). At the trial of the Marquis of Argyll, at which he was a witness, he stated that Leslie had confessed to him on more that one occasion that were it not for the continued imprecations of his Chaplain, for which Argyll was directly responsible, he would not have committed that butchery (ibid., p. 240). The fact that Argyll was responsible for this cold-blooded massacre after quarter had been promised is stated by Guthry (Memoirs, p. 243), and also by Nicoll (Diary, p. 238). It is confirmed by a Decree in an action raised after the Restoration against Argyll and others who were concerned in the massacre (A.P.S., Vol. VII, p. 337).

Two contemporary documents relating to the massacre at Dunavertie—one containing the names of ninety of the slain—are cited in Highland Papers, Vol. II, pp. 248-260.

447-448. Cf.:
<gai>Luchd bhualadh nam buillean
Far’m bu chruaidh air càch fuireach.<eng>
(Mac Talla nan Tùr, p. 76)


IORRAM DO MHAC GILLE EATHAIN DHUBHAIRD

During the Civil War Sir Lachlan Maclean of Duart, who was a staunch Royalist, had paid none of the public dues, as he was of the opinion that the expenses involved by him in equipping and maintaining a force for the King’s cause entitled him to withhold payment. After the Royalist forces had been disbanded he had little inclination to pay them to those whom he regarded as usurpers. Argyll bought up all his debts, both public and private, and established a claim against him for £30,000 Scots. On his way to negotiate with Argyll, Sir Lachlan was arrested by him in Inveraray and imprisoned in Carrick Castle in 1647. His health declined and eventually he was prevailed upon by his friends to sign a bond acknowledging his debt to Argyll—this being the sole condition of his liberation (MacFarlane, Genealogical Collections, Vol. I, pp. 135-136; History of the Clan Maclean, pp. 175-176). It was during Maclean’s enforced confinement in Carrick Castle that this poem was composed.

The two primary sources are the manuscripts of Dr Maclean and of John Maclean. The sequence of lines in each stanza is the same in both sources, but the order of stanzas, with the ex-

[TD 252]

ception of the first three, is different. In the former the fifth stanza is not included while the latter source omits the twelfth stanza. The text followed is that of M supplemented by several readings from JM. The order of stanzas in M is followed with the exception of the tenth stanza, which in the original is interchanged with the succeeding one.

456. Maclean Sinclair reads do ranntachd—the line would thus mean “Foremost among your supporters”. Sir Lachlan was married to a daughter of Sir Roderick MacLeod of MacLeod (History of the Clan Maclean, p. 176). The MSS. read mo rantan (M), mo rauntachd (JM), my versification i.e. at the outset of my song.

459. The MacDonalds of Sleat, who claim descent from Hugh, son of Alexander, Earl of Ross and Lord of the Isles (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 7 ff.).

461. Note idiomatic usage of rùsgadh referring to weapons being got ready for action, in this case arrows being taken out of the quiver.

466. The Camerons.

477-479. Possibly a variant of the previous stanza.


GLACADH MORAIR HUNNDAIDH

This poem, which was composed shortly after the capture of the Marquis of Huntly in November 1647, shows that the poet’s attitude to the latter had undergone a complete transformation, as his sentiments here are very different from those to which he gave expression in his earlier poem on Inverlochy. What the reason for this transformation was one cannot say with certainty, but it may possibly be due to the fact that after the Royalist forces had been disbanded and Montrose had gone into exile overseas, Huntly mustered a force of men in response to a secret message which he claimed to have received from King Charles, who was then a captive, and during the winter of 1646 he remained in Banff, hoping to be joined there by the King when a favourable opportunity presented itself (Britane’s Distemper, p. 198 ff.; cf. Earldom of Sutherland, p. 536). This effort on behalf of the Royalist cause, although belated, may have restored him to favour in the poet’s eyes.

There are two versions of the poem, the shorter of which is

[TD 253]

supplied by Turner, entitled: “Oran do Mhoir-fhear Hunndaidh, nuair a ghlacadh e le Crùnair Mèinn ann am baile Strath bhalgaidh”. It consists of vv. 1, 2, 3, 4, 8, 11, and one additional stanza which refers to the skirmish of Sròn a’ Chlachain. The version in GlB, p. 14, derives from this source. The longer version entitled “Glacadh Mhoir’ir Hunti le Maidseir Meine” is found in McL MS. 137, and has been edited and translated in Highland Monthly, Vol. I. This version is also included in GlB, p. 328, and O. The foregoing text derives from McL.

507. tairrgne: McL reads tairne but the form must be tairrgne to preserve liquid svarabhakti rhyme with airgid.

512. General Leslie and Major Middleton were despatched to the north with a strong detachment to reduce Huntly—the former with a force of several thousand foot and twelve hundred horse, while the latter had about six hundred horse. The Marquis, who had meantime taken to flight, was pursued by Middleton, while Leslie attacked the Gordon castles of Huntly and the Bog of Gight, and when they had surrendered he placed garrisons in them (Britane’s Distemper, p. 199).

514. Patrick Gordon states that his enemies would not permit the Marquis to be imprisoned in Edinburgh Castle, where noblemen used to be put, but instead placed him in the common gaol, where he was allowed only two rooms for himself and his servants (ibid., p. 205).

518. Red deer were first introduced to the Bog of Gight by George, first Marquis of Huntly, father of the subject of this poem (Spalding, Trubles, Vol. I, p. 73 n.).

520. Major—afterwards Lieutenant-Colonel—Menzies, Huntly’s captor, is described by James Fraser as “a base villain” (Wardlaw MS., p. 335). He is known in Gaelic as Crùnair Ruadh nan Cearc, “Red-haired Crowner of the Hens”. The term crùnair was applied to one who lifted the (crown) rents, some of which were paid in kind. The lowest form of kind in the Highlanders’ estimation consisted of hens, the “cain fowl” by which rents were sometimes paid in part. Hence Crùnair nan Cearc is a term of contempt. A reward of £1,000 had been promised to any who should apprehend Huntly, which sum was duly paid to Menzies by the Committee of the Estates. (See Act of Sederunt of the Committee in the Appendix to William Gordon’s History of the Family of Gordon, Vol. II, p. 547.)

[TD 254]

thachar: straying, wandering. “Stray sheep are commonly called caoraich thachair to the present day” (HM, Vol. I, p. 278). The reference here may be to the fact that Menzies had wandered about the Continent as a soldier of fortune at an earlier stage of his career, or that he had on this occasion covered a considerable distance before he eventually discovered Huntly’s hiding place.

522. The poet’s statement is corroborated by Gordon of Sallagh, who asserts that Huntly was captured in November 1647 (Earldom of Sutherland, p. 537), while Montereul, in a letter to Cardinal Mazarin written in Edinburgh and dated 7th December 1647, refers to the capture of the Marquis of Huntly by Lieutenant-Colonel Menzies in a house called Elnabo in the district of Strathdowne (Fotheringham, Diplomatic Correspondence of Jean de Montereul, Vol. II, p. 346). D. P. Menzies states that Huntly was captured in December 1647, as does also the Gordon historian (The “Red and White” Book of Menzies, p. 292; History of the Family of Gordon, Vol. II, p. 545).

525. Dalnabo, the scene of Huntly’s capture, is in Strathavon, as stated by the poet, and not in Strathdon as stated by Montereul, D. P. Menzies, and James Fraser.

526. The word brothair is translated “betrayer” (as though brathadair) in HM, Vol. I, p. 280. James Fraser does in fact state that Huntly was betrayed by Alexander Innes of Condraught “his supposed trusty friend” (Wardlaw MS., p. 334). The word used in McL is, however, brothair, butcher, bruiser.

529. Parliament ordained that Huntly’s head should be cut off, but his body was interred in Seaton (Balfour, Annals of Scotland, Vol. III, p. 393).


CUMHA MORAIR HUNNDAIDH

This lament was composed after the death of the Marquis of Huntly, who was executed at the Mercat Cross in Edinburgh on Thursday 22nd March 1649 (A.P.S., Vol. II, p. 327), and before the arrival of Montrose in Scotland on 12th April 1650. The poet rebukes Charles II for his tardiness in coming to claim his kingdom, and reminds him of the hardships which his loyal subjects now suffer at the hands of the Covenanters.

The text followed is that of McL MS. 106, where the poem is entitled “Srath chuaiche”. The position of vv. 2, 3, has been

[TD 255]

altered as against the original, where they are placed fourteenth and ninth respectively. The final stanza is placed fifth from the end in this source but it is indicated in the manuscript that it is the last verse. A similar version of the poem is found in McN¹, the only major difference being that the second last stanza is omitted. With the exception of v. 6 (foregoing text), which is placed fifth in McN, the sequence of lines and stanzas is the same in both sources. A shorter version, containing a substantial number of different readings, is found in T entitled: “Oran do Dhiuc Gordon, ri linn fogradh Righ Tearlach a dhà”. It consists of: chorus (v. 11 foregoing text), vv. 1, 2, 4, 3, 6, 7, 8, 10, 12, in that order. Similar to T is the version in I entitled: “Oran da Dhuic Gordan—Ri linn fogradh Righ Tearlach a dha”. O is based on T, while MC derives directly from McL, with several readings emended.

534. Iarl Einnidh: the Earl of Enzie—Enzie being part of Huntly’s property in Banffshire. (Cf. Marcus na h-Einne, BGh, l. 2555.) In the proclamation read out at the Mercat Cross, Aberdeen, 16th March 1639, appointing Huntly the King’s Lieutenant in the north of Scotland, he is referred to as “Marquis of Huntly, Earl of Enzie, Lord Gordon and Badzenocht” (Spalding, Trubles, Vol. I, p. 145). Iain Lom gives no specific reference here to the exact location of Huntly’s camping place in 1647 when he was a fugitive. Patrick Gordon merely states that he was pursued to Badenoch and from there to Lochaber, where he was betrayed to General Middleton—his camping place being “in such a strong ground” that without the aid of the country people his enemies could do him no harm (Britane’s Distemper, p. 204).

549. foirinn: pl. of foireann, a troop (in this context of chessmen). In the “Birlinn” Alasdair mac Mhaighstir Alasdair uses the form fòirne as nom. sg.—fòirne fearail—a manly crew, but both forms are used by him in “Oran nam Fineachan”—am fòirne fearail, and am foirionn guineach. (Songs of Alexander MacDonald, pp. 376, 80. Cf. l. 171 n.)

554. Cf.:
<gai>A’ cur sàraidh air fìon
Is ’ga leigeadh an gnìomh òircheard.<eng>
(Songs of Mary MacLeod, ll. 763-764)

567. I.e. arrows.

573-574. Huntly’s lands were forfeited in 1647, and Argyll and his son, Lord Lorne, took possession of them and occupied the

[TD 256]

Bog of Gight and Strathbogie till the Restoration (A.P.S., Vol. VI, 1, p. 786; Vol. VII, p. 570).

576. allaidh: MSS. read alloil (distinguished, glorious, renowned), which is surely an error.

579 ff. Charles I was executed on 30th January 1649, and the Prince of Wales proclaimed King of Great Britain, France and Ireland on 5th February—his proclamation in Scotland being qualified by the condition that he should ratify the National Covenant and the Solemn League and Covenant (A.P.S., Vol. VI, II, p. 157). At this time the young King was at the Hague as the guest of his brother-in-law the Prince of Orange, and commissioners from Church and State left Scotland with the intention of getting his signature to the Covenant. The poet is somewhat impatient with the tardiness displayed by the King in claiming his kingdom and avenging his father’s death. On 17th September 1649 he did in fact land in Jersey, but a Parliament fleet forced him to return to Holland in February 1650.

593-594. I.e. do not pretend to be dignified if you are not prepared to live up to it.

599-600. A reference to the exclusion or near-exclusion of the Royalists by the Covenanting clergy from ecclesiastical privileges as though they were unbaptised.

603. The reference is to the supporters of Charles I, who lost even the right to reside in their own country, prominent among whom was of course Montrose (Balfour, Historical Works, Vol. IV, pp. 65-66), while the Marquis of Argyll, on the other hand, benefited to the extent of £45,000 for alleged loss of rent and damage to his property (A.P.S., Vol. VI, I, pp. 643, 675)

619. A scathing reference to the Parliament Navy. On 11th January 1649 Prince Rupert set sail for Ireland, where the Marquis of Ormonde led a Royalist rising. At the end of January he anchored in Kinsale Bay, and while he was there it was reported that no ship dared venture out of the Bristol Channel by daylight. Rupert’s occupation of Kinsale had brought home to Parliament the fact that unwonted efforts must be made to strengthen the navy if the mercantile marine was to be protected, and as early as 2nd February it was resolved to add thirty merchant ships to the armed force of the Commonwealth (Journals of the House of Commons, Vol. VI, p. 129; Gardiner, History of the Commonwealth and Protectorate, Vol. I, p. 15 ff.).

[TD 257]

627. Cf. ll 309-316.

635. an roth: the wheel of fortune; elsewhere a’ chuibhle (ll. 910, 2224).

641. I.e. Cromwell.


CUMHA MHONTRÒIS

The poet gives expression to his grief at the death of Montrose, and to his indignation at his betrayal by Neil MacLeod of Assynt, whom he reproaches bitterly and scathingly. The general state of the country at this time is deplored by him and his outlook is one of gloom. A good account, based on contemporary records, of the events leading up to the defeat of Montrose at Carbisdale in Sutherlandshire (27th April 1650), his flight and subsequent capture, is given by Morland and Simpson in their edition of Wishart’s Memoirs of Montrose, pp. 249-321.

The two primary sources, which appear to be independent of each other, are S, where the poem is entitled “Oran cumhaidh air cor na Rioghachd”; and McL MS. 5, which bears the title “Mu ghlacadh Mhontrois”. The former source is followed here with the exception of the twelfth stanza, which is found only in SO. A different version of the third stanza is quoted by Campbell, and one stanza, a variant of v. 14, is supplied by McL MS. 241, with no heading. SO and O are based on S. A further version of the poem, with a number of independent readings, is found in I.

651. Druim-uachdair: i.e. the ridge of the upper ground, or the top or highest ridge. Druim, Eng. Drum, a ridge, is very common in place-names throughout Scotland, e.g. Druim-na-Drochaid in Inverness-shire, Druim-an-tulaich in Atholl, Perthshire. Probably the best-known example is Druim Alban, the great mountain chain which runs from Dunbartonshire in the south to Caithness in the north. It is translated by Adamnan as Britanniae Dorsum, the ridge or backbone of Britain (Reeves, Vita Sancti Columbae, I, p. 64).

653-654. I.e. I am sad and the gloom but increases my dejection.

656. Alasdair mac Cholla.

657-658. The submission of the country to the Protector.

659. cìos: tribute (cf. l. 2381).

càin (L. census): in Scottish Gaelic tax, tribute; originally

[TD 258]

denoted a burden upon land. The càin in Scotland was a fixed payment in kind, the nature of the payment changing in the course of its history. In Old Irish cáin meant originally law, and in the preface to the first volume of the Senchus Mór, a compilation of Old Irish Law Texts, it is described as a law or decision which applied to the whole of Ireland. It was also applied to certain branches of the law such as Cáin Airraith, law of fosterage, etc. (See Cameron, Celtic Law, pp. 173-174; Skene, Celtic Scotland, Vol. III, pp. 227-229.) In 1652 a general assessment of £10,000 per month was levied on Scotland to support the army of occupation (Firth, Scotland and the Commonwealth, p. xxx).

661. calpa: the calp was the equivalent of the Lowland heriot due to the landlord, and consisted of the best stock given to him on the death of a vassal.
