Calps were abolished in 1617 by an Act of Parliament (Skene, op. cit., pp. 318, 368; Grant, Social and Economic Development of Scotland, p. 503). The reference here is probably to the extortionate sums of money wrested from the Scots for the upkeep of Cromwell’s garrisons.

663. faireigneadh: metri causa. The general reading is foireigneadh.

670. Seoc: all versions, with the exception of I, read siuc, rendered “away, begone, disperse”, SO, Glossary. The final vowel rhyme of each stanza is o throughout the poem, and it is probable that this stanza should follow a similar pattern.

681. leómhann: often applied in Gaelic to a brave or courageous person. In “Gaoir nam Ban Muileach”, for example, Mairearad Nighean Lachlainn, a younger contemporary of Iain Lom, refers to Sir Hector Maclean thus:

<gai>Ard leómhann bu mhùinte.<eng>
(BGh, l. 3606)

In this poem it has perhaps the added significance that the standard of Montrose portrayed a lion rampart (Balfour, Historical Works, Vol. III, p. 440).

682. Montrose was executed at the Mercat Cross, Edinburgh, on 21st May 1650, on a gallows 30 feet high which had been erected on a four-foot square scaffold (Wardlaw MS., p. 359). The last phase of his career is well reconstructed by Morland and Simpson in Wishart’s Memoirs of Montrose, pp. 322-324. See also

[TD 259]

the account given by John Nicoll, a notary public at this time, and himself an eye-witness, contained in his Diary, p. 12 ff.

With regard to the general description of Montrose given in this poem, it is of interest to note the close similarity which it bears to that given by James Fraser of Wardlaw, who witnessed the final scenes in the career of Montrose: “He stept along the streets with so great state, and there appeared in his countenance so much beuty, majesty and gravity, as amazed the beholders; and many of his enemies did acknouledg him to be the bravest subject in the world, and in him a gallanty that braced all the croud, more becoming a monarch than a mean peer” (Wardlaw MS., p. 359).

691. Father and son bore the name of Neil. (See 1738 MS. cited Trans. Gael. Soc. Inv., Vol. XVI, p. 198 ff.).

699-700. Neil MacLeod was married to Christian, daughter of John Munro of Lemlair (Earldom of Sutherland, p. 555).

703. The abhall or apple tree was regarded as one of the most highly prized of trees, and in an Old Irish tract it is classed among the “chieftain trees”—Airigh fedha (Calder, Auraicept na nÉces, p. 88). Hence it is frequently used in Gaelic poetry to denote the chieftain and his lineage. Màiri Nighean Alasdair Ruaidh in her elegy to Roderick MacKenzie, Laird of Applecross, refers to him as “A lùb abhall nam buadh”—“Thou scion of the apple-tree of virtues” (Gaelic Songs of Mary MacLeod, l. 183). In her lament for Roderick MacLeod, 17th Chief of Harris and Dunvegan, she uses a similar metaphor:

<gai>Gur e abhall an lios so
Tha mise ag iargain.<eng>

The apple tree of this orchard do I now lament.
(ibid., ll. 613-614)

708-710. The poet here states bluntly that Montrose was betrayed by MacLeod in return for meal from Leith, the greater part of which was sour. Neil MacLeod’s betrayal of Montrose has been the subject of keen controversy (National Observer, March-April 1892), but in the light of contemporary evidence it is impossible to repudiate the poet’s statement. The evidence of witnesses hostile to Montrose is of particular significance in this connexion. There is first of all the account given by Gilbert Gordon of Sallagh, himself a Covenanter, written not later than

[TD 260]

1651 (see Wishart, Appendix XII), in which he states that Neil MacLeod was instructed by his brother-in-law, Captain Andrew Munro, son of Munro of Lemlair, to apprehend any who should happen to come to Assynt, especially Montrose. MacLeod despatched parties in various directions and one of these came upon Montrose, who was accompanied by Major Sinclair, whereupon both were escorted to MacLeod’s Castle of Ardvreck (Earldom of Sutherland, p. 555). Sir James Balfour, Lyon King-of-Arms, also states that Montrose was captured by MacLeod’s tenants (Balfour, Historical Works, Vol. IV, p. 10).

According to a local tradition, MacLeod was away from home at this time, as he had gone to Caithness with the intention of joining Montrose (Old Stat. Acc. of Kincardine, pp. 511-512; cf. also MS. of 1738, where it is stated that MacLeod was sixty miles from Ardvreck when Montrose was taken there). According to the evidence of the anonymous author of Montrose Redivivus, pp. 178-179, MacLeod was in charge of the party that captured Montrose. The latter recognised him as a former follower and, expecting friendship from him, revealed his identity. Both James Fraser and John Nicoll are very emphatic in their statement that Montrose was betrayed by Neil MacLeod, from whom he expected protection. “This Asson was the Marques his follower before”, says James Fraser, while Nicoll refers to Neil MacLeod’s father as “ane of his auld acquentance and complyer with him in his former plottes and bloodie courses” (Wardlaw MS., p. 353; Diary, p. 11). It is reasonable to suppose that Montrose would have expected sanctuary in this quarter. When the Earl of Seaforth joined Montrose at Inverness in 1646 he had 100 of MacLeod’s men with him (Document of 1738, p. 201, cited above), and while it cannot be proved conclusively that MacLeod was a Royalist in 1650, yet it seems probable that his political views would be on a par with those of Seaforth, who in that year was a Royalist.

The Parliamentary Records of the period confirm MacLeod’s treachery, and show that he claimed and was granted the reward that the Estates had offered for the capture of Montrose (A.P.S., Vol. VI, II, pp. 563, 568, 594). The reward, first placed at £250,000 Scots, was later reduced to £20,000 Scots—Sir Robert Farquhar being commissioned to make the award (ibid., p. 560; cf. Balfour, Historical Works, Vol. IV, pp. 35, 36). There is no evidence extant to show whether or not MacLeod ever received this money, but he did apparently receive 400 bolls of meal from Sir Robert

[TD 261]

Farquhar in return for his services in capturing Montrose (Scott-Moncrieff, Records of Justiciary Court, p. 226). According to Wishart, Farquhar’s receipts for the meal were extant after the Restoration (Appendix XIII, p. 525).


BRIAN AGUS IAIN LOM

Iain Lom and Brian, the bard of Assynt, meet at one of the annual markets in Inverness, each being ignorant of the other’s identity (GlB, p. 24). On ascertaining that the person with whom he was conversing was a native of Lochaber, Brian asks him to convey a none too complimentary greeting to Iain Manntach. The latter replies in kind and displays his scathing powers of repartee in his impromptu reply.

Very little is known of the Assynt bard, who flourished in the second half of the seventeenth century. An elegy to Captain MacKenzie of Suddie, who was killed in the battle of Mulroy (1688), is ascribed to him, and has been edited in the MacDonald Collection (p. 156). The Editors state that they have in their possession a song of eulogy addressed to John MacKenzie of Applecross, known as “Iain Mollach”, who succeeded his father in 1646 (ibid., p. xxix). I have been informed by the Rev. William Matheson that both elegy and eulogy form part of the McL MSS., and were restored to the Library of Glasgow University in 1941.

The text followed is that of Turner, from which derives also the version in GlB and O, with the exception of one additional stanza not found in the original.

719. mhìorailtich: the form in GlB is mhi’raltach, which is explained as not skilful or prompt, not distinct in utterance, while the form in O is miorailteach—incomprehensible. It is probably a syncopated form of mhì-riaghailtich, unruly.

721. There are various forms of the word meaning a grinding stone or whet-stone, e.g. clach nianradh, clach-lìobhair. The form in Turner is clach shniaraidh (probably from nianraidh, the medial n being dropped before r). The Lochaber form is clach lìonraith.

736. Maclean Sinclair’s reading, dhèabh, appears to be the correct one. A similar usage is found in G (p. 183):

<gai>’S a’ cheart uair a bha thu guineach
Thun ar fuil a dhèabhadh.<eng>

At the very time when you were keen to drain our blood.

[TD 262]

741. thunndaidh (rendered by Maclean Sinclair thionndaidh), derives according to Mr Angus MacLeod from tunnta—an old gen. form of tunnadh (or tonnadh). For a note on the development of the word, and examples of its usage, see MacLeod, Songs of Duncan Ban Macintyre, l. 1533 n.

746. A reference to the surrender of Charles I by the Scots Army to the English Parliament in 1647. His safety had been ensured conditionally on his signing the Solemn League and Covenant, and this he had refused to do (Montereul, Correspondence, Vol. I, pp. 163-164). The Scots, realising that the Parliament was anxious to secure the King, decided to use him as a means for extracting the arrears of pay which were long overdue them, and they ultimately consented to accept £400,000, half of which was to be paid immediately, and the remainder by instalments (A.P.S., Vol. VI, 1, p. 634; Montereul, op. cit., pp, 353-355, 441, 444). The Earl of Sutherland was a Covenanter—hence the poet’s taunt to his clansman in these lines.

A common expression in English at this time was:

Traitor Scot,
Sold his King for a groat!

while the equivalent in French was:

L’Écosse, parjuré à sa foi,
Pour un denier vendid son Roi!
(Lang, History of Scotland, Vol. III, p. 182)

747-750. The betrayal of Montrose by Neil MacLeod of Assynt is here referred to. Robert Bain states that the story of the sour meal originated with Iain Lom—“a West Highland bard and satirist of repute”—and that it was a figment of his imagination (History of Ross, p. 195). As has been noted above, however, the poet’s statement is corroborated by contemporary evidence.

An additional stanza said to have been addressed by the Keppoch bard to Brian on this occasion is quoted in MC (p. xxix):

<gai>Le mheud ’s a fhuair mi bhagradh orm,
Gun d’chum e raoir an cadal uam;
’S chan ioghnadh mi bhith abartach,
’S ann agam a tha chòir.<eng>

[TD 263]

DÒMHNALL GRUAMACH AGUS IAIN LOM

The poet’s antagonist on this occasion may have been Dòmhnall Gruamach, youngest son of John, fourth of Bohuntin. The MacDonalds of Bohuntin were descended from Iain Dubh, third son of Ranald, seventh Chief of Keppoch, from whom derived the patronymic of the Keppoch family. Although Dòmhnall Gruamach was not such a well-known poet as his elder brother Dòmhnall Donn, several of whose compositions are still extant, his powers of repartee are shown to good advantage against the invective of his opponent.

Another possibility is that Iain Lom’s antagonist was Sir Donald MacDonald, third Baronet of Sleat, when he was still his father’s heir.

The text followed is mainly that of McL MS. 163. The manuscript is somewhat tattered, however, and in three stanzas the beginnings of some lines are missing. Where the manuscript is defective the text follows McN¹,², which supply similar versions. A further version of the poem obtained in Brae Lochaber about a century later has been edited by “Abrach” (D. C. Macpherson) in An Gàidheal, Vol. V. The same sequence of lines and stanzas is observed but several different readings occur. In the three sources it is stated that the dialogue took place between the bard and Gill-easbuig Gruamach (Mac Mhic Dhonuill, McL; Mac Mhatha’-Coibhnil, McN). Gill-easbuig Gruamach was the soubriquet of the Marquis of Argyll, and it seems probable that a confusion of names has arisen here and that the person concerned is not Gill-easbuig but Dòmhnall Gruamach. This conjecture is supported by line 779, where the latter is referred to as the poet’s kinsman. Maclean Sinclair, whose version derives from AG, makes a similar alteration.

783. fuidheall feachda: the leavings of a host; men who were rejected as unfit for service were described as such (Watson, Scottish Verse from the Book of the Dean of Lismore, pp. 268-269).

Cf.:
<gai>Mìleadh is fuidheall feachta
ní cóir a gcur cuideachta.<eng>

“Unmeet it is to place together a warrior and the leavings of a host”.
(Ibid., ll. 480-481)

According to the Rev. William Matheson the term has survived in Ness, Lewis, in the corrupted form fiudhall-feach.

786. clàr: the common wooden dish out of which all ate. (Cf.

[TD 264]

BGh, l. 959 n.) The poet is represented as living on the leavings.

788. mac mo cheann-cinnidh: if this reading, found in the earliest sources, is the correct one, the poet’s antagonist could not have been a son of MacDonald of Bohuntin, in which case the more general reading, Chaill thu dualchas do chinnidh, as supplied in AG, should be adopted.

792. beò, which is inserted after fhear in McN and AG, is almost certainly an interpolation, as the line is thereby hyper-metric.

798. A reference to the members of the clan at the chief’s board.

There is record of a further encounter which Iain Lom had with Dòmhnall Gruamach, when the latter addresses him as follows:

<gai>
Thugadh greis air Greumaich leat
Gu ’n euchdan a chur suas,
Is thugadh greis air Duibhnich leat
’S air muinntir an taoibh tuath;
Chan eil feum do Dhòmhnallach
Ri bheò bhith ort a’ luaidh,
’S e donnal a’ choin bhadhail ud
Dh’fhàg bodhar mo dhà chluais.
<eng>

Only one line of Iain Lom’s reply is extant:

<gai>“A shean chràidhneach mhor nan smugaidean”,<eng>

which shows that he follows the same scurrilous vein as in the poem above (MacDonald Bards, p. 97).


IORRAM DO MHAC GILLE EATHAIN DHUBHAIRD

These lines are addressed to Allan Maclean who in 1651 succeeded his brother, Hector Roy, as 19th Chief of Maclean. The death in close succession of Sir Lachlan and Sir Hector, two staunch pillars of the Clan, has cast a gloom over the poet, and the general condition of the country, which was at this time under the heel of the Protector, does nothing to alleviate his depression.

The four primary sources are M, McL, McN², JM. The version in AG is based on the first of these, as is that in O. The text followed here is that of M, supplemented by several readings

[TD 265]

from McL, and including two stanzas from the latter not found in any other source (vv. 12, 15). The McL and McN versions are closely related. In the former source several alternative readings are supplied, of which one only is found in McN, but otherwise there are no substantial differences. A shorter version of the poem is found in JM. It consists of the first five stanzas and vv. 16, 17, 11 in that order. With the exception of the first five stanzas the sequence differs in M, McL, JM, but the order of lines in each stanza remains the same throughout. The same sequence of lines and stanzas is observed in McN and McL apart from two stanzas in the latter not found elsewhere. A further version is found in I. (See Appendix E, p. 393.)

809-810. I.e. it was not a matter of routine.

812. Sir Lachlan Maclean of Duart, who died on 18th April 1648, and was buried in Iona (MacFarlane, Genealogical Collections, pp. 135-136; History of the Clan Maclean, pp. 175-176).

815. Hector Roy, eldest son of Sir Lachlan, fell in the battle of Inverkeithing, which was fought on 20th July 1651. He is described in one source as “one of the compleatest Gentlemen of his Time for Parts and Person” (MacFarlane, op. cit., p. 136). The Macleans suffered heavy losses at Inverkeithing. According to Iain mac Ailein, seven hundred of their number fell:

<gai>Thuit Eachann Ruadh ann an Inbhir-chéitein
Agus seachd ceud fear de threunfhuil dhìrich.<eng>
(Maclean Bards, Vol. I, p. 112)

In connexion with the battle there is a well-known tradition that eight of Sir Hector’s clansmen came forward in a vain endeavour to protect the person of their Chief. They fell in close succession, but each was replaced by another who came forward with the cry: “Fear eile air son Eachainn” (History of the Clan Maclean, p. 180).


DO MHAC FHIONGHUIN AN T-SRATHA

The subject of this poem is Lachlan Mackinnon of Strathswordale, 16th Chief of the Clan, who was born in 1628. During his minority he was placed under the protection of Archibald, 8th Earl and 1st Marquis of Argyll, but notwithstanding this fact he declared for the crown on attaining his majority, and

[TD 266]

fought on behalf of King Charles II at Worcester in 1651—the Clan having already fought at Inverlochy and Auldearn. According to the author of Clan Fingon, he was knighted on the field of battle, and in one version of the present poem there is a single reference to him as An Ridir, but of this honour there is no further evidence (Celtic Review, Vol. IV, p. 38; Mackinnon, Memoirs of Clan Fingon, p. 30 ff.).

The text followed is that of the McN MSS., which is closely related to the McL MS. version. In the latter the second and third stanzas are transposed, otherwise the sequence of lines and stanzas is the same and differences in readings are few. The former version has been edited in GSI, XXVII, and BGh (1st and 2nd edns.). The versions in E and OG bear a close similarity to each other but they are not identical. They contain three stanzas not found in any other source (except GB which is based on E), and with the exception of the sixteenth stanza in OG, not found elsewhere, the sequence of lines and stanzas is the same in both. In BGh (3rd edn.) a number of readings from the E version have been adopted. For an English translation see The Golden Treasury of Scottish Poetry, pp. 97-99.

It is questionable whether the author of this poem is in fact Iain Lom, although it is ascribed to him in McN. In E it is ascribed to Griogair Og Mac Griogair, while in McL and OG no authorship is stated.

872. The Mackinnons claimed descent from Alpin father of Kenneth mac Alpin, as did also the MacGregors and the Grants.

Cf.:
<gai>An t-Ailpineach dubh fìrinneach
’Gan dùthchas cian an Srath.<eng>
(BGh, ll. 3986-3987)

This claim, however, is unhistorical.

877. Cf.:
<gai>Bu tu beannachd na tuatha,
’S tu nach teannadh iad cruaidh mu’n a mhàl.<eng>
(Songs of John MacCodrum, ll. 1279-1280)

886. In a bond of friendship entered into by Lachlan Mór and James MacGregor on 6th June 1671, the MacGregors and the Mackinnons are represented as descendants of “twa breethern of auld descent”, i.e. Doungallus and Findanus, grandsons of King Alpin (Skene, Highlanders of Scotland, p. 342; Memoirs of Clan Fingon, p. 35). Nevertheless the alleged kinship is to be regarded

[TD 267]

as a fiction of the seanchaidhs. See Skene, Celtic Scotland, Vol. III, pp. 487, 488.

891. The pine was the clan badge of the Grants.

893. Lachlan Mackinnon married as his second wife Marion MacLeod of Dunvegan (Celtic Review, Vol. IV, p. 38; Memoirs of Clan Fingon, p. 36).


CRÙNADH AN DARA RÌGH TEÀRLACH

The poet, having heard of the return of King Charles II, breaks his long silence, and gives expression to the joy and exuberance which he experienced on this occasion. The poem reflects the spirit of joyful relief with which the Restoration was acclaimed in the Highlands. The wheel of fortune has turned in favour of the loyal clans, restrictions which were imposed during the usurpation are now gone, and their period of bondage is at an end. The poet is at liberty to move about the country, and he rejoices in the knowledge that once again he is a free agent, now that the King has come into his own.

The text followed is that of T, where the poem is entitled “Oran le Iain Lom, air dha a chluinntinn an dara Righ Tearlach bhi air a chrunadh”. From this source derives the version in SO and O, although the latter introduces a number of readings which differ substantially from the original. The eighteenth stanza is an emended version of the eleventh in “Tùirneal a’ Chnatain” (in Text), and vv. 20, 21, 23 have been omitted because the Editor objects to their content. The last stanza is also omitted in this version. In I the poem is entitled: “Oran air Crunadh Righ Tearlach 2d—Le Iain Lom, air dha bhi chomhnuidh ’n Clachaig”. This version is related to T, but there are several differences in readings, and four stanzas are not included, viz. vv. 13, 14, 15, 21. McL MS. 106 supplies six stanzas with no heading, viz., vv. 1, 7, 8, 10, 11, 12 in that order. These are also found in McN¹ with several minor orthographical differences, and three additional stanzas—variants of vv. 3, 16, 18. In this source the poem bears the following heading: “Oran do’n Righ air son an d’fhuair Ian Lom £50 pr. ann, a shuidheachadh air ged nach b’fhada mheal se e”. For an English rendering of fourteen stanzas of the poem see C, pp. 168-169.

905. A reference to his enforced silence during the usurpation.

929-931. The poet expresses his disapproval of those who

[TD 268]

abandoned the King’s cause when it was weak, but who proceeded to ingratiate themselves with the King now that he was restored to power. Nicoll states that many departed from Scotland under pretext of congratulating the King, but in actual fact to procure honours and dignities for themselves, and in general to promote their own interests (Diary, p. 295). Among these was the Marquis of Argyll, who at the end of June 1660 went up to London to seek the royal favour, but was committed to the Tower by order of the King (ibid., p. 334).

935. A reference to their lack of enthusiasm and general apathy.

947. On Wednesday 13th February 1661 the Marquis of Argyll was summoned before the Scottish Parliament to answer a charge of high treason (ibid., p. 321).

953. The “Maiden” was the Scottish beheading machine, which continued in use until 1710, and which is still preserved in the Museum of Antiquities at Edinburgh. For a detailed account of its history and notices of criminals who suffered by it, see W. T. McCulloch’s article entitled “The Maiden or Scottish beheading machine” contained in the Proceedings of the Society of Antiquaries of Scotland, Vol. VII, App., pp. 539-556; Duncan MacRae refers to it as “maighdean sgoraidheach sgathail” (Fernaig MS., p. 181).

fiaras: a reference to his obliquity of vision or crookedness of conduct. Cf. contemporary statement: “Cam (crooked) is thy name, Cam ar thyne eyes and wayes” (Maidment, Scottish Pasquils, Vol. II, p. 8).

955. The Marquis of Argyll was beheaded on Monday 27th May 1661 (Nicoll, op. cit., p. 334), and even before the event the poet regards his fate as sealed, and is not content with this but predicts his fate in the next world as well.

966. A grant of £45,000 was made to Argyll for maintenance and annual rents in lieu of damage to his property during the Montrose Wars (A.P.S., Vol. VI, 1, pp. 643, 675).


MURT NA CEAPAICH

Shortly after the Restoration Alexander MacDonald, Chief of Keppoch, and his brother Ranald were murdered—an event

[TD 269]

which caused considerable consternation throughout the country. So much contradictory evidence has been adduced that it is sometimes difficult to distinguish fact from fiction. The chief sources of information for the facts of the murder are the Records of the Privy Council, a contemporary account written in English by the Rev. James Fraser, Wardlaw, and the compositions of the Keppoch bard.

During the Civil War, the Chief of the MacDonalds of Keppoch was Dòmhnall Glas, who in 1644 joined Montrose at Blair Atholl (Balfour, Historical Works, Vol. III, p. 271; Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 180). A decree of forfeiture was passed against him on 11th February 1645 (A.P.S., Vol. VI, i, p. 322), but there is no further record of him, and it is impossible to ascertain the exact date of his death, although it must have occurred prior to the year 1651. In that year his brother Alasdair Buidhe is listed among the Highland chiefs called out shortly after the coronation of Charles II at Scone, and is designated “McRanald, tutor of Keppoch” (ibid., Vol. VI, 11, p. 625). From this it may be inferred that Dòmhnall Glas was dead, and that his son and heir was a minor, hence the appointment of a tutor on whom should devolve the affairs of the clan during the minority of the chief.

The first appearance in public records of Alexander MacDonald as Chief of the Clan occurs on 2nd August 1661, when he is directed by the Privy Council to find caution for his dependants (P.C.R., 1661-1664, Vol. I, pp. 55-56). For failing to appear before the Council he was put to the horn. Early the following year, on 30th January 1662, a complaint was lodged against him by Alexander MacDonald, tacksman of Inverlair, for having invaded his lands the previous month, accompanied by sixty of his dependants, and conducting himself in a most riotous and barbarous manner, as a result of which he himself was forced to seek refuge among strangers. He further stated that Keppoch boasted that he would eject him from his lands, and that before he would be satisfied “one of them tuo most dy” (ibid., pp. 150-151). According to tradition the cause of the quarrel was that during the Chief’s minority MacDonald accepted from the Marquis of Huntly a tack of the lands of Fersit and Inverlair independent of Keppoch. The young Chief strongly resented this, and refused to acknowledge any titular rights on their part to these lands (Clan Donald, Vol. II, p. 635; Lochaber in War and Peace, p. 246; MacDonells of Keppoch, p. 61).

The Chief of Keppoch did not compear to answer the charges,

[TD 270]

and as a result of further complaints of a similar nature it was enjoined that no caution of peace should be accepted from him until he appeared personally before the Privy Council (ibid., p. 220). This measure at last evoked some response from the young Chief. He asserted that the charges lodged against him were false, and had been made solely to inconvenience him, “to put them to a troublesome journey in this tempestuous season”. He requested that the sentence which had been passed upon him should be suspended, and he in turn now lodged a complaint against the tacksman of Inverlair. The decree of suspension which he sought is incomplete, and it is impossible to ascertain the nature of the charge which he lodged with the Council (ibid.). It is obvious, however, that relationships between these two septs of Clan Donald were somewhat strained, and this atmosphere of discord constituted a sombre prelude to the murder of the Chief and his brother shortly afterwards—an event in which the MacDonalds of Inverlair were deeply implicated.

The conception of the plot is ascribed by Fraser to Archibald (Allan?), son of Alasdair Buidhe, former Tutor of Keppoch. According to this source he conspired with Alexander Roy, Dugald’s son in Inverlair, and encouraged him to kill the two boys, his motive being that he himself should take possession of the lands of Keppoch, while Alexander Roy should have Inverlair. He then withdrew from the scene and left MacDonald of Inverlair together with six accomplices to perpetrate the murder (Wardlaw MS., p. 462). The aspiration of Alasdair Buidhe’s son might well have afforded a pretext for the murder, and this was an excellent opportunity for enlisting the help of the MacDonalds of Inverlair, who were on none too cordial terms with the Chief at this time. In another source it is stated that prominent in the conspiracy to murder the youths were two uncles on their father’s side, the younger of whom is represented as the arch-contriver of the plot. He found it convenient to be away from home on the day appointed, and left his brother to execute the deed along with about a dozen of his fellow-clansmen (Lang, Highlands of Scotland in 1750, pp. 97-98).

According to a Lochaber tradition the murder was committed to avenge atrocities committed by Keppoch upon the MacDonalds of Glen Roy—Sliochd Iain Duibh—who were descended from an illegitimate son of a former Keppoch chief. It is thus represented as a just act of retribution (Stuart, Lays of the Deer Forest, Vol. II, p. 402 ff.). If this tradition could be relied upon it would

[TD 271]

certainly provide an explanation of the murder, but it is inaccurate in some respects. The year of the murder is stated to be 1665, whereas it occurred on 25th September 1663 (Wardlaw MS., p. 462; P.C.R., Vol. II, p. 62). Furthermore the Chief of Keppoch is represented as an old man with three sons, which in fact was not the case.

The facts of the murder are clearly set forth by Iain Lom. He states that the boys were brutally put to death in their own home by two of the sons of Alasdair Buidhe, and seven of the MacDonalds of Inverlair (ll. 1438, 1440). Fraser, on the other hand, says that they were out on the field harvesting when MacDonald and his accomplices arrived (Wardlaw MS., p. 462), but as the poet was on the scene shortly after the event, and was also present at their funeral, his evidence is obviously to be preferred.

With regard to the motive which prompted the murder he is also very specific, and states explicitly that it was committed solely on account of the attempt made by the young Chief to curb the unruly habits of his assailants. Fraser states that “the young chief being piously inclined resolves to reform that rude country” (ibid.; cf. Highlands of Scotland, p. 97), while in another source we are told that he made himself very unpopular as a result of rules and regulations which he drew up for the guidance of his people (MacDonells of Keppoch, p. 60). It seems clear that an attempt was made to raise the general standard of conduct within the clan, and although it is not possible to ascertain the nature of the rules in question, it is probable that they were resented by the MacDonalds of Keppoch, who were a predatory clan by tradition and economic necessity. This would help to explain their general apathy after the event. The Chief and his brother were murdered in cold blood, yet there was no reaction whatsoever on their part to avenge their death. The callous attitude thus displayed appalled the poet, who was horrified not only by the murder, but by the indifference of his kinsmen. He set forth clearly his plea for revenge, but realising that no help was to be obtained within the clan, he addressed his muse to Chieftains outwith it, notably Glengarry and Sir James MacDonald of Sleat, whom he knew were in a position to exert their influence and power upon the perpetrators of the murder. His determined efforts to secure retribution eventually bore the desired results. On 29th July 1665 a commission of fire and sword was granted by the Privy Council to Sir James MacDonald of Sleat against the following:

[TD 272]

Allan MacDonald, son of the Tutor of Keppoch;
Donald MacDonald, brother to the said Allan;
Alexander MacDougall in Inverlair;
John Roy MacDougall, brother to the said Alexander;
Donald Orie McCuill there;
Dugall McCoull in Tallie;
Patrick Dunbar there.
(P.C.R., Vol. II, pp. 62-63)

There is a tradition that the murderers anticipated retribution from Glengarry, and maintained a constant vigilance, but as there was no sign of any activity in that direction they relaxed their vigilance, with the result that they were taken by surprise (Stuart, Lays of the Deer Forest, p. 413 ff.). Fraser’s account is somewhat different from the above, but similar to that given by the poet. He states that MacDonald of Inverlair sought refuge in a stronghold which he built near a linn not far from his home, and which he considered impregnable. After a prolonged siege he was eventually smoked out with six of his accomplices. Their heads were then cut off, and sent to Edinburgh to be set up on poles on the Gallows Hill of Leith (Wardlaw MS., pp. 462-463). It is said that MacDonald was assisted in defending the stronghold at Inverlair by his wife, who supplied him with ammunition by melting all the pewter vessels at her disposal. After these had failed and he was compelled to make his exit because of the smoke which filled the house, he continued to offer resistance with his broad-sword (Stuart, loc. cit.).

At a meeting of the Privy Council on 7th December 1665 it was ordained that the heads of the following be set up on the Gallows Hill between Edinburgh and Leith:

Alexander MacDougall in Inverlair;
John Roy MacDougall, brother to the said Alexander;
Donald Orie McCoull in Inverlair;
Dugall McCoull in Tallie;
Patrick Dunbar there.
(P.C.R., Vol. II, p. 110)

Although only five names appear in the Register of the Privy Council the decapitation of seven of the defenders of the stronghold of Inverlair is a fact that has been established beyond reasonable doubt. About the middle of last century Dr Smith of Fort William, who displayed a certain scepticism towards the various traditions current relating to the event, decided to

[TD 273]

excavate the mound where the decapitated bodies of the murderers were said to have been buried. To his surprise he came across seven skull-less skeletons, one of which he was able to identify as that of MacDonald of Inverlair, who, according to tradition, was lame on account of a fractured thigh-bone which had been badly set in his youth. It is said that Dr Smith was so impressed by his findings that he printed and circulated among his friends the results of his excavation (Lays of the Deer Forest, Vol. II, pp. 416-417; Campbell, Language, Poetry and Music of the Highland Clans, p. 66).

A comparison between the names of those whose heads reached Edinburgh and the list of names contained in the Privy Council’s Commission of fire and sword shows that Donald and Allan, the sons of Alasdair Buidhe, and Alexander Beg MacDougall were not among the number of the decapitated. Of the latter there is no further record, but Donald survived to give rise to the Clianaig branch of the MacDonalds of Keppoch (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 421). Allan his brother died an outlaw—possibly in Lewis as suggested by a tradition still current there—but the family settled in Badenoch and at a later date some of them emigrated to Nova Scotia (Highlands of Scotland, p. 99; Celtic Magazine, Vol. V, p. 277). Alasdair Buidhe, whatever suspicion may have attached to him, became Chief of the Clan in 1665 without any apparent opposition, and continued in that position until 1669 (P.C.R., Vol. II, p. 586).

There is a tradition that Iain Lom presented himself at Invergarry Castle bearing the heads of the murderers, which he had tied by the hair to a rope of heather. On his way there he is said to have washed the trophies of his mission in a spring near the south-west end of Loch Oich, since known as Tobar nan Ceann. (1) In 1812 a monument to Lord MacDonell was erected over this well by Colonel MacDonell, seventeenth Chief of Glengarry, with inscriptions by Ewen MacLachlan in Gaelic, English, Latin and French, to the effect that the Keppoch murderers were brought to justice by Lord MacDonell. (For the Gaelic inscription see SO, p. 368.) The murder is stated to have been committed in the first quarter of the sixteenth century, which is incorrect. It has also been alleged that Iain Lom along with several others made their way to Inverness, acting on instructions from Lord MacDonell, who wished to have their action endorsed by magisterial approval.

(1) See also “Severed Heads in Wells: An Aspect of the Well Cult”, by Dr Anne Ross, Scottish Studies, Vol. 6, Part I, p. 31 ff.

[TD 274]

On arriving at the Inn of Cluanmore in Glen Urquhart, the man who carried the creel with the heads threw it down carelessly, thereby causing the heads to rattle. The poet is said to have remarked, “Ud ud, nach còrd sibh, nach còrd sibh, ’s gur cloinn chàirdean sibh” (Celtic Monthly, Vol. XII, p. 223; Celtic Magazine, Vol. V, pp. 101-102; GSI, Vol. XXXII, p. 95; MacDonald Bards, p. 9; MacDonald, Story and Song from Loch Ness-side, p. 282).

Despite the fact that Glengarry’s name appears as one of the Commissioners appointed by the Privy Council in 1665 to bring the murderers to justice (P.C.R., Vol. II, pp. 63-64), there is no record of his having taken any part whatsoever in the execution of the commission. That Iain Lom entertained high hopes in this direction there can be no question, judging from the tenor of several of his poems, but it would appear that these were belied.

It is difficult to say why the poet acted differently from the rest of the Clan. He himself was the great-grandson of a former Keppoch Chief who had been deposed, and this factor, together with his keen sense of justice, may have influenced his actions considerably. It is possible also that he himself may have approved of the policy adopted by the Chief, and this would help to explain the hostility of his kinsmen, who appear to have resented it.

Several versions of the poem are extant, the oldest of which are found in McL MS. 73 (NL), housed in the National Library, Edinburgh, and in the manuscript of Jerome Stone. These sources are closely related. With the exception of vv. 5-9, inclusive, in the former, not found elsewhere, the sequence of lines and stanzas is the same in both sources, and differences in readings are mainly orthographical. McL MSS. 129, 154, T and I supply shorter versions of the poem with substantial differences in readings. A further version, based partly on Turner, and partly on the version in the Dornie MSS., has been edited in GSI, Vol. XII, p. 122. (See Appendix F, p. 395.) SO and AM derive from T, as does the version in Am Filidh Gaidhealach, p. 10, while Maclean Sinclair follows GSI. The foregoing version is based on that of the two oldest sources, but the text is corrupt in a number of instances, and expected assonances are frequently lacking. Several stanzas may well be later additions by other inferior hands.

985. Doubtful line. T provides an easier reading but differs substantially from the earlier sources. Maclean Sinclair reads

[TD 275]

flod, floating. Plot, one reading found in the McL MSS, occurs elsewhere in the text (l. 1637) in the sense of parboil or scald. But there is also plod, m., carnage, and a possible emendation as suggested by the Rev. William Matheson is: Cha bu nì chaidh air plot. It was not cattle that were consigned to carnage.

994-995. Cf. ll. 1492-1493. In an entry in the Privy Council Register (Vol. II, p. 63) it is stated that the Chief suffered thirty-two wounds, and his brother twenty-eight.

1003. The text is defective in the earliest sources. The version in the Dornie MSS., the only other primary source for this stanza, provides a more intelligible reading, and this has been adopted.

1005. In view of the metaphor in l. 1002, tì is probably a pun referring to the backgammon board. (Cf. l. 171.)

1026. Sìol Dùghaill: the MacDonalds of Inverlair, so called after their ancestor Dùghall Ruadh of Sunart, who settled in Brae Lochaber about the middle of the sixteenth century (Celtic Magazine, Vol. II, p. 63).

1028-1029. lit., the set of dirks left your skins like the shadow of a sieve (with light showing through holes).

1034. Turcach: used in the sense of infidel. The text of this stanza is corrupt in NL and JS.

1045. A fact which galled the poet considerably was that the murderers moved about the country for a time quite unperturbed—their marauding expeditions taking them as far afield as Drum on Deeside (P.C.R., Vol. II, p. 331).

1054. éighre: metri causa. Cf.:

<gai>Beir gu ar n-éibhneas leat Rìgh Seumas
’S an t-éighre-s’ a Bhreatainn.<eng>
(Fernaig MS., p. 223)

1063. The reading in the MS. sources is of course incorrect as a statement of fact.

1068. dìogh’ltas: pronounced diaoghl’tas, to assonate with naomhtha. The text in the original is at fault as there is no aicill.

1075. lit., that has settled badly for us.

1117. According to James Fraser the youths had been fostered by Sir James MacDonald of Sleat until they were “fitt

[TD 276]

for letters” (Wardlaw MS., pp. 461-462). Hence, no doubt, the poet’s added confidence that he would avenge their death.

1134-1135. In 1660 Glengarry was raised to the peerage with the title of Lord MacDonell and Aros (Scots Peerage, Vol. V, p. 564). This rank entitled him to a seat in Parliament, and his prolonged periods of absence from home are frequently referred to by Iain Lom.

1138. itean: the feathers in the chief’s bonnet—a symbol of his traditional authority and responsibility.

1155. gun ghrab: lit. unhindered.


ORAN DO AONGHAS OG MORAIR GHLINNE GARAIDH

In this song of eulogy to Lord MacDonell, Iain Lom is primarily concerned with the controversy which arose over Glengarry’s claim to the chiefship of the entire Clan Donald. The discord thus aroused within the ranks of the Clan is distasteful to him by its very nature, and he urges his kinsmen to lay aside their differences, not only on account of natural ties, but also in order to ensure their own safety, and to enable them by their united forces to withstand outside attack. It is possible that the poem may have been composed with a view to enlisting Glengarry’s support after the Keppoch murder. Although there is no direct evidence to this effect, the last two stanzas would seem to point to an underlying motive which was of even greater consequence to the poet than that which prompted the main theme of his song. For a discussion of the Glengarry claim, which was revived a hundred and fifty years later by Alexander MacDonell of Glengarry, see Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 168 ff.

The text followed is that of E with the exception of the fourteenth stanza, which is omitted in this source and derives from T. In the latter the twelfth stanza is not included, the sequence of stanzas differs from that of the earlier version, and substantial differences occur in the text. The version in I is similar to T. Although some of the original readings in McN differed from E these have been deleted with a few minor exceptions, and the versions are similar. Maclean Sinclair’s text is based on E, but also includes the additional stanza in T referred to above.

1214-1215. Turner’s reading is perhaps more satisfactory here:

[TD 277]

<gai>Gun robh maoim air do naimhdean
Anns gach camp dhiu ri shireadh.<eng>

In truth your enemies suffered defeat in every encampment where they were sought out.

1222. The threat became a reality in 1668 when Argyll received a charter of the lands of Knoydart (A.P.S., Vol. VII, pp. 581, 582).

1226. Cf. metaphor in l. 1236.

1230-1231. Turner reads:

<gai>Tha nas mò orm mar iongnadh
An dràsd anns an leum so.<eng>

The word leum in this context probably means either a sudden flash of anger or assault. If the latter is the correct rendering the reference may well be to the Keppoch murder.

1243. I.e. Lord MacDonell and Aros.

1252-1253. Cf. ll. 2538-2539.

1295. Cf. Macintyre, Songs, l. 711 n.


IORRAM DO BHÀTA MHIC DHÒMHNAILL

This poem was composed after a brief visit which the poet paid to Sir James MacDonald of Sleat in the hope that he would take measures to secure retribution for the murder of the young Chief of Keppoch and his brother. On leaving Duntulm the poet composed the above song to Sir James’s galley in the course of which he takes the opportunity of bestowing a liberal measure of praise upon the Chief and his family. The first two stanzas suggest that the poet regarded his stay at Duntulm as being too brief, and was not altogether satisfied when he departed on Easter Sunday—probably 1664.

The three primary sources, which differ substantially in many readings, are M, McL MS. 167, S. Three stanzas bearing the title “Long” are found in McL MS. 222, the first of which is a variant of v. 7. (For the other two see “Iorram do Shìol Dùghaill”, vv. 27, 25.) SO is based on S, the only significant difference in readings occurring in v. 21. AM derives directly from S, while BGh follows SO. Maclean Sinclair’s version derives

[TD 278]

from M, but with normalised spelling. Fourteen stanzas with an English translation are included in C, pp. 161-163. The foregoing text derives mainly from S, which provides the most complete version of the poem.

1324. I.e. to visit Uist.

1333. I.e. when the wind blew in uncertain gusts.

1334. luchd-taighe: “There was a competent number of young gentlemen called lucht-taeh or guard de corps who always attended the chief at home and abroad. They were trained in managing the sword and target, in wrestling, swimming, dancing, shooting with bows and arrows, and were stout seamen” (Martin, Description of the Western Isles, p. 167).

1338. Rubha na Caillich: a cape in S.E. Skye—A’ Chailleach being a sea rock between Kyleakin and Kylerea.

1347. In view of the fact that all sources read riabhach (grey, brindled), it is probable that “Dubh-Chnòideartach” (“The Black Galley of Knoydart”) was a traditional title in which dubh was not felt to be a description of the boat. Cf. “An Dubh-Ghleannach”, MacDonald of Glenaladale’s galley (SO, p. 346), and “An Làir Dhonn” (BGh, p. 217), which was not necessarily brown.

1353. Dòmhnall an Dùin: i.e. of Duntulm—Donald, eldest son and successor of Sir James.

1355. stoighle: style, i.e. title.

1359. Probably James, a younger brother of Dòmhnall an Dùin, who got sasine of the lands of Aird Sleat in 1682 (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 472).

1361. Dùn Sgàthaich is in Sleat. According to tradition Cú Chulainn crossed the sea to Alba, there to acquire an education in warfare and arms in the fort of Sgathach (“The Wooing of Emer”, trans. by Kuno Meyer, Archaeological Review, Vol. I, p. 70).

1368. Ruaidh: the river of Glen Roy in Brae Lochaber. Professor Watson states that the word probably stands for Ruaidhe and may be gen. sg. fem., “Glen of the Red”, with “abhann” understood (Watson, Celtic Place Names of Scotland, pp. 437-438).

[TD 279]

1370. Carn na Làirge: i.e. Làirig Thurraid (in MacVurich Láirc Thurraid), the Pass at the head of Glen Turret. The word Làirig, a pass, is common in certain districts, particularly in North and West Perthshire, but is also represented elsewhere, e.g. Làirig Bhaile Dhubhthaich, the Lairgs of Tain, a pass between the parish of Tain and Edderton in Ross-shire; Làirig an Tùir, “the pass of the tower”, south of Strathnairn in Inverness-shire (Watson, ibid., pp. 483-485).

1372. The MacDonalds of Glencoe, known in Gaelic as Clann Iain Abraich, were descended from Iain Og an Fhraoich, a natural son of Angus Og of Islay (Rel. Celt., II, p. 158). According to a MS. History of the MacDonalds, written by the Sleat historian, Hugh MacDonald, c. 1680, his mother was a daughter of Dougall MacHenry, a Glencoe man, and the progenitor of this sept of Clan Donald afterwards settled in this district as a vassal under his brother, the Lord of the Isles. Very little is known of the early history of the family. Gregory states that one of their number was probably fostered in Lochaber and acquired the surname Abrach, hence the designation Clann Iain Abraich (Gregory, p. 67; Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 212; Highland Papers, Vol. I, p. 23; MacKenzie, History of the MacDonalds, p. 524).

1379. The principal expedient employed by the chief for summoning his men in time of emergency was the crois-tàra or fiery cross. It consisted of a piece of wood burnt or burning at one end, with a piece of linen or white cloth stained with blood hanging from the other. The cross was sent round from hand to hand among the clansmen, and as its significance was known to all, the chief was able to muster his men within a very short period of time (Stewart, Sketches of the Highlanders, p. 308).


CUMHA DO MHAC MHIC RAGHNAILL NA CEAPAICH AGUS A BHRÀTHAIR

The poet, having incurred the wrath of the murderers as a result of his assiduous efforts to have them punished, found it expedient to leave Brae Lochaber. He composed this lament on the borders of Glenmoriston on his way to Kintail, where the Earl of Seaforth extended hospitality to him, and assigned a farm to him in Gleneilchaig. He is not perturbed by the fact that he has to remove himself and his belongings to Kintail. Wounded he has been, not by any physical hurt, but by one which has

[TD 280]

touched him even more closely. The poem opens on a quiet note of subdued grief for the murdered youths, and proceeds in typical panegyric style to give an account of the Chief’s pursuits and his genealogy. There follows an attack upon the murderers in forceful language, and in the final stanza the poet gives vent to the full force of his fury.

The first nineteen stanzas and also v. 21 are based directly on T, from which derives also the version in GlB. The twentieth stanza and the last fourteen were supplied to Maclean Sinclair by Alexander MacDonald, Ridge, and are included by him in GB and O. They are not known in any other source. This version has been reproduced in Loyal Lochaber, p. 435. In the foregoing text the sequence of the first seven stanzas is the same as in T, and the order of the remainder in this source is as follows: vv. 11, 12, 13, 17, 18, 19, 21, 14, 15, 16, 8, 9, 10.

1388. According to the late Mr Colin Chisholm, Inverness, the poet was obliged to leave Kintail on account of a scurrilous satire upon the men of Kintail of which he was the alleged author. He further states that it was on the occasion of his departure from Kintail that he composed this elegy, but this is incorrect, as it was obviously composed on his way there. To support his statement he quotes the following lines:

<gai>’Ga mo chur a Cinn-tàile
Gun fhios dé ’n t-àite don téid mi.<eng>
(GSI, XII, 121)

The first line is a misquotation of Turner’s reading.

1391. Iain Lom would have already witnessed the death of four Keppoch Chiefs:
(1) Alasdair nan Cleas, who died in 1635 (Clan Donald, Vol II, p. 628).
(2) Raghnall Og, his eldest son. (Cf. pp. 2, 6 supra.)
(3) Dòmhnall Glas, who died ante 1651.
(4) Alexander MacDonald, his elder son, the subject of this elegy.

1394. Cf. Mairearad Nighean Lachlainn’s lament for Sir John Maclean of Duart:

<gai>Có an neach ’ga bheil sùilean
Nach gabhadh d’a ionnsaigh
Mar tha a’ choille air a rùsgadh?<eng>
(BGh, ll. 3698-3700)

[TD 281]

1398. tuaileas: originally slanderous tale, then tale with the element of slander less pronounced. Its usage here is not very different from that of sgeul in “Chan eil sgeul air”.

1433. cothrom na Féinne: an expression generally used to mean fair-play, which derives from the fact that the Fianna were alleged never to have attacked a force of men numerically inferior to their own—their idea of fair-play being:

<gai>Gaisgeach air ghaisgeach, is laoch ri laoch.<eng>
(Nicolson, Gaelic Proverbs, p. 155)

1434-1435. A similar statement though less direct is made by the sister of the murdered youths, who composed an Elegy on this occasion known in Lochaber as “Cumha Ni Mhic Raghnaill” (ll. 11-12 infra).

<gai>
Dh’éirich mise moch Di-dòmhnaich
Hì rìthill ùthaill ó
’S shuidh mi air an tulaich bhòidhich.
Fàth mo lionn-duibh o hao ó.

’S daingeann bhuail iad as gach taobh sibh,
Bhràithrean nan gaol, ochóin.

Shuidh mi air an tulaich bhòidhich
’S leig mi air an tuireadh bhrònach.
Chunna mi taigh m’athar gun chòmhla,
Gun smùid gun deathaich gun cheò dheth.
Dh’fhosgail mi dorus ur seòmair,
Thàinig ur fuil thar mo bhrògan,
’S teann nach d’òl mi fhìn mo leòr dhith.

’S i fuil Alasdair a leòn mi
Is fuil Raghnaill duinn a b’òige.
Dìol na muice duibhe dòighte
Air gach aon a bha mu’n fheòlach.
Thoir fios uam-sa gu Mac Dhòmhnaill,
Gu Mac Mhic Alasdair Chnòideart,
’S gu Mac Mhic Ailein o’n mhórchuan.
Tha mi ’n earbsa ’n Rìgh na glòire
Gun toir sibh dhachaigh an tòrachd—
’S càirdean duibh fhéin, bràithrean dhòmh-s’ iad.<eng>
(D, pp. 22-25).

[TD 282]

I rose early on Sunday
Hi rithill uthaill o
And I sat upon the lovely knoll.
Cause of my grief o hao o.

Fiercely did they strike you from all sides,
Beloved brothers, ochoin.

I sat upon the lovely knoll
And let the sorrowful lament have its course.
I saw my father’s house without a door,
Without smoke, reek or fume.
I opened the door of your chamber—
Your blood came over my shoes.
I scarcely refrained from drinking my fill of it.
It is the blood of Alexander that grieved me
And the blood of brown-haired Ranald the younger.
The fate of the black singed pig
Be upon all who were concerned in the butchery.
Convey a message from me to MacDonald (i.e. of Sleat),
To MacDonald of Glengarry from Knoydart,
And to MacDonald of Clanranald from the open sea.
In the King of Glory I trust
That you will avenge them—
They were your relatives and my brothers.

1436. Maclean Sinclair’s reading Di-domhnaich is incorrect historically and metrically. The murder was committed on a Friday. Absence of aicill shows that the poet did not say Di-domhnaich; aoine (pronounced éine) might be regarded as a sufficient assonance with mèir-.


IORRAM DO SHÌOL DÙGHAILL

The poet describes his expulsion from Lochaber, the dispersal of his possessions on the hill-side while he himself is hunted as a hare is by dogs, and this because he stands aloof from the murderers. He expresses regret that Sir James MacDonald of Sleat has not yet taken any steps to avenge the dastardly crime, but anticipates with joyful eagerness the approach of his fleet with this end in view. Glengarry’s absence from home is also a source of deep disappointment, and the poem ends with a brief commentary on the corrupt state of Parliament and its disloyalty to the King.

[TD 283]

The text followed is that of NL, which bears the following heading: “an toran sho, rin ian lom e mandfaig e Bre Lochaber dhionsigh Mhicynich chintail ga thermŭn ŭai no droch dhaoin Mhŭrt mac oighr, s, tainistar no Ceppich, mar Gheibh ŭ sios romhad”. A similar version is found in JS with the same sequence of lines and stanzas. Differences in readings are slight. An introductory note reads: “John Lom the Author of the above poem” (i.e. “Murt na Ceapaich”) “having incurr’d the hatred of the murderers by his verses was constrained to fly the country for fear of them and in his exile composed the following song still more to enflame the country against them.” T provides a shorter version of the poem, entitled “Iorram le Iain Lom do shiol Dughaill air son iad bhi ga chuir air imrich, chionn e bhi labhairt na’n aghaidh air son murt na Capaich”. A parallel version is found in I entitled: “Oran a rinn Iain Lom nuair a bha e fagail Lochaber”. It consists of nineteen stanzas, the last two of which are unnumbered and have different end rhyme. These are not known elsewhere. Differences in readings are mainly orthographical, but I omits vv. 1, 7, 16 as found in T.

1459. Crachaig: the farm in Gleneilchaig which was given over to the poet by the Earl of Seaforth. Mr Colin Chisholm states that it was pointed out to him (c. 1830) as Iain Lom’s old farm by an elderly man who said that it was on Mam-an-tuirc in Gleneilchaig that this poem was composed when its author was obliged to leave Kintail (GSI, Vol. XII, p. 121). Internal evidence suggests that it was composed when he left Lochaber.

T reads: Mi ga m’ fhogradh a Clachaig—I am being driven out of Clachaig. Clachaig is situated on the right bank of the River Spean about seven miles east from Keppoch. It appears to have paid no rent, as the holding appertaining to the bards of the ancient possessors (Fraser-Mackintosh, Antiquarian Notes, 2nd Series, p. 166).

1494. lobhta: generally a loft, but also applied to a wooden floor on ground level.

1498. lùb: may be used metaphorically in the sense of guile, deceit; lùb cham: an unjust trick.

1495-1500. According to this law a thief was brought before a jury of women for stealing a horse, and was allowed to go scot-free while the horse was condemned to be hanged for allowing himself to be carried away. The man had been tried on a previous

[TD 284]

occasion for stealing the same horse, and had been acquitted, but according to the story he proved a better master than the actual owner, so much so that the horse returned to the thief. The incident is perpetuated in a well-known Gaelic proverb: Cha tugadh an Cille-mo-Cheallaig breith bu chlaoine: “No worse judgement was given in Kilmacheallag” (Nicolson, Gaelic Proverbs, p. 134; cf. Turner, p. 47; Sar Obair, p. 38). The tale is told in full in An Gàidheal, Vol. I, p. 146.

1505. I.e. in this present context, as the Sìol Dùghaill also fouled their own nest.

1506. I.e. fouled the well. Maclean Sinclair has the following note on the harper’s wife: “A réir na naidheachd a thugadh dhòmh-sa fhuair an Cruitear a bhean aige ann an suidheachadh maille ri duine eile anns nach bu chòir di bhith. An àite nàire a ghabhail ’s ann a thòisich i air a chàineadh ’s air tilgeadh chlach air” (O, p. 117).

1507-1512. It is doubtful whether these two stanzas formed part of the original version, as the vowel in the final stressed syllable of the strophe is not the one required for assonance.

1519. This stanza derives from T, as the version supplied in the MSS. is unmetrical and appears to be incomplete.

1522-1524. Cf. ll. 1320-1323.

1537-1543. The two final stanzas, found only in the two earliest sources, are corrupt.


ORAN DO CHOINNEACH OG IARLA SHIOFORD

This song, which is obviously in a fragmentary state, is addressed to Kenneth MacKenzie, who succeeded his father, Coinneach Mór, as fourth Earl of Seaforth in 1678 (MacKenzie, History of the MacKenzies, p. 281). It may have been composed during the poet’s residence in Kintail, although it is by no means a complimentary tribute paid by a guest to his host. It is possibly a parody of a eulogistic song, probably in the form of a Tàladh, composed to Coinneach Og, a version of which may be that preserved in the Dornie MSS. and published in GSI, Vol. XLI, p. 319.

The text is that of McL MS. 126, slightly emended, where it is entitled “Am Bo Neidh”, probably a copyist’s error for Am Bo

[TD 285]

Hoidh, part of the vocables in the chorus. An edited version of the poem is found in MC, which bears the title given above.

1551. dear-lomlan: an unusual compound—the more common forms being lomlan, and dearlan (dearrlan).

1562. Seòras: emended from the original (Seumas) metri causa. The former is also a common name among the MacKenzies.

1565. McL reads Deint ann (dèant’?) for dòrngheal (MC), but the latter as well as being necessary for rhyme is also a standard epithet.

Cf.:
<gai>Gur a h-iomadh laoch dòrngheal
Chaidh an òrdugh mu d’ bhrataich.<eng>
(Maclean Bards, Vol. I, p. 43)


ORAN DO MHORAIR CHLANN DÒMHNAILL

Iain Lom was in residence in Kintail when he composed this song, which is addressed to Lord MacDonell. He no doubt still cherished the hope that the latter would exert his power to bring the murderers to justice, and his disapproval of his lengthy sojourn in England is quite obvious.

The source is Ewen MacLachlan’s MS., from which derives also the version in MC. For a longer version of the poem, found in I, see Appendix G, p. 405. For another poem addressed to Glengarry, found in I only, see Appendix H, p. 407.

1571. The triubhas or trews consisted of breeches and stockings in one piece fitting closely to the skin. The following observation is made by Martin Martin towards the end of the seventeenth century: “Many of the people wear trews. Some have them very fine woven like stockings of those made of cloth. Some are coloured, and others striped. The latter are as well shaped as the former, lying close to the body from the middle downwards and tied round with a belt above the haunches. There is a square piece of cloth which hangs down before. The measure for shaping the trews is a stick of wood, whose length is a cubit, and that divided into the length of a finger and half a finger, so that it requires more skill to make it than the ordinary habit” (A Description of the Western Islands of Scotland, p. 246).

1582. In the first half of the sixteenth century the Chief of Glengarry received a crown charter of half the lands of Lochcarron, Lochbroom and Lochalsh, with the Castle of Strome, but

[TD 286]

in 1570 these were resigned to Kenneth MacKenzie of Kintail by Alasdair Dearg, the father of the subject of this poem (Scots Peerage, Vol. V, pp. 559-562; Clan Donald, Vol. II, p. 380 ff.). Despite the fact that Iain Lom is enjoying Seaforth’s hospitality in Kintail, he quite openly expresses his wish that these lands be restored to Glengarry.


AN CIARAN MABACH

It would appear from the first line of the poem that Iain Lom was still in Kintail when he composed this song to Archibald MacDonald, who with skill and efficiency had executed the commission of fire and sword against the murderers which had been delegated to him by his brother Sir James MacDonald of Sleat. According to the poet, who it is said acted as their guide (C, p. 167), he set off from Uist on Wednesday accompanied by a party of men, travelled on foot over the mountains which were then covered with snow, and slew the murderers at Inverlair the following Sunday.

The text followed is that of McL MS. 154, where the poem is entitled: “An Ciaran Mabach, Do Ghilleaspuig Ruadh Mac Mhic Dhonuill”. The version in G appears to be derived from this source, although there is one different reading in the text, and the alternative readings in McL are not supplied. A different version is found in NL and JS, with the same sequence of lines and stanzas. Both sources omit the chorus and vv. 6, 8, and there are no substantial differences in readings. No title is given but in each version there is a brief introductory note: “Gheibh ŭ, n, lorg sho mar dŭbhuirt ughdar chŭmhidh, agŭs no mŭrtarrin ar fhogra e dhŭich, ar son no bha e grait ŭmp, n, sheol dŭsg chŭir air a Chinnidh dhonillich ar fad, gŭ dheanibh, mar rin̅n̅ Sir Shemŭs, s, chŭir shin gean̅n̅ ar Ian lom, a ghra mar chi ŭ romhad arda hseol̅l̅, -gŭ dŭthrachdich, tabhairt taing do dhia mar hachir do na mŭrtarin” (NL).

“The Poet having by his Verses spirited up the Friends of the deceac’d Children to take a Solid Revenge on their Murderers by putting them to Death returns Thanks in the following Manner” (JS).

SO and O derive from G, but Maclean Sinclair has altered a number of the original readings and included an additional stanza not known in any other source. Four stanzas of the poem are quoted and translated by Campbell, and two are wrongly

[TD 287]

inserted in the version of “Là Inbhir Lòchaidh” found in McL MS. 220 and McN¹. (See Appendix C, p. 387, vv. 6, 7.)

1598-1600. Some of the vocables may or may not carry any meaning, e.g. có, sin (seinn, SO), calman comhdhail (codheal, McL, G, = co-gheal?).

1610-1611. In former days it was a regular practice to re-thatch houses once a year and in order to complete the task within the same day it was customary to begin very early.

aitreabh: i.e. the stronghold at Inverlair.

1616. lainn: line hypermetric with MS. reading lannaibh.

1617. gad: a withe or switch; a number of fish as carried home on a string or withe (Dwelly’s Dictionary). The significance of the line is that the heads were strung on a withe passed through the mouth and that there was a full catch.

1620. Gill-easbuig Ruadh: i.e. An Ciaran Mabach, known also as Gilleasbuig Dubh. (Cf. BGh, p. 179.) Maclean Sinclair maintains that Ruadh is an error (GlB, pp. 160, 391; O, p. 117). It is certainly difficult to reconcile the two epithets, as ruadh when applied to a person generally means red-haired, but whatever the explanation there can be no doubt that the reference in these lines is to Archibald MacDonald, the poet’s coadjutor in punishing the murderers.

1630. Hypermetric in MS. (bu chruaidh).

1633-1634. The women were brought out and used as a decoy to draw out the culprits from their stronghold.

NL reads: nŭair fŭaridh mar le maol snishan. There is a note at the end of the poem which states that Alexander MacDonald, who is here referred to as Alasdair Ruadh mac Dhughaill mhic Ruairidh, was captured after being in company with a woman. Maol snishan is explained as an implement used by hunters to trap the roe and roebuck as well. In like manner was Alasdair Ruadh captured in the end along with those who were with him.


ORAN DO MHORAIR GHLINNE GARAIDH

It is difficult to say what the exact circumstances were which gave rise to the composition of this poem or to whom it was addressed, but it is quite possible, as suggested by Maclean Sinclair (GlB, p. 142), that the occasion may have been Glen-

[TD 288]

garry’s expedition to Mull in 1675 to assist the Macleans against Argyll. Iain Lom may well have accompanied him and composed this poem on the journey to Aros. (Cf. p. 295 infra.)

There are four versions of the poem:
(1) Ewen MacLachlan’s MS.: “Do Mhorfhear Ghlinne Garadh” (Scottish Celtic Review, p. 77; GlB, p. 142).
(2) Dr Maclean’s MS.: no title given.
(3) Turner: “Iorram le Eachunn Bachdach”.
(4) Alexander MacDonald, Ridge: “Oran do dh’Aonghas mac Raghnaill Oig” (GlB, p. 145; O, p. 99).

The text followed is that of EM, with the exception of the third stanza and the last three, which are omitted in this source, but found in M and T. The two latter, which are closely related, omit vv. 4, 10, 11, 13, 15, and with the exception of three additional stanzas in T the same sequence of lines and stanzas is observed in both sources. Certain stanzas in the poem have a close affinity to stanzas which are found in the Elegy composed to Sir James MacDonald, and it is obvious that there has been a confusion of two or more poems even in the earliest sources.

1649. Cf.:
<gai>Tha mise fo ghruaim
’S gun mi ’n caidreabh a’ chuain;
Cha chaidil mi uair air chòir.<eng>
(BGh, p. 217)

1651. Dhòmhnaill: emended reading. If the poem is addressed to Lord MacDonell nighean Sheumais as found in EM is incorrect, as he was married to a daughter of Sir Donald MacDonald of Sleat, to whom Iain Lom addressed his muse prior to his death in 1643. (See p. 14 supra.) It is quite possible, however, as Maclean Sinclair points out (O, p. 120), that the subject may have been MacDonald of Sleat, either Sir James or his son, in which case the reading in M and T would be appropriate, i.e. oighre Dhùn-tuilm.

1658. The reference is to the Chief’s wife, who is said to surpass everyone in wisdom except her husband. According to M and T the person referred to is oighre Dhùn-tuilm, i.e. Donald, eldest son and successor of Sir James MacDonald.

1682. Possibly Alexander Robertson, 12th Chief of the Clan, who was a minor during the Montrose wars. His uncle Donald Robertson, the Tutor of Struan, served with distinction at Inverlochy and Kilsyth.

[TD 289]

MARBHRANN DO SHIR SEUMAS MAC DHÒMHNAILL

In this elegy, which was composed to Sir James MacDonald of Sleat, who died on 8th December 1678, we have an interesting glimpse of the social structure which prevailed in the Highlands in the seventeenth century, when the chief was still regarded as the leader of his men in time of war, and his hall as a centre of culture and social intercourse in time of peace, where music was played, and wine lavishly dispensed by a generous host.

The poem derives directly from Turner, as does the version in SO, but the sequence of stanzas, with the exception of the first three, has been altered in the foregoing text. Maclean Sinclair’s version is also based on T, but certain stanzas are omitted by him and incorporated in “Oran do dh’Aonghas mac Raghnaill Oig” (GlB, p. 145; O, p. 99). In the GlB version vv. 11, 13, 16, 17 are excluded from the text, while in O the poem consists of the first five stanzas and vv. 8, 9, 10, 11, 12, 14, 18, 20. This version is reproduced in Celtic Monthly, Vol. IX, p. 200. The following stanzas are included by him in the above-mentioned song: vv. 5, 6, 9, 10, 11, 17, and the last of these is also incorporated in the text of “Cumha do Ghill-easbuig na Ceapaich”.

1718. John of Moidart was present at Inverlochy, Auldearn and Kilsyth. He died in 1670 in the Island of Eriskay, South Uist, and was buried at Howmore (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 201).

John MacLeod, Chief of the MacLeods of Dunvegan, refused to join Montrose, no doubt because Argyll was superior of most of his estates. The MacLeods took a prominent part in the battle of Worcester in 1651, when Roderick MacLeod of Talisker, uncle and tutor of the young Chief, raised a regiment of seven hundred men, mostly MacLeods, whom he placed under the command of his brother, Norman MacLeod of Bernera.

1719. The MacFarlanes, whose principal seat was Arrochar at the head of Loch Long, were staunch Royalists (Skene, Highlanders of Scotland, pp. 270-277).

1721. The Macnabs, whose lands lay on the shores of Loch Tay, and in Strathfillan and Glendochart, joined Montrose after his winter campaign of devastation in Argyll in 1644 (Rel. Celt., Vol. II, p. 182). Their Chief, Iain Mín, joined the standard of King Charles II in 1650, with three hundred men, and was killed at the battle of Worcester (Macnab, The Clan Macnab, p. 15 ff.).

[TD 290]

1724. The MacGregors of Roro were descended from Gregor, third son of John MacGregor of Glenorchy, who died in 1390. When Montrose raised his Highland army, the greater part of the Clan Gregor joined him under the command of Patrick MacGregor of Glenstrae (Skene, op. cit., p. 338). According to Lochiel’s biographer, a party of MacGregors came in three days after the battle of Killiecrankie, commanded by MacGregor of Roro (Memoirs of Locheill, p. 283).

1728. Glengarry? The Chief of the entire Clan Donald was referred to as Mac Dhòmhnaill.

1730. If the reference is to Glengarry, rìoghbhrat sròil may apply to the robes which he wore as a peer.

1733. John Murray, 2nd Earl and 1st Marquis of Atholl.

1734-1735. Cf. ll. 1680-1682.

1741. Possibly Alasdair Buidhe, who is said to have been drowned in a pool on the River Spean (MacKenzie, History of the MacDonalds, p. 489). The same source states that he was dead before 1665, but this is incorrect, as he was summoned before the Privy Council on 15th January 1669 for using violence against a party of foot who were assisting in levying excise and public dues in Inverness-shire (P.C.R., Vol. II, p. 586). According to the Clan Donald historians he died c. 1670 (Clan Donald, Vol. II, p. 639).

The poem which follows, addressed to Seumas Og, is found in McN¹, MS. C, pp. 48-50, and bears a close similarity to the above elegy.

<gai>
Iorraim

Tha Liunn dubh air mo chragh
Chaidh mo shugradh mu Làr
Ce mar dh’fhaodas mi tamh ’s mi beo.

’S tha do Bhaille gun ghleus
’T Eich gun mhùnadh le Srein
Gh’fhalbh gach cleachdain le Seumas òg.

Nur a thigeadh tu mach
’S ard a chluinnte do neart
Bhidh Iain Muideartach leat ’s McLeoid.

[TD 291]

’S leat McIain McNeil
McAlasdair Eil
Bhidh McInnein leat fein o’n chro’.

Fir a Bhraghad seo shuas
Connlain ghasda gun ghruaim
Clainn a Ghriogair o chluainn nan cnò.

Thig na h Abbraich a nall
O Bharrabh nan Gleann
Chuireadh Fiughai gu teann an Feoil.

Thig fir Aghail a nios
Connlainn ghasda gun sgios
Ceannart orra ’s e finealt og.

Bha McPharlainn na Sgia
Aig fear h Aite se riamh
’S Mc An Abba le chiad na dho.

’S ann a d’ chodal-tigh fein
Chluinnte farrum nan teid
Mnathan grinne cuir ghreis le meoir.

Coilinn aca do n cheir
’S iad air lasadh gu geur.
Urlar farsuing o’n eisd ’n ceol.

Gill’ a fritheal mu seach
Teireirt gach Dith b’fhearr blas
Bhidh Fion frangach dearg ac ’s Beoir.

Uisg beatha no’m Pios
Reachadh Airgid ga dhiol
Dol sa ghlainne mar ghris an Rois.

Noise scureadh mi d’ chainnt
O’n nach d’theid aig mo cheann
Air bhi leanailt do Rann no’s mo.

(Do Sheumas og ma tha Comhnuil Le oide)
<eng>

[TD 292]

ORAN DO MHAC GILLE EATHAIN DHUBHAIRD

This song is addressed to Sir John Maclean who succeeded his father Sir Allan Maclean in 1674. Both Sir John and his father were minors when they fell heir to the estates of Duart, and the policy of aggrandisement which the late Marquis of Argyll had commenced against the Chief of Maclean was prosecuted with equal vigour by his son Archibald Campbell. Iain Lom reproaches the Clan for not offering a more stubborn resistance to the encroachments made by Argyll into Maclean territory, and expresses regret for the passing of former chieftains who would have firmly resisted the wiles and machinations of Mac Cailein.

The oldest version of the poem is found in John Maclean’s MS. From this source derives the version in An Gàidheal, Vol. V, which was supplied by Maclean Sinclair. It is also included in GlB and O.

1755. pàisde: the normal meaning, child, does not suit the context, as the first line of the stanza is clearly predicated of the third. The reference in this stanza is to the death of Hector Roy Maclean at Inverkeithing—a fatal wound from a musket shot having penetrated his breast (History of the Clan Maclean, p. 181).

1767. Gregory also states that the MacDonalds were deprived of Islay and Kintyre by false means. According to him they were stirred to rebellion in the first quarter of the seventeenth century through the wiles of Argyll, whose policy was dictated by a desire to prevent them coming to terms with the Government, in the hope that he himself might profit thereby and gain possession of their estates. Many of the Highland proprietors were somewhat lax about registering their estates, and others looked with contempt upon the formality of holding their lands by virtue of a scrap of parchment. In 1605 Lord Scone, Comptroller of Scotland, was directed to repair to Kintyre to receive the obedience of the principal chiefs and surety for the payment of the King’s rents. Angus MacDonald of Dunnyveg duly presented himself and paid the arrears of rent due for his lands in Kintyre and Islay, and furthermore gave his son Archibald as a hostage for his future obedience. Notwithstanding this fact the Privy Council in 1607, influenced by Argyll, gave the latter a charter for the lands in North and South Kintyre, and the Island of Jura also (Gregory, pp. 306-312, pp. 349-361).

1772. On 10th October 1678 Argyll was granted a commission

[TD 293]

by the Scottish Council to take possession of Mull, and when he landed there the Macleans, who had not yet recovered from the heavy losses they had sustained at Inverkeithing, offered no resistance, and instead retreated to the mountains (Historical Notices of Scottish Affairs by Sir John Lauder of Fountainhall, Vol. I, pp. 204-205).

1776. “The brave Lauchlan Mór, the Scourge of the MacDonalds”, was killed in 1598 at Gruineard in Islay, where he was treacherously attacked by James MacDonald of Islay with a force of men numerically superior to his own. He had come to negotiate with MacDonald concerning the division of Islay, half of which the latter had consented to give over to him after he had been defeated in a skirmish by Lachlan Mór (Wardlaw MS., pp. 232-233).

1781. Lachlan Maclean of Brolas, who was one of the tutors appointed to the young Chief—the other being Lachlan Maclean of Torloisk.

1782. The reading in JM is preferable to that of Maclean Sinclair, but there are two difficulties: (a) the Maclean chiefs were buried not in Oran’s Chapel but in Reilig Odhrain; (b) Chaluim is a syllable too many.

1787. Maclean’s tutors allied themselves to MacDonald of Glengarry and Cameron of Lochiel in order to strengthen their position against Argyll, but in 1678 the latter secured the non-interference of Lochiel by forgiving him a debt of 40,000 merks, whence arose the bye-word: Chain Eóghan a Dhia, ach chaill an t-Iarla a chuid airgid (MacFarlane, Genealogical Collections, Vol. I, p. 138; cf. Nicolson, Gaelic Proverbs, p. 136).


ORAN DO SHIR DÒMHNALL SHLÉITE

In this song of eulogy, which appears to be addressed to Sir Donald MacDonald, eldest son and successor of Sir James MacDonald of Sleat, the poet follows the pattern which is usually associated with songs of this type. Reference is made to the noble descent of the family, their honourable record in time of war and their pursuits in time of peace. The hospitality of the chief is warmly acclaimed and a tribute of praise conferred upon his wife.

Several versions of the poem are extant. In McL MS. 173 it is

[TD 294]

entitled “Oran Molaidh le Ian Manndach do Mhatha Comhnuil” (i.e. Mhac Dhomhnaill). It is marked “Achandarroch, Octr. 1774”. The versions in E and G, in which only slight differences in readings occur, are similar to but not identical with this version. The title given is “Oran le Iain Lom air dha fearg a ghabhail ri Mac mhic Alasdair Ghlinne garragh thoisich e air moladh Shir Alasdair M Comhnuil agus Shir Seumas a mhac”. McL MS. 185 provides quite a different version of the poem entitled: “Beannacha Cuain Shir Alastair”, which is parallel in many respects to a version found in the Cluny Charter Chest dated 1790-1800, although the text of the latter is corrupt (GSI, Vol. XXIII, p. 278). Subsequent versions are substantially the same as that of E, and with the exception of O significant differences in readings are few.

The foregoing text derives from G supplemented by one reading from McL MS. 173.

1791. This line is omitted in McL MS. 173, but although the number of lines in the strophe is usually five this varies, and its omission leaves no one to whom the action can be attributed except the ale-wife, which is improbable.

1792. an deoch-sa: possibly an interpolation although found in all sources except CCC, which reads Deoch-slàinte.

With regard to the title Captain, MacBain has pointed out that it was synonymous with that of Chief and not applied to one who was merely de facto head of the clan as Skene maintained (Highlanders of Scotland, p. 413). Donald MacDonald succeeded his father on his death in 1678.

1793. Alasdair òg: Professor Watson states that the poem was composed shortly after the Keppoch murder in 1663, and that Sir Alasdair Og, as found in the earliest sources is an error—there being no such person at this time (BGh, p. 314; cf. Celtic Magazine, Vol. V, pp. l00-101). The reading which he adopts derives from a manuscript which Dr MacDonald, Killearnan, claims to have had, and it is certainly an easier one in this context. The unanimity of the sources is so remarkable, however, that the original reading may have been ’S air Alasdair, referring to some occasion on which Dòmhnall an Dùin was accompanied by his brother Alexander. Confusion between ’S air and Sir would be almost inevitable.

an Caol: Kyleakin or Kylerea. Cf. l. 2752.

[TD 295]

1813. gill: pl. of geall, frequently a promise, pledge, bet or wager, e.g.:

<gai>Gu bràth cha till
Gun téid na gill a chur leotha.<eng>

Never will they return until the wagers are won by them.
(MacCodrum, Songs, ll. 2468-2469)

Professor Watson suggests two possible interpretations of the phrase roimh ’n rùisgte na gill: (1) “before whom the wagers (pledges) are declared”; (2) “before whom the wagers (pledges) are stripped” (BGh, pp. 314-315. For further examples of the usage of geall v. BGh, Glossary.) A further possibility is that gill may refer in this context to the prizes of hunting.

1820. ceòl-cluaise: music for the ear.

Cf.:
<gai>Leat bu mhiann mar cheol cluaise
Feadain bhinn nan sgal cruaidhe.<eng>
(MacDougall, Songs, p. 119. See also BGh, l. 2319)
