1838. Sir Donald married Margaret Douglas, second daughter of Robert, 3rd Earl of Morton, on 24th July 1662 (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 473).

1843. A house and half Scotland, i.e. the greater part of the country. A similar phrase occurs in a lament ascribed to Deirdre: Bail 7 leith Albann fein do bhi agam (Rel. Celt., Vol. I, p. 151); cf.

<gai>Bhuinnig iad baile ’s leth Alba…
Bhuinnig iad latha Chath Gairbheach.<eng>
(MacCodrum, Songs, ll. 1903, 1905)

1849. A description of the MacDonald coat-of-arms.

1852-1853. Reference obscure.


BLÀR TOM A’ PHUBAILL

In 1675 the MacDonalds of Glengarry, the MacDonalds of Keppoch, and the Camerons went to the assistance of the Macleans, who were being harassed by the Earl of Argyll, but no fighting took place—a fact which greatly displeased the poet,

[TD 296]

who would have them take a more positive course of action, and enter Glen Aray to hack the wry-mouth Campbells. The following year the Earl proceeded to Edinburgh and then to London to gain official support for his campaign against the Macleans. (Cf. l. 2272 n.) In order to ensure a fair hearing of the case, Glengarry preceded him to Edinburgh, and thereafter to London. The poet expresses his appreciation of the firm stand thus taken by Glengarry against Argyll. The latter was confirmed in the possession of many of the Maclean estates in 1681, the year after Lord MacDonell’s death, but the poet is confident that the Macleans would find ample support, if only they took the initiative to reclaim their lands.

The text of the poem derives from McL MSS. 14, 213. McL MS. 14 contains vv. 1, 2, 4, 6, 8, 9, 10, 11, 12, 13; McL MS. 213 vv. 1, 3, 5, 7. No title is given apart from “Ho ro gur fada”. The version in SO and O derives from Turner, where the poem is entitled “Oran air blar Tomaphubuill, eidear na h-Earra-gha’laich ’s na h-Athollaich, le Iain Lom, ’s e ’g iomadan nach robh Moir-fhear Chlann Donuill aig an tigh, gu cuideacha’ leis na h-Athollaich agus na h-Earragha’laich air faotainn buaidh”. The fifth and seventh stanzas are not found in this source and a number of different readings occur throughout. Three stanzas are found in McL MS. 247, two of which are variants of vv. 3, 5. The third refers to the Marquis of Huntly, and a parallel version is also incorporated in T. Six stanzas of the poem (vv. 2, 4, 9, 11, 12, 13), communicated by Mr Alexander MacDonald, Upper South River, Nova Scotia, are included in the Supplement to the Gesto Collection of Highland Music. The readings are the same as in Maclean Sinclair’s version.

1894. McL reads Iarl Ilich, which is surely an error for Iarl Adhraich, the Earl of Argyll, a name which derives from the River Aray. (Cf. Gleann Adhra, Inbhir Adhra.)

1943. euradh (T): preferable to iarradh (McL) from the point of view of context and assonance.

1963. The first chief of the clan was known as “Gille Eathain na Tuaighe” (fl. c. 1240)—so called because of a battle-axe which he is said to have carried on all occasions (History of the Clan Maclean, p. 33).

1974. frioghan: a bristle; cf. the metaphorical usage in Scots of birse, a bristle, to indicate rage or displeasure; “to set up one’s

[TD 297]

birse”—to put one in a rage. The birse is also said to rise when one’s temper becomes heated (Jamieson’s Scottish Dictionary, Vol. I, p. 198). The metaphor derives from the fact that the bristles or hackles of some animals rise with rage.


MARBHRANN DO MHORAIR CHLANN DÒMHNAILL

The subject of this elegy and of the one which follows is Lord MacDonell, who died in Edinburgh on 6th December 1680, without issue, and was buried in the Chapel of Holyrood (Scots Peerage, Vol. V, pp. 562-565; Clan Donald, Vol. II, p. 448).

The two primary sources for the poem are EM and T. The seventh and eighth stanzas and also the last three are omitted in the latter source, and different versions of the fourteenth and fifteenth stanzas are supplied. One stanza is included which is not found in the earlier version. The version in I is similar to T, and differences in readings are slight. GlB and O are based directly on T. The foregoing text derives from EM with a slight alteration in the order of stanzas.

2005. From the point of view of assonance Turner’s reading fàth is good.

2015. A reference to his leaving no issue. (Cf. l. 2018.)


MARBHRANN DO’N MHORAIR CHEUDNA

The oldest version of this, the shorter of the two elegies composed to Lord MacDonell, is found in T, and from this version derive the versions in O and GlB, although GlB adheres more closely to the original. The version in I is parallel to T, and is marked “incomplete” by the writer.

2018. He was succeeded by his first cousin, Ranald MacDonald, son of Donald Gorm of Scotus (Clan Donald, Vol. II, p. 449).

2023. The Earl of Argyll prosecuted the policy of tyranny and aggrandisement against the Macleans of Duart which his late father had commenced. (For the facts of his extortionate demands see Hist. MSS. Com. Report VI, App. pp. 617-633.) He attempted by various means to secure the non-intervention of Glengarry but without success, for the latter did go to the assistance of the Macleans, and confirmed the guardians of the young Chief, Allan Maclean, in the resistance offered by them to

[TD 298]

the demands of Argyll. The fact that he made his intervention felt in these critical years, when he espoused the cause of the Macleans of Mull, is evidenced by the notices of complaints lodged against him by Argyll, which are entered in the minutes of the Privy Council (P.C.R., Vol. V, p. 5; pp. 59-62). In 1679 a commission was granted to the latter, authorising him to raise a force for the capture of Lord MacDonell and others, and later that year a decree of forfeiture was passed against him (P.C.R., Vol. VI, pp. 170-173).

2027. The reading in GlB is of course incorrect. Glengarry died in Edinburgh and not in Mull.


CUMHA DO GHILL-EASBUIG NA CEAPAICH

Archibald MacDonald, 14th of Keppoch, was the second eldest son of Alasdair Buidhe. He was implicated in the Keppoch murder, but appears to have been held in great esteem by Iain Lom. The fact that he was a staunch supporter of Glengarry in his campaign with the Macleans against Argyll would have been a strong recommendation in itself, but, in addition, his wisdom and equity in the administration of clan affairs commended themselves to the poet, and he laments his death with genuine grief. The poem ends with an exhortation to his eldest son and successor, the famous Colla nam Bó, to maintain the standards of integrity set by his forebears.

The poem is found in D, from which derives directly the version in GlB. The version in O is also based on D, but eight additional stanzas are incorporated. Of these the first three and the sixth are found also in “Oran do dh’Aonghas mac Raghnaill Oig” (GlB, p. 147; O, p. 101). The fourth is Maclean Sinclair’s own composition; the fifth is an emended version of one in “Iorram le Eachann Bacach” (T, p. 114), while the seventh is included in both poems mentioned above. The final stanza is contained in Turner’s version of “Marbhrann do Shir Seumas Mac Dhòmhnaill”, and is also included in “Oran do dh’Aonghas mac Raghnaill Oig”.

2046. “This man in his Old Age was tormented with Gangrene all over his Body of which Loathsome Distemper he at last Died” (The Highlands of Scotland in 1750, p. 99). James Fraser states that he was the contriver of the plot to kill the Chief of Keppoch and his brother, and that the finger with which he signed the

[TD 299]

contract to murder the youths turned gangrenous and eventually caused his death (Wardlaw MS., p. 463). The general tenor of this poem tends to contradict Fraser’s statements.

2050. According to the song which he composed on his deathbed Archibald MacDonald suffered great pain from an ailment which affected his jaws.

<gai>Cràiteach glé phianmhor
So dh’fhàg mi gu h-iargainneach tinn,
Tha còrr is dà bhliadhna
On a thàrmaich so ’n giallaibh mo chinn.<eng>
(MC, p. 69)

Fraser-Mackintosh shows that he died in December 1682. (Antiquarian Notes, 1st Series, p. 11.) According to Macpherson he died before the battle of Killiecrankie (D, p. 113). The dates given by MacKenzie and the authors of Clan Donald are 1682 and 1688 respectively (History of the MacDonalds, p. 490; Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 422). The author of the MacDonalds of Keppoch (p. 66) states that he is said to have died c. 1683, although according to Lochaber tradition he was present at Killiecrankie. His son Coll is on record on 1st March 1688, presumably as chief of his clan (Mackintosh Muniments, No. 624).


ORAN DO MHARCUS ATHOLL

In 1684 the Marquis of Atholl was appointed Lord Lieutenant and Sheriff of Argyll in place of the Earl of Argyll, who in 1681 was found guilty of high treason and imprisoned in Edinburgh Castle whence he succeeded in making his escape and fleeing to Holland. There he conceived the plot of invading Scotland, in conjunction with Monmouth’s Rebellion in England, and when the Government became aware of his intended invasion, Atholl was granted full jurisdiction over all Campbell estates. On 5th August 1684 a commission was granted to him appointing him Lieutenant of the shires of Argyll and Tarbert, and, on 16th August, Lieutenant of Inveraray (P.C.R., Vol. IX, pp. 81-83, p. 96).

The two primary sources, which differ substantially in many readings, are:

(1) McL MS. 16: “Oran do Mharcus Adhoill le Ian Lom, o bheul Uilleam Stuart am Piteaghabhann”. This is the text

[TD 300]

followed here with the exception of v. 15, which is not found in this source.

(2) Turner: “Oran le Iain Lom, oir latha blair na h-Airdereanaich aig Ionmhuraora, far an d’fhuair na h-Athollaich buaidh ann an eirig latha Toma-phubaill”. From this source derives the version in SO and O. In both sources the sequence of the first eight stanzas is the same, as is the order of lines, but throughout the remainder of the poem the sequence of verses differs although generally the order of lines in each verse remains the same. Turner omits vv. 9, 12, 24, and includes vv. 14, 15 in his version of “Blàr Tom a’ Phubaill”. Two stanzas are found in his version for which he is the only primary source. McL MS. 14 contains vv. 14, 15, 25. These are also found in McL MS. 213 together with two half-stanzas wrongly joined (vv. 4, 22). A version of the poem, consisting of twenty-four stanzas, is found in I entitled: “Oran da Mharcus Atholl agus a Thurras da dh’Earra-ghael sa bhliadna 1685”. The order of the first ten stanzas is the same as in T, and the versions are somewhat similar, but vv. 15, 23 as in T are omitted in I.

2068. Reference obscure.

2073. This saying is quoted by Sheriff Nicolson (Gaelic Proverbs, p. 7; cf. MacCodrum, Songs, l. 2400).

2075-2076. In 1685 Kintyre underwent systematic pillaging at the hands of Atholl’s followers and others, and these excesses became known as the Atholl raids. It has been pointed out that these should not be linked up with the name of the Marquis of Atholl. After the Revolution an enquiry into the matter was held, and a list of those pillaged and making claims for compensation was prepared and printed, but whether or not they received compensation is not recorded (McKerral, Kintyre in the 17th Century, pp. 130-133). Wodrow heard vaguely of cruelties exercised there until Atholl was checked by the Privy Council. After the Revolution Atholl, while defending himself against these charges, stated that beyond cutting down trees for huts his Highlanders behaved well (Lang, History of Scotland, Vol. IV, p. 310). In a letter to the Marquis from the Earl of Melfort, dated 28th September 1686, the latter assures him that, whatever may have taken place in Argyll without his knowledge, he stands exonerated (Hist. MSS. Com. Report XII, App. 8, p. 35, No. 89).

2085. Dunstaffnage Castle, the principal stronghold of Clan

[TD 301]

Campbell, is situated near the point of a peninsula at the entrance to Loch Etive, and is about four miles north of Oban. The site of the Castle is a rocky platform rising from twenty to thirty feet above ground level. The walls follow the outline of the rock and are built sheer from the edge, so as to allow no foothold on the rock outside. (For a detailed description of the Castle see The Castellated and Domestic Architecture of Scotland, by MacGibbon and Ross, Vol. 1, pp. 85-93; Dunstaffnage Castle and the Stone of Destiny, by Dr W. Douglas Simpson, Edinburgh, 1958.)

2090. T reads gunna praise, a brazen cannon. In a report furnished by the Marquis c. September 1684, relating to the instructions which he received on his appointment to the Lieutenancy of Argyll, he states that the arms all over the shire were collected at Inveraray (Hist. MSS. Com. Report XII, App. 8, p. 12, No. 16).

2091. Inch-connel Castle, the original home of the Argyll family, stands on a small island near the eastern side of Loch Awe. (For a description of the Castle see C.D.A.S., Vol. III, pp. 87-90.)

2103. McL reads an fhir nach a maireann, i.e. Lord MacDonell, while T reads an fhir a tha maireann, i.e. Ranald, ninth Chief of Glengarry, who was at this time an old man—his son, Alasdair Dubh, being de facto Chief (Clan Donald, Vol. III, pp. 311-312).

2107-2108. The clergy and Puritans. Turner reads:

<gai>A luchd nan ceann-bhearta cràbhaidh
Thionndaidh falachd a’ chrùin ruibh.<eng>

You who wear head-dress of religion, the displeasure of the crown has turned against you. (A reference to Argyll and his followers who were Presbyterians and supporters of the Covenant.)

2110. Ranald MacDonald, third Chief of Kinlochmoidart, who fought at Killiecrankie and Sheriffmuir and died in 1725 (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 299).

2111. The MacDonalds of Keppoch.

2113. Nach cumadh qualifies fiùrain: bows of yew, that could not be held, i.e. strung.

2116. I.e. to the full length of the arrow.

2117. Angus MacDonald of Largie, whose estates were for-

[TD 302]

feited in 1649 and given over to the Marquis of Argyll (ibid., pp. 383-384)

2128. The Marquis of Atholl marched to Argyll in August 1684 to enforce his commission as Lord Lieutenant of Argyll and Tarbert (Atholl Chronicles, Vol. I, p. 187).

2133. There may be a reference in this stanza and in the two succeeding ones to the tale of “Belling the Cat” (cur an chluigín), which appears to have been imperfectly understood as there seem to be only half remembered phrases in some lines. (See Watson, Scottish Gaelic Studies, Vol. II, p. 78 ff.)

2153. torc refers to the Campbells, and fiadh presumably to the men of Atholl.

2157, 2159. ad and currac are used metaphorically to indicate the reduction in status which Argyll had undergone.

2164. Cf. l. 2663.

2168. The reading fear (McL 14) appears to be pear in McL 16, 213, which is interesting as the p is probably for b, and that would be an instance of f into b after n as heard sometimes in common speech, e.g. am beil for am bheil, and such an expression as gus am boi for gus am faigh (till one gets). The reading is an indication of the text being taken down from a reciter.

2172. Tom a’ Phubaill is on the top of the hill pass leading to Rannoch and Strath Tummel.

2178. I.e. the Campbells.

2180. The reference in these lines is to the occasion on which Sir Ewen Cameron of Lochiel unwittingly attacked a party of Perthshire horse whom he mistook for the enemy, killing five of their number and wounding others. The incident took place on the night of 9th June 1685 (Hist. MSS. Com. Report XII, App. 8, p. 18; Memoirs of Locheill, pp. 212-213; Fountainhall, Historical Observes, p. 177).

2196. Maclean of Loch Buie and Maclean of Duart.

2200. I.e. one capable of stabbing you in the back.

2203. I.e. two men, striking in turn.

2212. A reference to the forfeiture of the Earl of Argyll’s estates in 1681, and to his exile.

[TD 303]

2215. In 1640 the Earl of Argyll proceeded to Atholl with a force of 5,000 men, to enforce the submission of John Murray, first Earl of Atholl, the father of the subject of this poem, who had hitherto held aloof from the Covenanters. Atholl despatched commissioners of peace to meet him, undertaking to support the Covenant, but Argyll refused to negotiate with him unless he appeared in person. Having been assured of “safe access and recess”, Atholl duly presented himself before the Earl, but was taken prisoner (Hist. MSS. Com. Report XII, App. 8, p. 25, No. 62; Guthry, Memoirs, p. 64; Spalding, Trubles, Vol. I, p. 271). He appeared before the Committee of Estates, and was released only after the payment of £10,000 Scots, and a promise to join the Covenanters with a regiment of 500 men (Balfour, Historical Works, Vol. II, p. 380). This is probably the iasad referred to in l. 2100.

2220. The house of Airlie, from which Lord Ogilvie had escaped only two days previously, was burnt to the ground in July 1640 (ibid.).

2231. Mac Choimisteir Stiùbhairt: apparently a son of John Stewart, Commissary Clerk of Argyll, whose testament was registered on 3rd December 1685. (Commissariat of Argyll, p. 46. See also Herbert Campbell, The Argyll Sasines, Vol. I, Nos. 513, 514.)

2252 ff. In 1676 the Earl was created Marquis of Atholl, Viscount Balquhidder and Lord Balvenie, and in 1685 he was appointed Lord Privy Seal. He had been appointed Keeper of the Palace of Falkland in 1658 (P.C.R., Vol. XI, p. 21; Atholl Chronicles, Vol. I, p. 114).

2257. I.e. sign as under the Great Seal.


TÙIRNEAL A’ CHNATAIN

This poem is addressed to Sir Ewen Cameron of Lochiel, who became Chief of the Clan in 1647 at the age of eighteen (Memoirs of Locheill, pp. 67-85). The immediate occasion of the poem was probably Lochiel’s visit to the court of King Charles II in 1685, to apply for a grant of superiority over the lands which he had formerly held under the jurisdiction of Argyll, and which at the time of Argyll’s forfeiture were made over by the Crown to the Duke of Gordon as part of Argyll’s forfeited estates. James II,

[TD 304]

then Duke of York, interceded on his behalf, and Lochiel’s request was given a favourable hearing, but owing to a technical error the deeds required to be re-written before they could be signed, and before this was done King Charles died (ibid., pp. 209-210).

The text followed is that of McL MS. 154, where the poem is entitled “Tuirneal a Chnatain. Do Shir Eoghan Loch-iall an diaidh laethe Sroin-neamhais”. The version in G probably derives from this source although not all the variant readings in McL are supplied. The sequence of stanzas in the latter does not appear to be established, and that of G has been adopted. In McL MS. 78 there are ten stanzas (vv. 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 10, 14, 15, 16, 17) entitled “Oran air Sir Eoghan Camaron Lochiall”. Three are found in McL MS. 204 (vv. 21, 22, 23), with the heading “Air Sir (Ridir written above) Eoghan Camaron Lochiall nar thug e scorn as t Shagsonach le Fhiaclabh”. McL MS. 222 supplies nine stanzas (vv. 6, 13, 18, 19, 20, 21, 22, 23, 26) entitled “Comhairlie Charmaig do mhac mu dheimhin nam mnai a bhuineadh do sheachnadh”.

The longest version of the poem is found in I. (See Appendix I, p. 408.) There is a note which says that Sir Ewen found fault with the poet for comparing him to a dog, in consequence of which he added the last ten strophes of the poem.

GB and O derive directly from G. A version obtained from Mr Donald MacDonald, a native of Lochaber, has been published in the Supplement to the Celtic Annual (1916), p. 85. Two stanzas are included which are not known in any other source. They refer to Argyll, and are couched in pithy and expressive language. (See p. 307 infra.) From the first of these it would appear that the poem was composed prior to the Earl of Argyll’s execution, although included in this version also is the stanza which states that the Earl had already been left all powerless by the “Maiden”. While most of the readings in Maclean Sinclair’s version of the poem are found in MacDonald’s text, the latter also provides a number of very interesting variants for particular lines.

2263. Fear: omitted in all primary sources and supplied by Maclean Sinclair.

2272. In September 1675 Argyll embarked for Mull with a large force to attack the Macleans, but his ships were driven back by a storm and his proposed invasion was thus postponed.

[TD 305]

He then proceeded to Edinburgh to apply for Government aid against the Macleans, but as his mission there did not meet with the success which he desired, he set out for London where he hoped to receive such assistance as he required from John Maitland, Duke of Lauderdale, who was in collusion with him. After several months’ delay the dispute was remitted to three members of the Privy Council, and in June 1676 the matter came up for consideration. The Earl charged Maclean with a debt of over £27,000 Sterling, and a year later a decision was given in favour of Argyll (History of the Clan Maclean, p. 187; Lauder of Fountainhall, Historical Notices, Vol. I, p. 108; Willcock, A Scots Earl, pp. 198-199).

2283. I.e. because it is its nature to do so.

2290. Cf. ll. 953-955.

2301. In 1672 Sir Ewen Cameron married a sister of Sir Allan Maclean of Duart (Memoirs, p. 182). These lines refer to the assistance which he rendered to the Macleans against Argyll.

According to one source Iain Lom composed a song to Sir Ewen and his first wife, a sister of Sir James MacDonald of Sleat, on the occasion of their wedding. His reason for so doing was partly due to a petition which he himself was presenting to have restored to him three cows taken from him in recent years. These he was given and 300 merks as well. An English translation of twenty-six lines of the eulogy is quoted, but the original does not appear to be extant (Cameron, The Clan Cameron, p. 38). Lochiel’s biographer, who also gives an English translation of the song in question, states that the author came from Braemar (Memoirs of Locheill, pp. 155-158). The latter is a more probable supposition. He may have been one of the Cameron descendants of the Tàilleir Dubh, who were numerous in Kincardine on the Spey.

2302. Ewen Cameron, 13th Chief of Lochiel, known in Gaelic as Eóghan mac Ailein, built a castle for his own residence on the banks of the River Lochy, about five miles from Fort William, called from the rock on which it was erected Tor Castle (MacKenzie, History of the Camerons, p. 39). Its ruin is known locally as “Banquo’s House”, i.e. Banquo, Thane of Lochaber, in Macbeth (Kilgour, Lochaber in War and Peace, pp. 178-179). This remained the seat of the family until the time of Sir Ewen, who had it demolished when he built a mansion for himself at Achnacarry—the old Castle of Achnacarry which was burned

[TD 306]

down in 1746, when the country was overrun and wasted after Culloden.

2316. A reference to a skirmish which took place between the Camerons and the Protectorate garrison at Inverlochy, about a mile eastwards from the garrison, at the foot of Ben Nevis. The Governor had destroyed some of Lochiel’s woods in order to supply him with winter fuel, and to avenge this action Lochiel set out with a company of men and lay in wait for the enemy. When the English emerged his several parties sallied forth and inflicted dire slaughter upon them, leaving the road to the garrison strewn with carcases (Memoirs of Locheill, pp. 130-131).

2319. earrdhearg: red murrain, a disease in cattle, like bun-dearg. See Carmichael, Carmina Gadelica, Vol. II, p. 238, s. v. bun-dearg.

These lines refer to a further clash between the Cromwellian soldiers from the garrison and a band of Camerons at Achdalieu on the shore of Loch Eil. In the course of the melée Lochiel was separated from his clansmen, and became involved in a hand-to-hand conflict with one of the English officers. At one point his opponent succeeded in dragging Lochiel to the ground and was on the point of despatching him with his dagger. In his effort to raise himself he lifted his head, thereby exposing his neck. Lochiel was quick to take advantage of any opportunity which the desperate situation presented, and he promptly jumped at the Englishman’s extended throat, which he said God had put in his mouth, biting it right through—“the sweetest bite he ever had in his life” was his own description of it later on (Memoirs of Locheill, pp. 118-119).

The story is told that Lochiel went into a barber’s shop in London several years later, and in the course of conversation the barber asked if he came from the North. Lochiel replied in the affirmative, and asked him if he knew anyone from those parts. “No”, replied the other, “nor do I wish to. Would you believe it, one of them tore the throat out of my father, and I only wish I had the fellow’s throat as near me now as yours is” (Lochaber in War and Peace, p. 22).

The incident at Achdalieu suggested to Sir Walter Scott the combat between Fitz-James and Roderick Dhu, which is so graphically described in the fifth Canto of The Lady of the Lake:

Like adder darting from his coil,
Like wolf that dashes through the toil,

[TD 307]

Like mountain-cat that guards his young,
Full at Fitz-James’s throat he sprung;
They tug, they strain! down, down they go,
The Gael above, Fitz-James below.
The Chieftain’s gripe his throat compress’d,
His knee was planted on his breast.

2334. eangach: translated nimble-footed. Primary meaning of eang is hoof, foot; secondary meaning, nimbleness, derives from frequent association of the word with deer. It is possible that mór may qualify eangach in this context and so mean large-hoofed. For a note on the word eang and examples of its usage see Éigse, Vol. VI, Pt. I, p. 61.

The following stanzas are found in CA:

<gai>Rìgh gur muladach thà mi
Siubhal iomall mo shràidean
’S nach fhaic mi fear-turuis nan làirig a Dùn-éideann.
(v. 1)

Mas e ’m bàs a fhuair t’athair
Bhios a’ feitheamh ort fhathast,
Bidh craos cam ort ’s thu ’g amharc na gréine.
(v. 8)

’S gum bi nead aig a’ chuileig
Ann an ìochdar do shlugain,
Anns an t-sloc ’s an do thuinich na breugan.
(v. 9)

La eile ’na dheaghaidh, (1)
Ann an Acha-da-Shleighe,
Chuir thu feachd agus faghaid mu’n treud ud. (2)
(v. 24)
<eng>

ORAN AIR FEACHD RÌGH SEUMAS

In these lines the poet gives an account of the activities of the clans prior to Killiecrankie. He describes their march from the head of Loch Eil and follows their route to the scene of battle; he refers to the formation of the clans on the battlefield, and gives a concise account of the battle itself, the death of


(1) I.e. after Sròn Nibheis.

(2) For variant forms of the first and fourth stanzas see Appendix I, pp. 408, 409.

[TD 308]

Dundee and the dispersal of the enemy’s troops. The poem is not a description of the itinerary of the MacDonalds of Keppoch as is supposed by A. H. Millar. (See his translation of the poem in Scottish Historical Review, Vol. III, p. 65 ff.)

The oldest version of the poem is found in Turner, where it is entitled: “Oran air feachd Righ Sheimis a gluasad gu cumail blar Raoin-Ruairidh, ’n aghaidh na’n Sasunnach”. From this source derive the versions in AM, GlB, and O. The poem is ascribed to Iain Lom by Turner, but it is very questionable whether this is his work. It has been alleged (O, p. 78) that the author is not Iain Lom but his son, who was a participant in the battle. (For his song on Killiecrankie see Turner, p. 141.) Mr Donald Campbell had in his possession a different version of this poem, recorded by a Lochaber man, but unfortunately it has been lost (C, p. 169).

2338. The earlier stages of Dundee’s campaign consisted of a series of marches and countermarches where little was gained, and in June 1689 he returned to Lochaber. Scarcity of provisions obliged him to dismiss temporarily the majority of his men, whereupon Lochiel invited him back to his old quarters at Strone, assuring him that while there was a cow in Lochaber neither he nor his men should want. Lochiel’s biographer points out that the cattle at this time were still very lean and the Camerons “had difficulty enough to subsist themselves in any tolerable manner”, so it is not surprising that a scarcity of beef should arise when an armed force had to be catered for in addition (Memoirs of Locheill, pp. 247-248).

2342. cuilean: frequently used as a term of endearment. The poet, composing after the battle, is probably here addressing one who had been deprived of some close relative, though there is no means of identifying the person referred to.

2346. an t-sàile: lit. the sea, here the shore of Loch Eil.

2350. The Colonel is Dundee, not Coll MacDonald of Keppoch, or as he was better known Colla nam Bó, as stated by Millar. The MacDonalds of Keppoch were not present at the battle, but came in three days later (Memoirs of Locheill, p. 283; cf. contemporary poem by Angus MacDonald, the Glencoe bard, MC, p. 75).

2354. thog: picked up, i.e. after sleeping in them.

2365. They pledged their fidelity to the cause. (For a parallel instance see The Grameid by James Philip, pp. 103-104.)

[TD 309]

2375. The poet gives a succinct account in these lines of the route followed by the clans from the head of Loch Lochy to Killiecrankie—l. 2373 indicating the general direction. (Cf. Balhaldy, who says that Dundee kept to the higher ground. Memoirs of Locheill, p. 265.) He states that from the head of Loch Lochy the clans marched through Glen Roy beyond Glen Turret over the moor of Drummond and the hills of Drumochter, and thence to Atholl. This is the route accepted by Mackay of Rockfield, Napier, and Mowbray Morris (Life of Lieutenant-General Hugh Mackay, p. 47; Memoirs of Dundee, Vol. III, p. 360; Claverhouse, p. 206). Professor Terry does not accept this view, but maintains that Dundee led his men across the front of the hill of Lude and did not make a detour round it (John Graham of Claverhouse, Viscount Dundee, p. 336). If this were the case then Dundee’s army would be in full view of Mackay as he emerged from the Pass. Dundee took the unexpected route, and herein may be the reason for Mackay’s hasty command to every battalion “to form by a Quart de Conversion to the right” and march up the hill. He had seen Dundee’s army descending a high hill and he feared they would take up their position on an eminence just above the ground where his troops were drawn up, and thus drive him across the Garry with comparative ease (Memoirs, p. 51).

2380-2381. Despite numerous requests from Dundee to join the King’s cause, Lord Murray, son of the Marquis of Atholl, would not commit himself. The men of Atholl, who had fought under Montrose, were traditionally Jacobites, and when Murray refused to declare his intentions, his men “without further ceremony ran to the river Tumble which was near them, filled their bonnets with water and drank King James his health, with many loud huzzas and acclamations and so deserted him in a full body” (Memoirs of Locheill, p. 257). Lord Murray, however, is said to have stated that most of his men had retreated to the hills to safeguard their cattle and that he had but 200 or 300 with him (Mackay, Memoirs, p. 50).

2385. am prasgan: Mackay’s men, referred to by the Glencoe bard as am pràbar—

<gai>’N uair a thòisich an làmhach
Sud am pràbar ’nan siubhal.<eng>
(MC, p. 77)

[TD 310]

Cf. MacCodrum’s reference:

<gai>Là eil’ ann an Coille Chnagaidh
Dh’fhalbh MacAidh ’s gun d’fhàg e bhagaids.<eng>
(Songs, ll. 1785-1786)

2389. The Pass or Valley of the Fender.

2390. Maclean Sinclair reads Ceann na buidhne which is an easier reading, but Turner, the oldest source, reads Ceann na cuimhne, which may be “the Adjutant who knew the numbers”. Cf. Cairnaquheen—a cairn of stones on the north bank of the Dee about a mile west of Balmoral—which has been etymologised as Càrn na cuimhne. According to tradition Cairnaquheen was a clan gathering place, and when setting out for battle each man placed a stone on the cairn which he removed on his return, thus providing a numeration and a memorial of the dead. (See Dr William Alexander’s article entitled “An Aberdeenshire Cairn and its Traditions”, Scottish Gaelic Studies, Vol. III, pp. 200-202.)

2394. The orderly formation of the clans on the field of battle is here referred to. The position of the clans at Killiecrankie was as follows: On the right wing were posted the Macleans under Sir John Maclean of Duart, “a youth of about 18 years of age” (Memoirs of Locheill, p. 266), then came the Irish contingent and the MacDonalds of Clan Ranald, while the MacDonalds of Glengarry completed the right wing. Dundee’s horse were in the centre and to the left were the Camerons under Lochiel, a battalion under Sir Alexander Maclean, and the MacDonalds of Sleat to the extreme left of the line (Original Papers by James Macpherson, Vol. I, p. 369).

2396. The Highlanders were ordered to withhold fire until within reach of the “very bosoms” of their enemy, and then “poureing it upon them all att once like one great clap of thounder they threw away their guns and fell in pellmell among the thickest of them with their brodd swords” (Memoirs of Locheill, pp. 267-268).

2398. About half an hour before sunset Dundee finally gave the order to attack (Mackay, Memoirs, p. 55). Both sides were in position in the late afternoon, but Dundee would not let his men move until the sun which was shining in their eyes “was nearer its decline” (Memoirs of Locheill, p. 267).

[TD 311]

2401. fearann: the ground or position occupied by the army. (Cf. MacCodrum, Songs, l. 1650.)

2410. Colonel Ramsay was in command of a regiment of the Scots Brigade from Holland in the left wing of Mackay’s army. Only half of the regiment engaged, and that half-heartedly, and Mackay states that after the battle he came upon Ramsay two miles from the scene of battle accompanied by a hundred of his runaways (Mackay, Memoirs, pp. 52-61).

2414. Brigadier Balfour’s regiment fled without firing a shot, and he himself was killed in the battle (ibid., p. 59).


CATH RAON RUAIRIDH

The battle of Killiecrankie was fought on Saturday 27th July 1689 between John Graham of Claverhouse, Viscount Dundee, who had unfurled the banner of the exiled King James in the Highlands, and Major-General Hugh Mackay of Scourie, who had been appointed by William of Orange to command the Government troops in Scotland. The immediate casus belli was the mastery of Blair Castle, which by Dundee’s orders had been seized by Patrick Stewart of Ballechin, who held it for the King. The battle, which was fought on the plain of Urrard below Aldclune in Atholl, ended in a signal victory for the Highlanders, but it was rendered ineffective by the death of Dundee, and by the Highlanders’ lack of confidence in his successor, Colonel Cannon.

The primary sources for the poem are:
(1) McL MS. 25 (reproduced GSI, Vol. XXII, p. 181).
(2) McL MS. 74.
(3) McL MS. 154.
(4) McN¹.
(5) G.

Of these the first two contain 22 stanzas, and apart from orthographical variations, differences in readings are slight. McL MS. 154 supplies twenty stanzas, and is similar to but not identical with G. The foregoing text derives from the two latter sources, apart from v. 27, for which Maclean Sinclair is the only authority. A shorter version is found in McN¹ with a number of independent readings.

Maclean Sinclair has two versions of the poem, the first of which derives from Gilles, with the exception of the last five stanzas, which were supplied by Alexander MacDonald, Ridge (O, p. 78). This version has been translated by A. H. Millar in

[TD 312]

the Scottish Historical Review, Vol. III. The second version, which is somewhat similar to McL 154, was supplied by Alexander MacDonald, Ridge (O, p. 103). The version in BGh is based on G and GB.

2420. aimsir seems to be the time when what the poet has in mind will come to pass. The reason why the fulfilment could not take place before the end of May is that in those days military planners had to take account of the seasons, not only because of weather but also because of seed time and harvest. The poet is hoping for an invasion by King James’ supporters, but he knows that this should not take place too early in the year. The clans would not rise until they had completed the spring work.

Professor Watson states that the poet probably means simply “our outlook is not that of men who see summer coming in” (BGh, p. 308).

2425. Belief in the divine right of kings is echoed frequently in the poetry of this period. Angus MacDonald, the Glencoe bard, voices the same sentiment when referring to King James in his poem entitled “Latha Raon Ruairidh”:

<gai>
Chan eil e ceadaicht’ dhuinn claonadh
No ’n rìgh saoghalta mhùchadh,
’S gur e ’n t-oighre fìor dhligheach
On a ghineadh o thùs e;
Chan fhaod deifir an creidimh
No neo-chreidimh ar tàladh,
’S gun ùghdaras laghail
Is gniomh foilleil dhuinn àicheadh.<eng>
(MC, p. 80)

2426. McL MSS. 25, 74, read eile for iasaid, but the latter is a more significant reading, and better also for assonance.

2429. an Eiphit: Egypt, i.e. a foreign land. A metaphor which of course derives from the exile in Egypt of the Children of Israel.

2430. The Scottish Convention of Estates which met in Edinburgh under the auspices of the Duke of Hamilton on 14th March 1689, preparatory to offering the Crown to the Prince of Orange. (See Minutes Acta Parl. Scot., Vol. IX, p. 3.)

2435. Cf. ll. 355, 746.

2439. ’m brathadair: the kindler (of your loyalty), i.e. King James. The word is translated by Millar as traitor:

[TD 313]

“’Twas not the traitor worthy man
That set fire to the peat,
But the head of a house whom natural ties barred
Became their beacon light.”

2441. William of Orange, whom the poet regards as a usurper.

2443. MacGeorge’s homestead was probably the former farm of Lagnabuaig, about a hundred yards to the west of Urrard House, which was held at this time by a man whose Christian name was George—an uncommon name in this district. See A Military History of Perthshire, Vol. II, p. 269. A good account of Killiecrankie is given in this work, including a note on the site and Dundee’s position in the battle. Lieutenant-Colonel Lawder, who was in command of the left flank of Mackay’s army, took up his position “on a little hill wreathed with trees” (Mackay, Memoirs, p. 56). Elsewhere it is stated more generally that Mackay’s army was marshalled in a young bushy wood (Memoirs of the Lord Viscount Dundee and of the Highland Clans, by an Officer of the Army, p. 31).

2445. “Many had their heads divided into two halves by one blow; others had their sculls cutt off above the eares by a back-strock like a night-cap” writes Lochiel’s biographer (Memoirs, p. 271).

2454. Dundee took up his position in the centre with the horse—Sir William Wallace of Craigie upon King James’ commission having taken over the command of the cavalry, much to Dundee’s dissatisfaction (Memoirs of Locheill, p. 268; Mackay, Memoirs, p. 59).

2456. This reading, which is from G, is more specific than that in the McL MSS.: Mo là deurach ’s mo chreach mar dh’éirich dhuit. The poet’s statement that Dundee’s death occurred at the commencement of the battle is beyond dispute, and is corroborated by a letter written by King James to Colonel Cannon dated 30th November 1689 (Leven and Melville Papers, p. 333; cf. Mackay, Memoirs, p. 265).

2459. The nature of Dundee’s wound has aroused wide-spread controversy and various conflicting statements have been made concerning it. The Earl of Balcarres writing shortly after the battle states that “Dundee received a shot in his right side

[TD 314]

immediately below his armour” (Napier, Memorials of Dundee, Vol. III, p. 644). John Drummond of Balhaldy, whose information derives from the evidence of several of the men who were with Claverhouse during the last moments of his life—his own father being one of them—states that the fatal shot was “about two hands’ breadth within his armour” (Memoirs of Locheill, pp. 269, 272, 281). Professor Terry maintains that these two statements contradict each other, but in fact they do not, as the somewhat ambiguous phrase used by Balhaldy, “within his armour”, must surely mean below his armour, i.e. his breastplate presumably, a conclusion warranted by the qualifying statement which he makes that it was on the lower part of his side he was shot, whence Balhaldy’s informants concluded that he had been wounded while he stretched himself to stand up in his stirrups (Memoirs of Locheill, p. 269). Both sources therefore support the poet’s statement that Dundee was wounded beneath the tail of his uniform, i.e. below his armour.

Professor Terry has advanced the theory that he was shot in the left eye—a theory based on a statement in An Account of the Proceedings of the Meeting of the Estates in Scotland (No. 56, p. 129), to the effect that some of Mackay’s officers had seen Dundee’s body lying in a vault in Blair Church about five weeks after the battle, and had described his mortal wound as having been inflicted “by a shot in his left eye”. In the same source it is stated that Sir Duncan Campbell of Auchinbreck saw the body of Dundee—about this time also it would appear—and Auchinbreck is alleged to have stated that it was not yet interred. This evidence conflicts with evidence recorded elsewhere in the Account, where it is stated that Dundee was buried on 29th July (ibid., No. 44, p. 106), and with the evidence provided by the Acts of Parliament (Vol. IX, App. 56), where it is stated that he was buried within a day or two of the battle (Terry, John Graham of Claverhouse, Viscount Dundee, p. 353).

The absence of a bullet hole in Dundee’s breastplate, which since its exhumation in 1794 has been preserved at Blair Castle, is regarded by Professor Terry as further proof of the fact that he was not shot in the body, thereby disproving the statements made by Balcarres and Balhaldy (ibid., p. 352). As has been noted above, however, both these authorities together with Iain Lom distinctly state that Dundee was shot below his armour, and the absence of a bullet hole in his breastplate in no way invalidates their statement.

[TD 315]

2462. The tradition that Dundee’s body was stripped on the field of battle (Balcarres, Memoirs, p. 47) has been rejected by Professor Terry (op. cit., p. 344). The explanation of this tradition as given by Mark Napier is that before Dundee could be carried off the field it would be necessary to remove his military equipment (Memorials of Dundee, Vol. III, p. 668). The stripping of the slain was not uncommon at this time, and James Philip in his epic poem refers to the enthusiasm with which the Macleans of Lochbuie entered into this practice after the skirmish of Knockbrecht in June 1689 (Grameid, pp. 216-217). It would appear from Iain Lom’s use of the word nochdaidh that Dundee suffered a similar fate after Killiecrankie.

2465. In view of l. 2459 the poet probably means here a wound received while facing the enemy—volnus adversum.

2481. Dòmhnall Gorm Og, son of Sir Donald MacDonald of Sleat, was in command of the Clan at Killiecrankie, as his father became ill at Lochaber and was obliged to return home (Memoirs of Dundee by an Officer, p. 30; Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 71). He is described by James Philip, Dundee’s standard-bearer at Killiecrankie, as “the noble off-spring of the great Donald Chief of the race, and Lord of the Isles, illustrious in war beyond his youthful years” (The Grameid, p. 125).

2483. The MacDonalds of Sleat were placed at the extreme edge of the left wing of Dundee’s Army. (See Letter dated 15th December 1689 to the Queen, contained in Original Papers by James Macpherson, Vol. I, p. 369; Napier, Memorials, Vol. III, p. 633; Memoirs of Locheill, p. 266.) Iain Lom’s son describes their position thus:

<gai>Bha Dòmhnall an Dùin
Gu dlùth air uilinn a’ bhlàir.<eng>
(T, p. 141)

They were opposed to Hastings’ and Mackay’s regiment who, according to Mackay, “made the best fire and all the execution” that day, and their losses were heavy as a result (Mackay, Memoirs, p. 59). Commenting on the casualties of the MacDonalds of Sleat, Angus MacDonald, the Glencoe bard, says:

<gai>
Och a Dhòmhnaill nan Dòmhnall,
’S òg a fhuair thu do dheuchainn,
’S iomadh bean umad brònach
Eadar Tròntairnis ’s Sléite;

[TD 316]

Gu bheil cinneadh mór t’athar
Is iad ’nan laighe gun éirigh,
Luchd bhualadh nam buillean
Am feadh ’s a dh’fhuirich an t-eug uath’.<eng>
(MC, p. 74)

2484. Tàinistear: the “tanist” or heir apparent was appointed during the chief’s lifetime in order to avoid confusion at his death. (For a discussion of the law of tanistry see Skene, Highlanders of Scotland, p. 104 ff.) Dòmhnall Gorm Og was heir apparent at this time and he succeeded his father on his death in February 1695 (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 79).

2487. Alasdair Dubh of Glengarry, referred to by one of the witnesses in Dundee’s forfeiture as “a tall man somewhat black” (Napier, Memorials, Vol. III, p. 625), led the Glengarry men at Killiecrankie, as his father, Ranald MacDonald, was at this time old and feeble, and unable to take part in the activities of the clan (Clan Donald, Vol. II, p. 450). He is described by Lochiel’s biographer as “brave loyall and wonderfully sagacious” (Memoirs, p. 261), and we find a number of references to the valiant conduct of Alasdair Dubh at Killiecrankie in the Gaelic poetry of this period. (See “Latha Raon Ruairidh” by Angus MacDonald, MC, p. 75, and the reference by Iain Lom’s son to “Alasdair ciar” in his song on the battle, T, p. 141.) For the prodigious feats of valour ascribed to Alasdair Dubh on the day of the battle see Chambers, History of the Rebellions in Scotland, pp. 104-105.

2490. Iain Og, who was wounded at Killiecrankie, was a younger brother of Alasdair Dubh, and was the progenitor of the MacDonalds of Loch Garry (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 328).

2492. spéileireachd: from Scots spiel to play, probably used in this context with the force of sword-play.

2495. Cf.: Ceann nathrach is earball peucaig air an Earrach (Nicolson, Gaelic Proverbs, p. 78). Professor Derick Thomson has drawn my attention to the following quotation: March comes in with an adder’s head and goes out with a peacock’s tail (C. N. French, A Countryman’s Day Book, p. 48); cp. The Poems of William Dunbar, ed. W. Mackay Mackenzie, p. 141 (“Of the Changes of Lyfe”, ll. 7-9).

[TD 317]

MURT GHLINNE COMHANN

The title of this poem bears little relation to the content, as there is but a single reference to the actual Massacre. It is rather the expression of a wish that the Campbell territories should be distributed among particular septs of Clan Donald, and that utter destruction should be the lot of the Siol Diarmaid. If Iain Lom is indeed the author of these lines the subdued tone of the poem is surprising, particularly when one recalls the forceful vigour with which he handled the theme of the Keppoch murder. The only extant version of the poem is that in MC.

The principal authority in English is the Report of the enquiry into the Massacre by the Commission of the Scottish Parliament presented in June 1695 (Gordon, Papers illustrative of the Political Conditions of the Highlands of Scotland, p. 99 ff.). On 27th August 1691 a proclamation was issued by the Government of King William offering pardon and indemnity to all who should take the oath of allegiance before the first day of January 1692 (ibid., pp. 35-37). Before the expiry of the specified time the Chief of the MacDonalds of Glencoe presented himself to Colonel Hill, who commanded the garrison of Inverlochy, in order that the oath of allegiance should be administered to him. As he did not possess magisterial powers, Hill was not entitled to administer the oath, but he urged him to hasten to Sir Colin Campbell of Ardkinglass, the Sheriff-Depute of Argyll at Inveraray, and furnished him with a letter to Campbell urging him to receive him “as a lost sheep”. On his way to Inveraray he was detained for twenty-four hours by Captain Drummond, who commanded the Government troops at the castle of Barcaldine, and he did not arrive in Inveraray till 2nd or 3rd January. His purpose was delayed further by the fact that the Sheriff-Depute was three days late in arriving in Inveraray and the oath was not administered until 6th January. MacDonald returned to Glencoe with the false assurance that he and his clan were now safeguarded. A certificate to the effect that Glencoe had taken the oath was forwarded to Edinburgh to Colin Campbell, Sheriff-Clerk of Argyll. It was produced by him to the Clerks of the Council, but they refused to accept it because the oath was taken after the specified time had expired. Lord Aberuchill, also a Campbell, and a member of the Privy Council, was consulted on the matter and expressed his opinion in conjunction with other Privy Councillors that the certificate could not be regarded as valid without a warrant from the King. The matter was not

[TD 318]

brought before the Council as a whole, though that was the intention of Ardkinglass (ibid., p. 99 ff.).

On 16th January 1692, instructions were issued, signed and countersigned by King William, in accordance with which the massacre was carried out: “If McKean of Glencoe and that tribe can be well separated from the rest it will be a proper vindication of the publick justice to extirpate that sect of thieves” (ibid., p. 65). On 1st February 1692 one hundred and twenty soldiers, mostly Campbells, entered Glencoe under the command of Captain Robert Campbell of Glenlyon, and were quartered on the inhabitants, with whom they dwelt under a false show of friendship for twelve days, enjoying their hospitality (ibid., p. 104 ff.). Glenlyon had received instructions that he was to fall upon the MacDonalds of Glencoe and put all to the sword under seventy. Special care was to be exercised that the “Old Fox and his sons should not escape” (ibid., p. 73). In the early hours of the morning of 13th February Glenlyon proceeded to put these instructions into effect. The inhabitants of Glencoe were surprised and thirty-eight of their number were massacred in cold blood.

2519. Ranald Mór of Tirnadris, second eldest son of Archibald, fourteenth Chief of Keppoch.

2522. John, elder son of Alexander, the principal victim of the Massacre, escaped with his younger brother and succeeded his father as thirteenth Chief of Glencoe. Very little is known of him after his escape, beyond certain privileges accorded to himself and to his Clan in view of the general destitution which prevailed in Glencoe after 1692. He died before 1714 (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 214).

2527. The insidious nature of the attack is also commented on by the Muck Bard. (See his poem entitled “Murt Ghlinne Comhann”, SO, pp. 375-376.)

2529. From contemporary evidence it would appear that Breadalbane was the chief instigator of the Massacre and not the Master of Stair as stated by Lord Macaulay (History of England, ed. by C. H. Firth, Vol. V, p. 2158). King William had commissioned him to negotiate with the clan chiefs in the summer of 1691 with a view to the peaceful settlement of the Highlands. Glencoe was among those who presented himself at Achallader to discuss the Government’s terms with Breadalbane. Relations

[TD 319]

between the Campbells of Glenorchy and the MacDonalds of Glencoe were far from friendly, and at the Conference of Achallader Breadalbane quarrelled with him in connexion with some cattle which he alleged the Glencoe men had stolen from him. Glencoe’s sons further stated, and were supported by Ranald MacDonald, an indweller in Glencoe, that they had heard their father say that the Earl of Breadalbane had threatened to do him much harm, and that “he feared a mischief from no man as much as from the Earl of Braidalbin” (Gordon, Highland Papers, p. 101).

Writing to Breadalbane from London on 3rd December 1691, Stair says: “By the next I hope to hear that these people are come to your hand or else your scheme of mauling them … the hardness of these people and their ungratefulness to you makes me plainly see that there is no reckoning on them but delenda est Carthago” (ibid., pp. 51-52). An indication of his sense of guilt is reflected in his endeavour to bribe the survivors of the massacre into silence concerning the part he himself played in it (ibid., p. 109). He is described by a contemporary as being “cunning as a fox, wise as a serpent, but as slippery as an eel” (Macky, Memoirs of Secret Services, p. 119).

2532. The MacDonalds of Achtriachtan were a cadet branch of the Glencoe family, and the tacksman at this time was John MacDonald (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 221). Despite the fact that he had already made his submission to the Government (Leslie, Gallienus Redivivus, p. 17), he was murdered by a party led by Sergeant Barber in his brother’s house at Achnacon (Gordon, Highland Papers, p. 106; Buchan, Massacre of Glencoe, p. 136). He was succeeded by his elder son Alexander, who escaped from the Massacre (Clan Donald, Vol. III, p. 222).

2536. Sir Colin Campbell of Ardkinglass, Sheriff-Depute of Argyllshire, who eventually administered the oath to Glencoe.

Cf. McL MS. 140:

<gai>Is mise bhiodh gu h-aighearach
Nam faighinn mar a dh’iarrainn
An ceann thoirt de Mhac Cailein
Agus fail air Mac Iain Riabhaich.<eng>

This stanza is quoted by Rev. John Kennedy in a paper on “Unpublished Gaelic Ballads from the Maclagan MSS.” (GSI, Vol. XXI, p. 219).

[TD 320]

2537. Both the possessions that had come to him (legitimately) by the light of day and those that had come (otherwise) under cover of night.

2538-2539. Cf. ll. 1252-1253.


ORAN AIR RÌGH UILLEAM AGUS BANRIGH MÀIRI

In this poem, which was composed c. 1692, there is clearly reflected the strong aversion which was felt throughout the Highlands towards William of Orange and the Revolution Government, and the keen anticipation with which the return of the exiled monarch was awaited. The Highland clans were firm believers in the divine right of kings. To them King James was their hereditary king and therefore they owed him implicit loyalty, and any attempt to resist the sovereignty, however much abused, appeared to them highly treasonable. Furthermore, they considered it a treacherous act on the part of a daughter and son-in-law to deprive a father and father-in-law of his rightful inheritance, and on this point the poet is particularly bitter. The poem opens on a note of optimism, for the news of King James’ proposed descent on England in April 1692 had obviously been conveyed to the poet, although the sequel had not yet come to his ears. The troops designed for that expedition were assembled at Cherbourg and La Hogue in Normandy. Much was hoped from this by the adherents of King James, and great enthusiasm was aroused, but it came to nought. (For an account of this expedition and the sea-battle which followed off Cape La Hogue see Clark, Memoirs of King James II, pp. 478-494; Dalrymple, Memoirs of Great Britain and Ireland, Vol. I, p. 490 ff.; Bishop Burnet’s History of my own Time, Vol. III, pp. 127-129.)

The text of the poem derives directly from Turner, which is the source for the versions in RM and SO, although a few differences in readings do occur. The version in I, entitled “Uilleam agus Marie”, omits the chorus and vv. 8, 11, and contains a number of independent readings. Five stanzas, written in a different hand, are found in the Collection of Miss Irvine Robertson, Clackmannan. O is based on T, but there is a substantial number of emendations in the text and seven stanzas have been omitted. The subtly allusive style throughout is not in character with the rest of Iain Lom’s work, and his authorship may well be questioned.

[TD 321]

2576 ff. A similar sentiment is expressed by Angus MacDonald, the Glencoe bard, in his poem entitled “Là Raon Ruairidh”:

<gai>
Tha cleamhnas ri coigrich
’Na mhór dhosgainn ’s ’na dhorainn,
Mar a chì sinn ’san am so
Anns an fheall ri Prionns Orainns;
Rinn e gnìomh tha mì-nàdurr’
Air bràthair màthar ’s athair-céile,
’N uair ghlac e crùn nan trì rìoghachd
’S ann air an Rìgh rinn e ’n eucoir.<eng>
(MC, p. 79)

Cf. Fernaig MS., p. 173:

<gai>Mac a pheathar—fath an euchd—
Comh-cheangailt’ ris air dhà ghleus;
A chliamhain ’fheoil agus ’fhuil,
Dh’imich d’a dheòin gu dhi-chrùnadh dhuinn.<eng>

2585. The correct reading is almost certainly sgeula as in SO and not seula as in T, sgeula gràin being the Catholic description of Dutch Protestantism as distinct from the true soisgeul.

2597. chaith’ (chaitheadh, T): the form may have been chaitht’, the genitive of the verbal noun (old form), blow of striking, i.e. blow struck.

2602-2603. A reference to an attack which was made on the Chapel Royal in 1689. Captain John Wallace was in command of the Palace, and when he opened fire on the rabble about a dozen men were killed and others wounded, whereupon a report was spread throughout the city that he had made a butchery of the inhabitants. A warrant was granted to the magistrates to obtain possession of the Castle but Wallace refused to surrender it. A brief skirmish ensued, and the latter, finding himself attacked front and rear, abandoned his post. On gaining access to the Castle the rabble killed some of his men and imprisoned others, whereupon they proceeded to demolish the Chapel (Balcarres, Memoirs, pp. 15-17).

2605. T reads an grain-shop which is probably a mistake for gràinseach, the tacksman’s crop as opposed to the farradh, the sub-tenants’ crop.

2608. On 9th April 1691 a violent fire broke out in Whitehall which caused considerable damage to the furnishings and build-

[TD 322]

ings. According to one source the fire was caused by the carelessness of a maid in leaving a bunch of candles lighted which quickly set fire to the buildings (Luttrell, A Brief Historical Relation of State Affairs, Vol. II, p. 206). The poet hints at a different reason.

2620. Cf. l. 1501.

2623. On the death of King Charles II on 6th February 1685 rumour was rife that he had been poisoned, and this suspicion was aggravated by the fact that he was not given a State funeral. The poet here states that suspicions were directed against his brother King James II. Bishop Burnet asserts that he himself had not heard that he was suspected of the murder of the late King, although it was a current belief that he had been murdered by the Popish party (Burnet, Vol. II, pp. 284-286; cf. Dalrymple, Memoirs, Vol. I, p. 105).

2624 ff. A reference to the malicious reports which were widely circulated by the enemies of King James, before and after the birth of the Prince of Wales, denying the pregnancy of the Queen, and asserting that it was merely a design to force a supposititious child upon the nation to the exclusion of the true Protestant heir (Burnet, Vol. II, pp. 472-481; Memoirs of King James II, Vol. II, pp. 129-130, pp. 158-161; Dalrymple, Memoirs, Vol. II, pp. 213-214).

Cf.:
<gai>’S bho’n d’dheòineadh dha bho Dhia mar ghràs
Mac an àit’ na dh’fholbh bhuainn,
Ghoin siod àdsan do chun bàis,
As gu’n d’fhàs iad borb dheth;
Is throg iad sglànrasg air a’ bhàn-righ
Le’n àrsachd ’s le’n seòladh
Gur e chàirich i ’na àite
Mac fir-cheàird nan òrd-dubh.<eng>
(Fernaig MS., p. 219)

2640. Cf.:
<gai>Bha ’m mac aluinn-s’ a bh’ aig Dàbhaidh
Gu h-àrdanach, gòrach
Le bhi cathachadh air athair
’S an t-abhairsear d’a sheòladh,
Thug Dia bàirigeadh thar chàich as,
Bho dh’fhàilig e ordugh-s’;

[TD 323]

Is chroch a’ chruaidh-choill air a ghruaig e,
’S bha ’n ruaig air luchd-’ga-chòmhnadh.<eng>
(Ibid., p. 215)

2663. Cf. l. 2164.

2670. Cf.:
<gai>Bidh sannt naoinear air aon mhnaoi gun sliochd.<eng>
A childless woman has the greed of nine.

Al.:
<gai>Bidh sannt nan seachd sagart anns a’ mhnaoi gun laogh gun luran.<eng>
A childless woman has the greed of seven priests.
(Nicolson, Gaelic Proverbs, p. 66)

2687. Reference obscure.

2694. Aithris cleas: lit. emulating the trick of.

2706. Mons was captured by King Louis XIV of France in 1691. The burghers, whose numbers exceeded those of the garrisoned troops, forced the latter to surrender by threatening to open the gates if they did not (Dalrymple, Memoirs, pp. 459-460). This event greatly encouraged the adherents of King James in Scotland to hold out against the Government of William of Orange, as they hoped for assistance from France (Leven and Melville Papers, pp. 610, 611).

2712. The Duke of Berwick, whom King James had hoped to send to Scotland with troops and ammunition as soon as an opportunity presented itself (ibid., pp. 331-332, pp. 407-408, p. 411). Colonel Hill, writing from Fort William on 29th October 1691, states that news of a considerable force, shipped from Dunkirk under the Dukes of Berwick and Gordon, had reached the Highlands—a fact which made the Clans even more refractory in their attitude to the Government (ibid., pp. 649-650).


CUMHA DO SHIR DÒMHNALL SHLÉITE

The subject of this elegy is Sir Donald MacDonald, 10th of Sleat, who was a staunch Royalist and among the first to join Dundee when he unfurled his banner in the Highlands on behalf of King James. Ill-health compelled him to return home before the battle of Killiecrankie and he himself did not participate. Despite the stiff measures which were brought to bear upon him after the battle, when the tide turned against the Royalist cause, Sir Donald for a time adamantly refused to submit to the Govern-

[TD 324]

ment of King William of Orange, but eventually he made his peace with the Government and took the oath of allegiance which King William demanded from the recalcitrant Highland chiefs. Very little is known of him after this, and he died at Armadale on 5th February 1695 (Clan Donald, Vol. III, pp. 70-79; MacKenzie, History of the MacDonalds, pp. 220-223).

The text of the poem derives from E, which is also the source for the version in SO, GlB and O. I provides a number of independent readings. A further version of the poem, communicated by Mr Daniel Fisher, is found in McL MS. 17, but the writing is inferior and difficult to decipher in parts (Appendix J, p. 412 infra).

2743. mhire: E reads mhìrreadh which would seem to point to mìorath, but a short vowel is indicated by assonance, so the reading in Turner’s revised edition of E is adopted. Maclean Sinclair reads bhioraidh which would be synonymous with colg (denoting resentment), and this may well be the correct reading.

2752. an Caol: cf. l. 1793. Probably a stock epithet based on the fact that traditionally MacDonald of Sleat would come to his kinsmen in Lochaber through the narrow seas between Skye and the Mainland.

2754. léireadh: liatha (E) cannot be correct, as there is an é rhyme in the final phrase of each stanza throughout the poem. The reading in the text derives from SO.

2769. I.e. every man who had reached maturity.

2791. If the reading in the oldest source is correct, presumably the poet means to convey that not one but many drinks were dispensed. Maclean Sinclair’s rendering, although easier, differs substantially from the basic version of the stanza, and may be translated as follows:

The thirst-quenching bowl was your desire, to empty it eagerly, and when it was emptied it would be refilled without delay by you.

2805. A reference to the heavy losses which the MacDonalds of Sleat sustained at Killiecrankie.

2811. Raghnall may be identified as the eldest son and successor of Donald MacDonald, founder of the Castleton family—a cadet branch of the MacDonalds of Sleat (Clan Donald, Vol. III,

[TD 325]

p. 517). Seumas was probably James MacDonald of Orinsay, second son of Sir Donald of Sleat, who fought at Killiecrankie and subsequently at Sheriffmuir. Eoin may have been a younger son of Sir James MacDonald of Sleat—the founder of the MacDonald family of Bernisdale and Scalpay (ibid., pp. 473, 528).


ORAN AN AGHAIDH AN AONAIDH

On 1st May 1707 the Treaty of Union between Scotland and England came into force, and the strong resentment which it aroused is clearly reflected in this poem. The unpopularity of the Union in Scotland and the general unrest which it created is corroborated in English contemporary sources. On the day on which the Treaty came into force a correspondent of the Earl of Mar in Edinburgh wrote as follows: “There is nothing so much taken notice of here today as the solemnity in the south part of Britain and the want of it here” (Papers of the Earl of Mar and Kellie, Hist. MSS. Com., p. 389). While there was great rejoicing in England, in many parishes in Scotland 1st May was observed as a day of fasting and humiliation (ibid.). The poet took a keen interest in the proceedings of Parliament and was obviously well acquainted with the political movements of the respective parties and their leaders. Being himself violently opposed to the Union, he subjects its supporters to many scathing comments.

At the opening of Queen Anne’s reign James Douglas, 4th Duke of Hamilton, led the opposition in Parliament and on that account was hailed as the protector of the nation. His appearance was greeted with tumultuous applause, and he was conveyed daily to his house by a large gathering who followed his coach from Parliament House through the city (Mar and Kellie Papers, p. 237). He proved a broken reed, however, for in the course of the Union deliberations he showed little stability of purpose when action was called for, and on more than one occasion he thwarted measures which were designed for the obstruction of the Union (Lockhart, Memoirs, pp. 196-214; Duke of Portland’s MSS., Hist. MSS. Com., Vol. IV, pp. 374, 376, 378). The poet’s epithet dùbailt appears to have been well merited. James Douglas, second Duke of Queensberry—a strong advocate of Union—was perhaps the most outstanding of the Scottish political leaders of the time. He represented the Queen as Commissioner in three sessions of the Scottish Parliament including the last, which opened under his auspices on 3rd October 1706, and it was due

[TD 326]

in a large measure to his adroitness that the articles of the Treaty were carried in spite of much opposition. He is described by the Jacobite Lockhart as “the ruin and bane of his country and the aversion of all loyal and true Scotsmen” (Memoirs, pp. 44-45). While Hamilton was warmly greeted, Queensberry was cursed and hissed at in the streets, and stones were thrown at his coach by the angry mob (Portland MSS., p. 352; Hume of Crossrig, Diary, p. 184). On 25th March 1707 the Union was confirmed, and the Scottish Parliament dissolved (Portland MSS., p. 396). Queensberry set out for London on 2nd April and, dismissing his Scottish retinue at Dunbar, he proceeded as far as Berwick almost unattended, but there his arrival was greeted with much pomp and ceremony. His journey southwards was attended by similar ceremony, the magistrates of every town and nobility of every county coming out to do him honour. On 16th April he made a public entry into London attended by forty-six coaches and several hundred horsemen, and the same evening he was waited upon by all the members of the Government with the Lord Treasurer Godolphin at their head (Memoirs of Sir John Clerk, ed. by John M. Gray, p. 67).

John Murray, first Duke of Atholl, opposed the Union. He is referred to by Lockhart as being endowed with good natural parts, “though by reason of his proud imperious haughty passionate temper, he was no ways capable to be the leading man of a party which he aimed at”. A plan was set afoot to obstruct the Union, whereby the Highlanders, under the Duke of Atholl, were to join forces with the Cameronians of the western shires and march on Edinburgh, about seven or eight thousand strong. According to Lockhart the plan fell through because the Duke of Hamilton, who had been one of its promoters, countermanded the design a day or two before the appointed tryst at Hamilton, so that only about five hundred appeared at the appointed time (Memoirs, p. 200).

The text derives from McL MS. 154, which bears the title: “Oran an aghaidh an Uinioin”, footnote Uinein, bracket reading aonuich (= aonaidh?). This is the source for the version in G; GlB and O are based on the latter.

2838. le dùrachd: with goodwill, i.e. to Scotland.

2839. If the metaphor of the oxen ploughing is maintained, lùban may refer to twistings and turnings, and bhris may here mean brought to an end. Perhaps there is a pun.

[TD 327]

2880. Thomas Hay of Balhoussie, who was created Viscount of Dupplin on 31st December 1698, and was one of the Commissioners for Union (Scots Peerage, Vol. V, p. 231).

2904 ff. The point in this stanza is that the even pull of the “straik” across the mouth of the bushel is likened to that of the gelding on the swingle tree.

2911. I.e. that their share of the rake-off would be less.

2918. That Scotland had been sold for English money was a common sentiment at this time (Duke of Portland’s MSS., p. 256). In August 1706 the Queen gave a loan of £20,000 to the Scottish Government. Of that sum over £12,000 (the stràic off the top of the bushel referred to in the text) was claimed by the Duke of Queensberry as Commissioner, for official expenses, and the rest was to be devoted to arrears of salaries and general expenses. The loan was to be acknowledged in an Act of Treasury, but lest this transaction should be misconstrued if noised abroad, Queensberry and those chiefly concerned, notably Seafield, Mar and Loudoun, induced Godolphin the Treasurer to suppress the Queen’s letter to the Treasury announcing the loan, and to consent that the money should be secured only on the personal acknowledgement of the Treasurer-Depute. In 1711 a Committee was appointed under Tory auspices to enquire into public accounts, and Lockhart, one of its members, states that certain individuals were paid to whom no arrears were due, and that others established fresh claims when the Equivalent was distributed (Cobbet, Parliamentary History, Vol. VI, pp. 1110-1115; Lockhart, Memoirs, pp. 262-272). For a full criticism of Lockhart’s accusations see Mackinnon, Union of England and Scotland, pp. 342-345.

2920. Sir Alexander Menzies, 2nd Baronet of Menzies (1682-1709). He sat in the Scottish Parliament of 1704 (“Red and White” Book of Menzies, p. 339 ff.).

2928. Uilleam Dubh, fifth Earl of Seaforth, who succeeded his father Coinneach Og in 1701. Very little is recorded of the earlier phase of his career, but he was apparently a strong supporter of the Union.

Bu mhath ’b’ fhiu thu an t-aite.<eng>

<gai>Gur mairg a dh’earbadh a cairdeas,
Neach a dh’fhas air an t-sloinne, (dheth, SO)

Fhuair iad t-athair fo’n comus;

Chaisteil Bhlair gu gle shoilleir;
’S beag bha dh’fhiughair an la sin, (dhòchas, SO)
Gu’m biodh iad paighte na’n comain. (na chomain, O)<eng>
(v. 13, T)

<gai>Gloir do’n Righ th’air a chathair,
’S mairg a ghabhadh mu na chluinneadh;
No ghuidheadh na bhreig e;
Gach nith dh’eirich sa chunnaic;
Mu’s ann le droch-bheart Iudais,
Dh’fhuaigh thu chlùd air an lunnainn;
Chaill thu’n luireach ’s na breidean,
’S gach aon eideadh bha umad.<eng>
(v. 17, T)

Miss Irvine Robertson, Clackmannan, Collection, p. 189:

<gai>
Guidheam buadh an droch geeol sin
air Priunsidh Ornish gun diaghachd
Gad a rachadh a bhadhigh
cha binnin bas da s ma dhiarin
Ach mo shulin ga fhaicin
eadar eachibh ga strialidh
S a chur na smal an asd aithear
mar luadh dhaghi gun criaridh.

Sgrios gun iarmid gun dulich
cha n iarin tulidh a bhi an dan dut
Gun sliochd iaghadh mid mhunnal
a gniomh bronadh droch Mhari
Gad a gabtibh mar theam ort
’s farsin beal a mhic lamhich
S do sheann Stile a bhi an cumhnart
na rinn u crionach do granaig.

[TD 374]

Ach sian gun tuslidh air Mari
mar mor an lan tha na togsead
Nar fhaic i laogh caraid
a nios gu brach as a poca
Cha bhi an sean facail claotich
air deo s claon theid a thogal
Aig na bha shant an san droch mhnaoi
bannsidh bodi na boban.

Ach nan tigidh an Righ sin
na mhac dilis na aidmhal
Gad a shaolidh Priunsidh Ornish
gur e a chor bha e aig agart
Cha bu mhu air Righ Ullam
air srad Lundin an Sassin
An ceann fhuadich g a mhunal
na cluas culan an radan.

Priunsidh Welsi a mhi radh
ma dhordich Triath a thor gu creademh
S cor an dulachd so a thenda
air a Bhanrigh nach cread e
Le bhi na bibhuandachd shandachd
mar mhac samhla gu gaodsin
Neo e righeachd le landubh
nian Sheanslar Hutchan.
<eng>

Caite n robh e fo’n adhar
Na sheall nar bathais gu geur						90
Nach tugadh dhuib athadh

A mheall an t aibhistear trein
Ged do rinn iad nar lotadh						95
Gur trom a rosad dhoibh fein.

Sios air Iain (On la riobhach) na pairce
Siad nan trail fo all fuinn						100
				(Sib gabhail sa fo all fuinn)

S math na toildinaich seine						105

Ga ba là thig a chrioch

[TD 398]

Gur a mollaichte an tal sin						115

Cha ba siod bha mi giontran

GSI, XII

MORT NA CEAPAICH

’S tearc an diugh mo chuis ghaire,
Tigh’n na raidean so ’niar;
’G amharc fonn Inbhir-laire,
’N deigh a stràchdadh le siol;
Ge d’ tha Cheapach na fasaich,					5
Gun aon aird’ oirre ’s fhiach:
Gu’m faice’ Dia, bhraithrean,
Gur trom a bhàrc oirnn an t-sian.

’S fad bhios cuimhn’ air an Aoine,
Dh-fhag a chaoidh sinn fo sprochd;					10
Ann an àm na Feill-Micheil,
Cha bu ni chall air phlod;
Ach bhi’n diugh na’r cuis-bhuird
Mar mhial-bhuirn air gach loch;
Nuair theid gach cinneadh a dh’aon taobh				15
Bidh sinne sgaoilte mu’n chnoc.

’S ann Di-sathurna gearra-bhuain,
Bhuail an t-earachall orm goirt;
’S mi fos cionn nan corp geala,
Bha ’sileadh fala fo’ n bhrat;						20
Bha mo lamhansa craobh-dhearg,
An deigh bhi ’taomadh nan lot;
’Se bhur cur ann sa chiste,
Turn is miste mo thoirt.

[TD 400]

B’iad mo ghaol na cuirp chul-bhuidhe,				25
Anns ’m bu dluth cuir na’n sgian:
’S iad na’n sineadh air urlar,
An seomar ùr dha’n cur sios;
Fo chasan Shiol Dughaill,
Luchd a spuilleadh nan cliar:						30
Dh’fhag àladh am biodag,
Mar sgaile ruidil ’ur bian.

Tha sibh ’n cadal-thigh duinte,
’Se gun smuid deth, gun cheo;
Far an d’fhuair sibh ’n garbh rusgadh,				35
Thaobh ’ur cuil a’s ’ur beoil;
Ach na’m faigheadh sibh ùine,
Bho luchd ’ur mi-rùin bhi beo;
Cha bu bhaile gun surd e,
Bhiodh aidhir, mùirn ann a’s ceol.					40

S fuar caidreamh tigh tabhairt,
’San robh gairich is cosd;
Far nach cluinnear guth clarsaich,
Ach gaoir galach nam bochd;
’Se mar thaileasg air aon teud,					45
Tha t’fhearann sgaoilte ’se nochdt’;
’Tilgear urchair na disne,
’S gur leir dha’n Ti a mheur ghoint.

’S ann oirnne thainig an diombuaidh,
’S an t-iomaguin tha geur;						50
Mar tha claidheamh ar fine,
Cho minig ’n ’ar deigh;
Pachda Thurcach gun sireadh,
Bhi a pinneadh bhur cleibh;
Bhi n’ ur breacain g’ ur filleadh,					55
’Measg ur cinneadh mor fein.

A leithid de mhurt cha robh ’n Alba,
Ged bu bhorbarr’ a gleus;
’S cha bu laghail an t-sealg e
Gu cosnadh sealbh righeachd Dhe;					60
Ge b’e ’m fàth mu’n robh sgionadh
Chaoidh cha’n innis mi ’n sgeul;
Cha d’thain’ a leithid do mhilleadh,
Air ceann-cinnidh fo’n ghrein.

Ghabh sibh roimhe so fath oirnn,					65
Dh’fheuch bhur cairdeas ruinn geur

[TD 401]

Chaidh sibh ’stigh ann san fhasaich
’Nuair a thar sibh bhi reidh;
Chuir sibh cungais a chàise
’Stigh an aros nan teud,						70
’S cuid de’n buailichean ba-chruidh
Ann an garadh nam peur.

C’aite ’n robh e fo’n adhar,
A sheall n’ur bathais gu geur,
Nach tugadh dhuibh athadh,						75
A luchd ’ur labhairt ’s ’ur beus;
Mach bho chloinn bhrathair ’ur n-athar,
A mheall an t-aibhistear treun,
Ged a rinn iad bhur lotsa,
Gur trom a rosad dhaibh fein.						80

Tha lionn-dubh na chàs cruaidh orm
Tighinn an uaigneas mo chleibh,
Le mar dh’fhas e na chuan orm
B’fhearr leam ’uam e mar cheud
Cia mar dh’fhaodas mi direadh					85
Gun ite dhileis na’m sgeith
’S luchd a dheanamh na sithne
Bhi feadh na tire gun deigh.

’S og a bha sibh do bhliadhna,
Ghlac a cheutaidh sibh luath,						90
Aig ro-fheothas bhur ciall
Gu cur ’ur riaghailtean suas.
Ge b’e ghabhadh rium fiabhrus
Bhi dha nur n-iargainn sibh’ uam;
Bidh m’ ’n deigh air bhur riasladh,					95
Gus an liath air mo ghruaig.

Chuir Dia oirnn mac oighre,
Gu bhi na choinnleir roimh chach,
Chum gu ’n soillsich a sholus,
Mar phreas-toraidh fo bhlath,						100
’S mi gu’m freagradh a chaismeachd,
Air fraoch-bhrataich gun chearb,
Dealbh do bhradan, do dhobhran,
Do luing, do leomhan ’s laimh dhearg.

Dh’ordaich Dia dhuinn craobh-shiochaint				105
Chumadh dion oirnn le treoir,
Da’m bu choir chuinn bhi striochdadh
Fhad ’s a’s cian bhiomaid beo;

[TD 402]

Mas sinn fhein a chuir dith oirre
Cha’n fhearr a’ chrioch a thig oirnn,					110
Tuitidh tuagh as na Flaitheas
Leis an sgathar na meoir.

An glan fhiuran so bh’againn
’N taobh so Fhlaitheas Mhic Dhe
Thainig sgiursadh a’ bhais air						115
Chaill sinn ’thoirt le strachd geur,
An t-aon fhiuran a b’aillidh
Bh’ann sa phairc an robh speis,
Mar gu’m buaineadh sibh àilean
Leis an fhaladar gheur.						120

’S math an toilltinneach sinne,
Bhi gu minig am pein;
Bho’ n a ghlac sinn fal-spiorad

Mar lorg neo-chinnte air linne,					125
Ge’d bu mhinig an sgeul,

Fhuair e bhuain as a bhreig.

Tha mulad air m’inntinn,
Bhi ag innse bhur beus;						130
’S ann a ghabh iad am fath oirbh,
’Nuair chaidh ’ur fagail libh fein;
’S bochd an sgeul eadar bhraithrean,
E dhol an lathair Mhic Dhe
Mar am bàt’ air an linne						135
Ge b’e shireadh na deigh.

Cha b’e sud bha mi ’g ionndrain

Air an lubadh san lion							140
’S e chuir stad air mo shugradh
’Sa dh’fhag mo shuilean gun dion

Mar tha ’n stoc as an d’fhàs sibh,					145

’S i gabhail sàth fo al-fuinn;

Gun t’fhuil a dh’eiridh fo thuinn,					150

[TD 403]

’S gur tu thog iad na’n oige;

Bho ’n is mallaicht’ an t’àl sinn					155

ORAN MA BHAS SHIR DONAL O’N CHAOL
